# 南华真经

*The Authentic Scripture of Southern Florescence*

**Scripture Title:** *The Authentic Scripture of Southern Florescence* (originally titled *Zhuangzi*). Traditionally attributed to Zhuang Zhou of Song during the Warring States period, it is in fact a compendium of works by the Daoist school of Zhuang Zhou. In the Tang dynasty, it was revered as one of the Four True Scriptures of Daoism. The original text comprised fifty-two chapters; the present version, annotated by Guo Xiang of the Jin, contains thirty-three chapters, divided into five scrolls.

**Source Text:** *Daoist Canon of the Orthodox Unity* (Dongshen section, *Benwen* category).
**Collated Edition:** *Sibu Congkan* facsimile of the Ming-era Shide Hall printed edition (hereafter "Shide edition").

Zhuangzi was a native of Song, named Zhou, styled You. He was born in Meng County of Suiyang. He once served as the warden of the Lacquer Garden in Meng. His learning left no field unexamined, but its essence returned to the words of Laozi. Thus, he composed a work of over a hundred thousand characters, most of which were parables. His words flowed boundlessly, following his own inclinations, so that even kings and nobles could not confine him. King Wei of Chu, hearing of Zhuangzi’s worth, sent envoys with generous gifts to invite him, offering the position of minister. Zhuangzi laughed and said to the envoys: "A thousand gold pieces are a great profit; the rank of minister is a lofty position. Have you alone never seen the sacrificial ox at the suburban rites? Fed for years, adorned with embroidered trappings, it is led into the great temple. At that moment, if it wished to be a lone piglet, could it do so? Begone at once, and do not defile me. I would rather roam and play in the mire for my own delight than be fettered by rulers. I will never serve, that my will may remain free."

During the Tang Dynasty, he was enfeoffed as the Perfected Man of Nanhua, and his book was titled *The True Classic of Nanhua*.

**Inner Chapters**
1. "Free and Easy Wandering"
In the Northern Darkness there is a fish, its name is Kun. The Kun’s greatness—no one knows how many thousand *li* it spans. It transforms into a bird, its name is Peng. The Peng’s back—no one knows how many thousand *li* it spans. When it rises in flight, its wings are like clouds hanging from the sky. This bird, when the sea stirs, will journey to the Southern Darkness. The Southern Darkness is the Heavenly Pond.

There is a man named Qi Xie, who records strange things. His words say: When the Peng journeys to the Southern Darkness, it strikes the water for three thousand *li*, then spirals upward on the whirlwind for ninety thousand *li*, departing on the sixth-month gale. Wild horses, dust motes, and living things are all blown about by the same breath. Is the deep blue of the sky its true color? Or is it so distant that it has no end? From its view below, it must be just the same.

Moreover, when water is not deeply accumulated, it lacks the strength to bear a great vessel. Pour a cup of water onto a hollow in the hall’s floor, and a mustard seed becomes a boat for it; set the cup there and it sticks fast, for the water is shallow and the vessel large. When wind is not deeply accumulated, it lacks the strength to bear great wings. Thus, only when the wind lies beneath ninety thousand *li* can it be borne up; only then, riding the wind, back pressing against the blue sky without obstacle, can one at last set course for the south.

The cicada and the fledgling dove laugh at this, saying: "We burst forth and fly, striking the elm and sandalwood, and when we cannot reach them, we simply settle back to earth—that is all. Why go ninety thousand *li* to journey south?" To travel to the near wilderness, one returns after a morning’s meal with belly still full; to travel a hundred *li*, one must spend the night grinding grain; to travel a thousand *li*, one must gather provisions for three months. What do these two creatures know?

Small knowledge does not reach great knowledge; short years do not reach long years. How do we know this is so? The morning mushroom knows not the alternation of dusk and dawn; the summer cicada knows not spring and autumn—these are short-lived. South of Chu there is the *Mingling* tree, which takes five hundred years as its spring and five hundred years as its autumn; in high antiquity there was the *Dazhuang* tree, which took eight thousand years as its spring and eight thousand years as its autumn. Yet Pengzu alone is renowned today for his longevity—how pitiful that the multitude compares itself to him!

This is what Tang asked Ji about: North of the lands where no plants grow lies the Dark Sea, the Heavenly Pond. There is a fish there, several thousand *li* in breadth, though none knows its length—its name is Kun. There is a bird there, its name Peng, its back like Mount Tai, its wings like clouds hanging from the sky. It strikes the whirlwind and spirals upward ninety thousand *li*, cutting through the clouds and qi, pressing against the blue sky, then sets course for the south, bound for the Southern Darkness. The minnow scoffs at it, saying: "Where is it going? I leap and soar, rising no more than a few fathoms before descending, wheeling among the mugwort and wormwood—this is the utmost of flight. Where is it going?" This is the distinction between small and great.

Thus, those whose knowledge suffices for one office, whose conduct matches one village, whose virtue pleases one lord, or whose merit wins one state—they view themselves as this minnow does. Song Rongzi would merely laugh at them. Even if the whole world praised him, he would not be encouraged; even if the whole world blamed him, he would not be disheartened. He firmly distinguishes between inner and outer, clearly discerns the boundary of honor and disgrace—this is all. In his dealings with the world, he is not eager. Yet even he has not yet established himself.

Liezi rode the wind and traveled, effortlessly graceful, returning after fifteen days. In his pursuit of good fortune, he was not eager. Though he thus avoided walking, he still depended on something. As for one who rides the constancy of Heaven and Earth, who harnesses the transformations of the six qi, and roams the boundless—what does he depend on? Therefore it is said: The Perfect One is without self; the Spirit Man is without achievement; the Sage is without name.

Yao offered the world to Xu You, saying: "When the sun and moon have risen, yet the torch still burns—is it not difficult for its light to be seen? When timely rain has fallen, yet one still waters the fields—is it not labor in vain? You stand and the world is well-governed, yet I still occupy the throne. I see myself as lacking—please take the world." Xu You said: "You govern the world, and the world is already well-governed. Were I to replace you, would I do it for the name? A name is but the guest of reality—would I become a guest? The wren nests in the deep forest, yet needs only a single branch; the mole drinks from the river, yet needs only a bellyful. Return, my lord—I have no use for the world. Even if the cook does not prepare the food, the master of ceremonies does not leap over the wine vessels and sacrificial stands to take his place."

Jian Wu said to Lian Shu: "I heard words from Jie Yu—grand yet without measure, going yet not returning. I was alarmed and terrified by them, as if the Milky Way stretched endlessly, vast and distant, far from human concerns." Lian Shu said: "What did he say?" Jian Wu replied: "On distant Mount Gushe, there dwells a spirit man. His skin is like ice and snow, his grace like that of a maiden. He eats no grain, but inhales the wind and drinks the dew. He rides the clouds and qi, drives flying dragons, and roams beyond the four seas. His spirit is concentrated, and he causes things to be free of blight so that the grain ripens. I consider this madness and do not believe it."

Lian Shu said: "Indeed. The blind cannot partake in the spectacle of patterns and colors, nor the deaf in the sound of bells and drums. Is it only the body that may be deaf and blind? The mind, too, may suffer such afflictions. His words are like those spoken to a child. This man, this virtue of his—it spans the boundaries of the myriad things to make them one. The world may be consumed by chaos, yet who would trouble himself with the affairs of the realm? This man: nothing harms him. Though great floods reach the heavens, he does not drown; though severe drought melts metal and stone and scorches mountains, he does not feel the heat. The dust and chaff of the world are but the clay from which Yao and Shun were molded—who would deign to occupy himself with worldly matters?

The people of Song carried ceremonial caps to Yue, yet the Yue, with their shorn hair and tattooed bodies, had no use for them. Yao governed the people of the world and brought order to the realm, yet when he went to see the Four Masters on distant Mount Gushe, north of the Fen River, he was so overwhelmed that he forgot his empire entirely.

Hui Zi said to Zhuangzi: 'The King of Wei gave me seeds of a great gourd. I planted them, and they grew to bear fruit weighing five *dan*. When I used it to hold water, its strength was insufficient to support itself. When I split it to make a ladle, its size was so vast it could hold nothing. It is not that it was not impressively large—it was simply useless, so I discarded it.'

Zhuangzi replied: 'You are truly inept at putting great things to use. There was a man of Song skilled in making an ointment that prevented chapping. For generations, his family had made their living by fulling silk. A traveler heard of this and offered to buy the formula for a hundred pieces of gold. The family gathered to deliberate: "For generations, we have fulling silk and earned no more than a few pieces of gold. Now, in a single morning, we can sell our craft for a hundred. Let us give it to him." The traveler acquired it and presented it to the King of Wu. When Yue raised a rebellion, the King of Wu sent him to lead his troops. In winter, they fought the Yue on the water and crushed them. The king then granted him a fiefdom. The ability to prevent chapping was the same, yet one man gained a fief while another remained a fuller of silk—the difference lay in how it was used. Now you have a gourd of five *dan*—why not consider making it into a great float and drifting upon the rivers and lakes, instead of fretting that its size leaves it with no place to hold anything? Your mind is still as tangled as a clump of weeds.'

Hui Zi said to Zhuangzi: 'I have a great tree, which people call the *chu*. Its trunk is so gnarled and swollen that it does not meet the carpenter’s line, and its branches are so twisted that they do not conform to compass or square. Though it stands by the roadside, no craftsman gives it a glance. Now your words are grand but useless—just as the world turns away from them.'

Zhuangzi replied: 'Have you never seen a wildcat? It crouches low to lie in wait, pouncing on whatever passes by. It leaps east and west, heedless of height or depth.'"

"Trapped in snares and crushed by traps, it dies in nets and toils. Now take the yak: its size is like clouds trailing across the sky. This creature may be great, but it cannot catch mice. You have a great tree and worry that it is useless—why not plant it in the land of Nothing-Whatsoever, in the vast and boundless wilds? Wander idly by its side, roam free and recline beneath it. No axe will fell it, nothing will harm it, and it will have no use. Where then is your distress?

---

In the second chapter, *On the Equality of Things*, Nan Guo Zi Qi leaned against his armrest and sat, gazing upward to exhale, lost in a trance as if he had forgotten his companion.

Yan Cheng Zi You stood in attendance before him and asked: 'What is this? Can the body truly be made like withered wood, and the mind like dead ashes? Yet the one who now leans against the armrest is not the same as the one who once did so.'

Zi Qi said: 'Yan, is it not well that you ask? Just now, I forgot myself—did you understand?'"

"You have heard the human *lai*, but not the earthly *lai*; you have heard the earthly *lai*, but not the heavenly *lai*."

Yan Cheng Zi You said: "May I ask the method?"

Zi Qi said: "The great clod breathes forth *qi*—its name is wind. This alone does nothing, but when it stirs, the myriad hollows roar and howl—have you alone not heard its ceaseless *shen-shen*? The towering forests and jagged peaks—great trees of a hundred spans, their hollows like noses, like mouths, like ears, like beams, like rings, like mortars, like pools, like pits. Some gush, some whistle, some shriek, some gasp, some wail, some bellow, some moan, some shriek—those ahead sing *yu*, those behind sing *yong*. A gentle breeze yields a faint harmony; a whirlwind yields a mighty harmony. When the raging wind subsides, all hollows fall silent. Have you alone not seen their trembling *diao-diao* and quivering *jiao-jiao*?"

Zi You said: "The earthly *lai* is simply the harmony of the myriad hollows; the human *lai* is merely the harmony of flutes. May I ask about the heavenly *lai*?"

Zi Qi said: "The wind blows in ten thousand ways, yet each moves of itself—all take their own form. What stirs them, if not the one who roars?" The wisdom of the great is vast and unhurried; the knowledge of the petty is narrow and contentious. Grand words blaze and burn; petty words prattle and chatter."

In sleep, the soul intertwines; in waking, the form unfurls. Engaged with the world, we weave our bonds, daily waging war within the heart. Some drift aimlessly, some burrow deep, some cling in secrecy. Small fears leave us trembling; great fears unravel us entirely.

Their eruptions are like the release of a crossbow’s trigger—this is what we call the judgment of right and wrong. Their restraint is like a sworn oath—this is what we call the clinging to victory. Their decay is like autumn and winter, speaking of the daily erosion. Their drowning in habit cannot be reversed. Their weariness is like a sealed casket, speaking of the stagnation of age. The heart near death cannot be revived. Joy and anger, sorrow and delight, anxiety and sighing, fickleness and caprice, indulgence and abandon—these arise like music from the void, like mushrooms from the steam. Day and night they alternate before us, yet none know where they sprout.

Enough, enough! If one could grasp this even at dusk, would it not be the very source of life? Without that, there is no self; without the self, there is nothing to grasp. This is close indeed, yet we know not what moves it. As if there were a true master, yet no trace of it can be found. It can act and be trusted, yet its form is unseen. It has essence but no shape.

The hundred bones, nine orifices, six viscera—all are complete within me. Which should I hold most dear? Do you delight in all of them equally? Or do you favor some? Are they all servants and maids? If servants and maids cannot govern one another, do they take turns as lord and subject? Or is there a true lord residing within? Whether one seeks to know its nature or not, it neither adds to nor diminishes the truth.

Once the formed shape is received, it does not perish until its end. It clashes and grinds against things, racing toward exhaustion like a galloping steed—yet nothing can halt it. Is this not lamentable? To toil a lifetime without seeing success, to wear oneself out in exhaustion without knowing one’s destination—how can this not be sorrowful? If one calls this not death, what good is it? The body transforms, the heart follows suit—can this not be called great sorrow?

Is human life inherently so confused? Am I alone in my confusion, while others remain unconfused? If one follows the heart that has already taken shape and takes it as a teacher, who is without a teacher? Why must one know the cycles of change and let the heart choose for itself? Even fools possess this. To have right and wrong before the heart is fully formed is like setting out for Yue today and arriving yesterday. This is to take what does not exist as existent. To take what does not exist as existent—even the divine Yu could not comprehend it, so how can I?

Speech is not the wind’s breath. Those who speak have words, yet what they speak of remains unsettled. Is there truly speech? Or has there never been speech? Do they deem it different from the chirping of fledglings? Is there distinction, or is there none?

Where does the Dao hide that it has truth and falsehood? Where do words hide that they have right and wrong? Where does the Dao go that it ceases to exist? Where do words abide that they cannot be trusted? The Dao is obscured by minor achievements; words are obscured by florid embellishment. Thus arise the disputes of Confucians and Mohists, each affirming what the other denies and denying what the other affirms. To affirm what the other denies and deny what the other affirms, nothing surpasses clarity.

Nothing exists that is not "that"; nothing exists that is not "this." From "that," one does not see; from self-knowledge, one knows. Thus it is said: "That" emerges from "this," and "this" likewise depends on "that." "That" and "this" are born together in the same moment. Yet even as they are born, they die; even as they die, they are born. Even as something is deemed right, it is deemed wrong; even as it is deemed wrong, it is deemed right. Thus the sage does not proceed by this path but illuminates all by the light of Heaven—this too is "that."

"This" is also "that," and "that" is also "this." "That" has its own right and wrong, and "this" has its own right and wrong. Is there truly a distinction between "that" and "this"? Or is there no distinction? Where "that" and "this" find no opposition is called the pivot of the Dao. Once the pivot is grasped, it responds endlessly to the infinite transformations, where "right" is inexhaustible and "wrong" is likewise inexhaustible. Thus it is said: Nothing surpasses clarity.

Is there truly a "that" and a "this"? Or is there no "that" and no "this"? Where "that" and "this" find no counterpart, this is called the pivot of the Dao. Once the pivot is grasped at the center of the ring, it responds to the boundless. "Right" is one boundlessness, and "wrong" is another boundlessness. Thus it is said: Nothing surpasses clarity.

To illustrate the finger’s non-fingerhood with a finger is not as good as illustrating the finger’s non-fingerhood with a non-finger. To illustrate the horse’s non-horsehood with a horse is not as good as illustrating the horse’s non-horsehood with a non-horse. Heaven and Earth are one finger; the myriad things are one horse.

What is deemed right is right; what is deemed wrong is wrong. The Dao proceeds and thereby achieves; things name it and thereby affirm. How is it affirmed? Affirmed as affirmed. How is it not affirmed? Not affirmed as not affirmed. Things inherently have what they affirm; things inherently have what they deem right. There is nothing that is not affirmed; there is nothing that is not deemed right.

Thus, take the examples of the tumbleweed and the pillar, the hag and Xi Shi, the vast and the grotesque, the strange and the monstrous—through the Dao, all become one. Their division is their completion; their completion is their destruction. Yet for all things, there is no completion and no destruction, for they revert to becoming one.

Only those who comprehend this unity do not employ it but lodge it in the ordinary. The ordinary is function; function is penetration; penetration is attainment. Attainment is the moment of nearness. Following this, one ceases, yet does not know how it is so—this is called the Dao.

To exhaust the spirit in striving for unity while failing to recognize their sameness is called "morning threes." What is "morning threes"? It is said: The monkey-keeper distributed chestnuts, saying, "Three in the morning and four in the evening." The monkeys were all enraged. He then said, "Very well, four in the morning and three in the evening." The monkeys were all pleased. The names and realities remained unchanged, yet joy and anger were employed—this too is following "that."

Thus the sage harmonizes with right and wrong and rests in the equality of Heaven—this is called walking two paths. The ancients, their knowledge reached a certain point—how far did it reach?

There are those who hold that things have never yet begun to exist—this is the ultimate, the utmost, beyond which nothing can be added. Next are those who hold that things exist, yet boundaries have never yet arisen. Next are those who hold that boundaries exist, yet right and wrong have never yet appeared. The manifestation of right and wrong is what causes the Dao to be diminished. The diminishment of the Dao is what gives rise to partiality. Is there truly completion and diminishment? Or is there truly no completion and diminishment? If there were completion and diminishment, then Master Zhao’s zither-playing would be an example. If there were no completion and diminishment, then Master Zhao’s not playing the zither would be an example. Master Zhao’s zither-playing, Music Master Kuang’s wielding of his baton, and Master Hui’s leaning against the parasol tree—these three men’s knowledge approached subtlety. All were at their peak, and thus their deeds are recorded at life’s end. Yet because they were fond of these things, they set themselves apart from others; because they were fond of them, they sought to make them clear. But what they sought to clarify was not truly clarifiable, and so they ended in the obscurity of "hard and white." And Master Zhao’s son, in turn, spent his life spinning the threads of his father’s art, yet achieved nothing. If this can be called achievement, then even I have achieved. If this cannot be called achievement, then neither things nor I have achieved. Thus, the dazzling confusion of doubt is what the sage contemplates. To refrain from using it while lodging it in the ordinary—this is called "illuminating through non-use."

Now, suppose I were to speak here: Would it be of the same kind as this? Or not of the same kind? Whether of the same kind or not, if they are taken together as kinds, then there is no difference from that. Nevertheless, let me try to speak of it. There is a beginning; there is a time before that beginning; there is a time before the time before that beginning. There is being; there is non-being; there is a time before non-being; there is a time before the time before non-being. Suddenly there is being and non-being, yet we do not know which of the two—being or non-being—is truly so. Now, I have already spoken, yet I do not know whether what I have spoken is truly speaking or truly not speaking.

Nothing in the world is greater than the tip of an autumn hair, yet Mount Tai is small. No one is longer-lived than a child who dies young, yet Pengzu died prematurely. Heaven and Earth were born together with me, and the myriad things and I are one. Since we are already one, can there be speech? Since I have already called it one, can there be no speech? One and speech make two; two and one make three. Proceeding from this, even the most skilled mathematician cannot arrive at a number, much less an ordinary person. Thus, from non-being to being, we reach three—how much more so from being to being! Do not proceed; follow this.

The Dao has never had boundaries; speech has never had constancy. It is for the sake of "this" that there are disputes. Let me speak of these disputes: There is left and right, there are ranks and principles, there are divisions and distinctions, there are competition and contention—these are called the eight virtues. Beyond the six directions, the sage acknowledges but does not discuss; within the six directions, the sage discusses but does not debate. The *Spring and Autumn Annals* and the chronicles of former kings’ reigns—the sage debates but does not argue. Thus, division implies what is not divided; distinction implies what is not distinguished. Why is this? The sage embraces it, while the multitude debates it to display to one another. Thus it is said: Those who debate do so because they do not see.

The great Dao is not named; great disputation does not speak; great benevolence is not benevolent; great integrity is not frugal; great courage is not aggressive. The Dao that is manifest is not the Dao; speech that debates does not reach; benevolence that is constant does not accomplish; integrity that is pure is not trustworthy; courage that relies on skill does not succeed. These five, though rounded, nearly tend toward the square. Thus, to know where to stop in what one does not know—this is the utmost. Who knows the disputation of no-speech, the Dao of no-Dao? If one could know it, this would be called the "Heavenly Storehouse." It can be drawn from without ever being filled, and ladled from without ever being exhausted, yet one does not know its source—this is called "preserving the light."

Thus, in ancient times, Yao asked Shun: "I wish to attack Zong, Kuai, and Xu’ao. When I sit facing south, I feel uneasy. What is the reason?" Shun said: "Those three rulers dwell among the weeds and brambles. Why do you feel uneasy? Long ago, ten suns rose together, and the myriad things were all illuminated—how much more so the virtue that surpasses the sun!"

Nie Que asked Wang Ni: "Do you know what things all agree upon as right?" Wang Ni said: "How would I know?" "Do you know what you do not know?" "How would I know?" "Then do things have no knowledge?" "How would I know? Nevertheless, let me try to speak of it. How do I know that what I call knowing is not not-knowing? How do I know that what I call not-knowing is not knowing? Moreover, let me ask you this: If people sleep in damp places, their waists will ache and they will be half-paralyzed—is that so for an eel? If they live in trees, they will tremble with fear—is that so for a monkey? Of these three, which knows the proper place to dwell? People eat the flesh of grass-fed and grain-fed animals; deer eat grass; centipedes delight in snakes; owls and crows relish mice. Of these four, which knows the proper taste? Monkeys mate with monkeys, deer consort with deer, eels swim with fish. Mao Qiang and Li Ji were considered beautiful by people, but when fish saw them, they dove deep; when birds saw them, they flew high; when deer saw them, they dashed away. Of these four, which knows the proper beauty of the world? From my perspective, the beginnings of benevolence and righteousness, the paths of right and wrong, are tangled and confused—how could I know how to distinguish them?"

Nie Que said: "If you do not know benefit and harm, then does the perfected person truly not know benefit and harm?" Wang Ni said: "The perfected person is spirit-like. If great marshes burned, they could not be hot; if the Yellow River and Han River froze, they could not be cold; if swift thunder split mountains and winds shook the sea, they could not be startled. One like this rides the clouds and mist, mounts the sun and moon, and roams beyond the four seas."

Death and life bring no change to oneself—how could they be the beginnings of benefit and harm?

Master Juque asked Master Changwu: "I have heard from the Master that the sage does not engage in worldly affairs, does not pursue profit, does not avoid harm, does not delight in seeking, does not follow the Dao/Way. Without words, there are words; with words, there are no words—and thus roams beyond the dust and grime. The Master considered this reckless speech, but I regard it as the practice of the wondrous Dao/Way. What do you think?"

Master Changwu said: "This is what the Yellow Emperor found enlightening to hear. Yet how could Confucius (Qiu) be sufficient to understand it? Moreover, you are far too hasty in your calculations—seeing the dawn and demanding the night, seeing the slingshot and craving the roasted crow. I have spoken to you idly; you have listened idly. Why cling to sun and moon, embrace the cosmos? For its harmony and union, set aside its confusion and chaos, and serve as equals in mutual respect. The masses toil; the sage appears foolish and dull. Through the myriad ages, all becomes one pure whole. All things are thus, and in this they are mutually enfolded.

How do you know that delight in life is not delusion? How do you know that aversion to death is not like one who has lost his way and does not know how to return? Lady Li was the daughter of the border warden of Ai. When the state of Jin first obtained her, she wept until her robes were soaked. Yet once she arrived at the king’s palace, shared his bed and ate fine meats, she regretted her tears. How do I know that the dead do not regret their former clinging to life?

One who dreams of drinking wine may weep at dawn; one who dreams of weeping may go hunting at sunrise. In the midst of the dream, one does not know it is a dream. Within the dream, one may even interpret the dream—only upon waking does one know it was a dream. And only with great awakening does one realize that all this was a great dream. Yet fools believe themselves awake, arrogantly claiming to know. Lords! Shepherds! How rigid they are! Confucius (Qiu) and you—we are all dreaming. When I say you are dreaming, I too am dreaming. Such words are called ‘uncanny.’ Even after ten thousand generations, should a great sage appear who understands their meaning, it would be as if we had met him at dawn or dusk.

Suppose you and I were to debate: if you defeat me, and I do not defeat you, does that mean you are truly right and I am truly wrong? If I defeat you, and you do not defeat me, does that mean I am truly right and you are truly wrong? Is one of us right and the other wrong? Are both of us right or both wrong? You and I cannot know, and others are surely as confused. Whom shall we ask to decide? If we ask someone who agrees with you, how can he decide since he already agrees with you? If we ask someone who agrees with me, how can he decide since he already agrees with me? If we ask someone who differs from both of us, how can he decide since he differs from both of us? If we ask someone who agrees with both of us, how can he decide since he agrees with both of us? Thus, you, I, and others cannot know—must we wait for another?

What does it mean to harmonize with the heavenly equality? It means: ‘Is’ or ‘is not,’ ‘so’ or ‘not so.’ If ‘is’ is truly ‘is,’ then the difference between ‘is’ and ‘is not’ requires no debate. If ‘so’ is truly ‘so,’ then the difference between ‘so’ and ‘not so’ requires no debate. The mutual dependence of changing voices—if they did not depend on one another—would be harmonized by the heavenly equality, carried along by the boundless, and thus one could live out one’s years. Forgetting years, forgetting righteousness, one is stirred by the boundless and thus abides in the boundless.

Wang Liang asked Qi: "In the past, you walked—now you stop. In the past, you sat—now you rise. Why are you so without constancy?"

Qi said: "I act as I must—is that it? What I must do also depends on something else—is that it? Do I depend on the snake’s scales or the cicada’s wings? How do I know why it is so? How do I know why it is not so?"

Once, Zhuang Zhou dreamed he was a butterfly—fluttering and joyous, a true butterfly! He was content, following his own will. He did not know he was Zhou. Suddenly he awoke, and there he was, solidly Zhou. Did Zhou dream he was a butterfly, or did the butterfly dream it was Zhou? Between Zhou and the butterfly, there must be a distinction. This is called the transformation of things.

**Inner Chapters, "The Secret of Caring for Life," Chapter Three**

Our lives are bounded, yet knowledge is boundless. To strive with the bounded after the boundless is perilous. And to strive thus as one who knows is perilous indeed. In doing good, avoid proximity to fame; in doing evil, avoid proximity to punishment. Follow the central course as your constant guide—thus may you preserve your body, fulfill your life, nurture your kin, and live out your years.

Cook Ding carved an ox for Lord Wenhui. Where his hand touched, where his shoulder leaned, where his foot pressed, where his knee braced—*hua!*—the skin parted, the blade sang, never missing the rhythm, harmonizing with the *Sanglin* dance, attaining the *Jingshou* melody.

Lord Wenhui exclaimed: "Ah! How excellent! Has skill truly reached such heights?"

Cook Ding set down his knife and replied: "What your servant loves is the Dao—it has advanced beyond mere skill. When I first began carving oxen, all I saw was the ox. After three years, I no longer saw the whole ox. Now, I meet it with my spirit rather than my eyes; my senses cease, and my spirit moves. I follow the natural grain, cleave through the great gaps, guide the blade through the great cavities, all according to its inherent pattern. I never touch the ligaments or tendons, much less the solid bone. A good cook changes his knife every year—it is worn from cutting. A mediocre cook changes his knife every month—it is broken from hacking. This knife of mine has lasted nineteen years, carving thousands of oxen, yet its edge is as if newly whetted. The joints have spaces, but the blade has no thickness. With what has no thickness entering where there is space, there is surely ample room for the blade to move—this is why after nineteen years, its edge is as if newly whetted. Still, whenever I come to a complex joint, I see the difficulty, pause in caution, focus my gaze, slow my movement, and move the blade with utmost care. Then—*tiao!*—it falls apart like earth crumbling to the ground. I stand holding the knife, look around in satisfaction, wipe it clean, and put it away."

Lord Wenhui said: "Excellent! I have heard the words of Cook Ding and learned the secret of caring for life."

Gongwen Xuan saw the Right Master and exclaimed in surprise: "What sort of man is this? Why is he one-legged? Was it heaven, or was it man?"

The Right Master said: "It is heaven, not man. Heaven’s creation made him thus unique; man’s form is meant to have symmetry. This is why it is attributed to heaven, not man. A marsh pheasant takes ten steps to peck, a hundred steps to drink, yet it does not wish to be fed in a cage. Though its spirit would flourish, it would not be well.

When Lao Dan died, Qin Shi went to offer condolences. He wailed three times and left. A disciple asked: 'Was he not your master’s friend?' He replied: 'Yes.' 'Then is it proper to offer condolences in this way?' He said: 'Yes. At first, I thought he was a man of the Dao, but now I see he was not. When I entered to offer condolences just now, I saw old men weeping for him as if for their own sons, and young men weeping for him as if for their own mothers. In their gathering, there must have been those who wept without being compelled to weep. This is fleeing from heaven and betraying one’s nature—what the ancients called the punishment of fleeing from heaven. When he came, it was his time; when he departed, he followed the natural course. Abiding in the right time and following the natural course, sorrow and joy cannot enter. The ancients called this the supreme release from the bonds of the Lord.

As the fuel is exhausted, the fire is passed on, and its end is unknown."

---

*Chapter Four: The Secret of Caring for Life*

Yan Hui saw Confucius and asked to go on a mission. "Where to?" asked Confucius. "I will go to Wei." "What will you do there?"

Yan Hui said, "I have heard that the Lord of Wei is in his prime, yet his conduct is willful. He treats the lives of his people lightly, and the dead in his state are measured by marshlands as if they were hemp—his people have nowhere to turn. I once heard from you, Master, that one should leave a well-ordered state and go to a disordered one, for a physician’s gate is crowded with the sick. I wish to apply what I have heard to ponder its principles, hoping that his state might yet be saved."

Confucius said, "Alas! You will likely go only to be punished. The Dao does not tolerate杂乱;杂乱 leads to excess, excess to disturbance, disturbance to sorrow, and sorrow cannot be remedied. The perfected of old first established it within themselves before establishing it in others. If what is established within oneself is not yet settled, how can one attend to the conduct of a violent ruler?

"Do you also know whence virtue’s dissipation and knowledge’s emergence arise? Virtue dissipates into fame, and knowledge emerges from contention. Fame is a means of mutual oppression; knowledge is an instrument of contention. Both are inauspicious tools, not to be fully enacted. Moreover, one whose virtue is profound and whose sincerity is proven may not yet penetrate the people’s *qi*; one whose fame is widespread and who does not contend may not yet reach the people’s hearts. To forcefully present the language of仁义 and the carpenter’s墨 before a violent ruler is to make others resent what they themselves possess as beauty—this is called ‘sowing resentment.’ One who sows resentment will surely be resented in return. Are you not then sowing resentment for others?

"If you merely delight in the worthy and despise the unworthy, what need is there to seek distinction? If you do not issue commands, the lords will surely exploit others to vie for advantage. Your eyes will be dazzled, your countenance smoothed, your mouth will flatter, your demeanor will conform, and your heart will comply. This is like extinguishing fire with fire or flooding with water—it is called ‘adding to the excess.’ To follow such a beginning without end is to risk, with insincere grand words, certain death before a violent ruler.

"In ancient times, Jie killed Guan Longfeng, and Zhou killed Prince Bigan—both were men who cultivated themselves to humble the people and opposed their superiors. Thus, their lords used their cultivation to cast them out. These were lovers of fame. Formerly, Yao attacked Congzhi and Xu’ao, and Yu attacked Youhu. Their states became wastelands, their persons executed. Their use of arms did not cease, their pursuit of substance knew no end. These were seekers of fame and substance. Have you alone not heard of this? Fame and substance are what even the sages cannot overcome—how much less can you?

"Nevertheless, if you must have a way, come and tell me."

Yan Hui said, "If I am upright yet empty, diligent yet single-minded, will that suffice?"

Confucius said, "Alas, how could it? One who is full of yang, brash and ostentatious, whose colors shift unpredictably, is not one whom ordinary people can oppose. To follow others’ impulses in seeking to please their hearts is called ‘daily accumulating virtue that never forms’—how much less can great virtue be achieved? To cling rigidly without transformation, to conform outwardly while inwardly undervaluing, how could that ever suffice?

"Then let me be inwardly straight yet outwardly yielding, achieving while aligning with the ancients. To be inwardly straight is to follow Heaven. One who follows Heaven knows that the Son of Heaven and oneself are both children of Heaven. Why should one alone take credit when others praise one’s words, or blame when they do not? Such a one is called a child—this is what it means to follow Heaven. To be outwardly yielding is to follow others. Bowing the head and bending the knees are the rites of a minister. If all do so, how dare I not? To do what others do is to incur no blame—this is what it means to follow others. To achieve while aligning with the ancients is to follow antiquity. Though one’s words may instruct, they are but borrowed from reality. What antiquity possessed is not what I possess. Such a one, though straight, is not reproached—this is what it means to follow antiquity. Would this suffice?"

Confucius said, "Alas, how could it? With too many policies and laws, though one may be steadfast, one remains blameless. Yet this is all there is to it—how can it lead to transformation? You are still guided by your own mind."

Yan Hui said, "I have no further progress to make. May I ask the method?"

Confucius said, "Fast! I will tell you. To act while possessing—is that easy? Those who find it easy are not favored by luminous Heaven."

Yan Hui said, "My family is poor. For months now, I have neither drunk wine nor eaten meat. Would this suffice as fasting?"

Confucius replied, "That is the fasting for ancestral sacrifices, not the fasting of the mind."

Yan Hui asked, "May I inquire about the fasting of the mind?"

Confucius said, "Unify your will. Do not listen with the ears but listen with the mind. Do not listen with the mind but listen with qi. Listening stops at the ears; the mind stops at mere correspondence. Qi is what is empty and awaits things. Only the Dao gathers in emptiness. Emptiness is the fasting of the mind."

Yan Hui said, "Before I was instructed, I was truly myself. Once instructed, it is as though I no longer exist. Can this be called emptiness?"

Confucius said, "You have grasped it fully. Let me tell you: If you can enter and wander within its bounds without being moved by reputation, respond when moved, and cease when not—this is near perfection. To sever ties with the near is easy; to walk without treading the earth is difficult. To be employed by others is easy to feign; to be employed by Heaven is difficult to feign. One has heard of flying with wings, but never of flying without wings; one has heard of knowing with knowledge, but never of knowing without knowledge. Look upon that emptiness—where the empty chamber generates brightness, and auspiciousness comes to rest. If one does not come to rest, this is called sitting while galloping. To follow the ears and eyes while inwardly communicating but outwardly cutting off the mind’s knowledge—even spirits and gods will come to dwell, let alone men. This is the transformation of the myriad things, the pivot of Yu and Shun, the ultimate path of Fuxi and the near-perfected—how much more so for those who are scattered?

When Viscount Gaoyang of Ye was about to be sent as an envoy to Qi, he asked Confucius, 'The king’s charge to me is most weighty. The Qi court treats envoys with great respect but does not hasten to match their rank. Even a common man cannot be swayed—how much less a feudal lord? I am deeply apprehensive. You once told me: In all affairs, whether great or small, few succeed without following the Dao to achieve harmony. If the affair does not succeed, one must face the calamities of human relations; if it does succeed, one must face the calamities of yin and yang. Only one of power can succeed or fail without harm. I eat coarse food and do not seek refinement, yet I am one who desires no cooling. Now, upon receiving my charge this morning, I drink ice by evening—am I not inwardly burning? Before I have even reached the truth of the matter, I already face the calamities of yin and yang. If the affair does not succeed, I must face the calamities of human relations. These two outcomes are beyond what a minister can bear. Pray, instruct me further.'

Confucius said, 'Under Heaven there are two great precepts: one is fate, the other is duty. A child’s love for parents is fate—it cannot be untied from the heart. A minister’s service to his lord is duty—there is no place between Heaven and Earth where it does not apply. These are the great precepts. Thus, in serving one’s parents, one finds peace without choosing the place—this is the utmost of filial piety. In serving one’s lord, one finds peace without choosing the task—this is the height of loyalty. In serving one’s own mind, to remain unmoved by joy or sorrow before what lies ahead, to know what cannot be helped and find peace in it as fate—this is the utmost of power. As a minister and child, one is fundamentally bound by what cannot be avoided. To act according to the truth of affairs while forgetting oneself—how could one have leisure to delight in life and fear death? Your conduct, sir, is exemplary.

Let me repeat what I have heard: In close dealings, one must be worn smooth by trust; in distant dealings, one must be loyal in speech, for speech may be transmitted. Transmitting words of mutual joy or mutual anger is the most difficult thing in the world. Where there is mutual joy, there is much exaggerated praise; where there is mutual anger, there is much exaggerated slander. All exaggeration is falsehood; falsehood destroys trust, and the destruction of trust brings calamity to the transmitter. Thus, the standard teaching says: Transmit the constant truth, do not transmit exaggerated words—then you will be near to wholeness.

Moreover, those who contend with skill and force begin in brightness but often end in darkness; when excess prevails, there is much cunning. Those who drink wine with ritual begin in order but often end in chaos; when excess prevails, there is much strange delight. All affairs are like this: they begin in good faith but often end in baseness. What starts simply will surely become vast. Words are like wind and waves; actions are like gain and loss. Wind and waves are easily stirred; gain and loss are easily endangered. Thus, anger arises without cause, and clever words lead to biased speech. When beasts die, they do not choose their cries; their gasping breath is desperate, and from this arises a heart of cruelty. When scrutiny is too severe, there will surely be an unworthy heart to respond, though one does not know why. If one does not know why, who can know how it will end? Thus, the standard teaching says: Do not alter commands, do not urge completion—excess leads to transgression. Altering commands and urging completion endanger affairs. Beauty is achieved in duration; evil, once done, cannot be undone. Can one not be cautious?

To ride things with a wandering mind, to entrust oneself to the unavoidable to nurture the center—this is perfection. What need is there for action in return? It is best to convey the charge faithfully—this is the difficult part.'

Yan Wei was about to be sent to transmit the heir apparent of Duke Ling of Wei. He asked Lian Boyu, 'There is a man here whose power is Heaven’s executioner. To deal with him without method endangers our state; to deal with him with method endangers myself.'"

"His knowledge suffices only to recognize others’ faults but not their causes. In such a case, what should I do?"

Lian Boyu replied, "Excellent question! Be vigilant, be cautious—rectify yourself first. In form, nothing surpasses compliance; in heart, nothing surpasses harmony. Yet even these two have their perils. Compliance must not become submission, nor harmony projection. If form submits, you risk ruin, destruction, collapse, or downfall. If the heart projects, you risk reputation, fame, calamity, or misfortune.

If he acts like a child, join him as a child. If he acts without boundaries, join him without boundaries. If he acts without limits, join him without limits. By thus attaining harmony, you enter flawlessness.

Do you not know the mantis? It brandished its arms to block the chariot wheel, unaware of its own incapacity—such was the beauty of its talent. Beware, be cautious. To accumulate praise and beauty while provoking him is perilously close.

Do you not know the tiger-keeper? He dares not feed it living prey, lest its killing rage be aroused; nor whole prey, lest its tearing rage be provoked. He times its hunger and satiety, understanding its angry heart. Though tiger and man are of different kinds, the tiger fawns on its keeper—this is compliance. Thus, those it kills are those who resist.

The horse-lover uses a basket for dung and a gourd for urine. Yet when a mosquito or fly lands, and the groom fails to brush it away in time, the horse may break its bit, smash its head, or shatter its chest. Love may reach extremes where care is lost—can one not be cautious?

Carpenter Shi traveled to Qi and reached Quyuan, where he saw a community altar tree. Its girth could shade an ox, its trunk spanned a hundred spans, its height towered over the mountains, and its branches began only at eighty feet. A dozen of its side branches could serve as boats. The crowd of onlookers was like a marketplace, yet the master carpenter did not glance at it, walking past without pause.

His disciple, having feasted his eyes, caught up to Carpenter Shi and said, 'Since I took up the axe to follow you, master, I have never seen timber so magnificent. Why did you not even look, walking past without stopping?'

'Enough,' he replied. 'Say no more.'"

A useless tree—if made into a boat, it would sink; if made into a coffin, it would rot swiftly; if made into vessels, they would break quickly; if used for doors, it would ooze sap; if planted as a tree, it would be devoured by worms. This is a timber of no worth, utterly unusable, and thus it attains such longevity.

Carpenter Shi returned home, and the altar tree appeared to him in a dream, saying: "With what would you compare me? Would you liken me to the so-called 'fine timber'? Consider the crabapple, pear, orange, pomelo, and other fruit-bearing trees: when their fruits ripen, they are plucked and shamed; their great branches are broken, their small branches stripped. This is how their usefulness brings suffering to their lives, so they do not live out their natural years but perish midway, struck down by the world’s demands. All things are like this. As for me, I have long sought to be of no use, and only now, nearing death, have I attained it—this is my great usefulness. Had you been of use, could you have grown so vast? Moreover, you and I are both things—why judge one another as things? And you, a dying man of no worth, what could you know of a useless tree?"

Carpenter Shi awoke and pondered his dream. His disciple asked: "If it sought uselessness, why serve as a community altar?" He replied: "Hush, say no more. It merely lodges there, enduring the scorn of those who do not understand it. Were it not an altar tree, it would long since have been felled. Yet what it preserves differs from the common, and to praise it with conventional standards—is that not far off the mark?"

Master Nanbo Zijing was wandering in the hills of Shang when he saw an extraordinary tree. A thousand four-horse chariots could shelter beneath it, its shade concealing all they carried. Ziyu said: "What tree is this? It must possess rare timber!" Looking up at its slender branches, he saw they were gnarled and unfit for beams or rafters; looking down at its great roots, he saw they were split and useless for coffins. Chewing its leaves, his mouth blistered and burned; inhaling its scent, he became drunk and delirious for three days without respite. Ziyu said: "This is truly a useless tree, and thus it has grown so vast. Alas, the spirit-like man—such is his uselessness!"

In Song there was a family named Ke who tended catalpa, cypress, and mulberry trees. Those of arm’s span or less, sought for monkey perches, were felled; those of three or four spans, sought for grand and ornate halls, were felled; those of seven or eight spans, sought by wealthy nobles and merchants for beams and pillars, were felled. Thus they did not live out their natural years but perished midway under the axe—this is the calamity of usefulness. Therefore, the white-foreheaded ox, the upturned-snouted pig, and the man with hemorrhoids are deemed unfit to cross the river. The shamans and priests know this well—these are what they call inauspicious. Yet this is precisely what the spirit-like man calls most auspicious.

There was a man named Zhili Shu whose chin nestled in his navel, shoulders rose above his crown, the nape of his neck pointed to the sky, his five viscera sat atop his frame, and his two thighs pressed against his ribs. Sewing and washing he did, enough to fill his mouth; sifting chaff and winnowing grain, enough to feed ten men. When the ruler conscripted soldiers, Zhili Shu waved his arms among the crowds; when the ruler called for labor, Zhili Shu, citing his chronic ailment, was exempt from service. When the ruler distributed grain to the infirm, he received three bushels and ten bundles of firewood. One whose form was so disjointed could still nourish his body and live out his natural years—how much more so one whose virtue is thus disjointed?

Confucius traveled to Chu, and Jie Yu, the madman of Chu, wandered to his gate, singing:
*"Phoenix! Phoenix!
How your virtue has waned!
The age to come cannot be awaited,
The age gone by cannot be pursued.
When the world follows the Dao, the sage achieves;
When the world loses the Dao, the sage survives.
In this present time, one barely escapes punishment.
Blessing is lighter than a feather, yet none know how to bear it;
Calamity is heavier than the earth, yet none know how to avoid it.
Enough! Enough!
To approach others with virtue!
Perilous! Perilous!
To tread where lines are drawn.
Beware the brambles! Beware the brambles!
Let them not harm my path.
My path winds and turns—
Let them not harm my feet."*

Mountain trees bring ruin upon themselves; fat in the flame consumes itself. Cinnamon is edible, so it is felled; lacquer is useful, so it is slashed. All know the usefulness of the useful, yet none know the usefulness of the useless.

**Chapter Five: Signs of Full Virtue**

In Lu there was a man named Wang Fu, who had lost his feet. Those who followed him were as numerous as those who followed Confucius. Chang Ji asked Confucius, "Wang Fu is a man without feet, yet those who follow him divide Lu equally with you. He stands without teaching, sits without discussing; yet those who go to him empty return full."

Is there not indeed a teaching without words, a shaping of the heart without form? What manner of man is this?

Confucius said, "This Master is a sage. I am but a latecomer who has yet to follow him. I shall take him as my teacher—how much more should those who are not my equal! Why limit ourselves to Lu? I shall lead all under Heaven to follow him."

Chang Ji said, "He is a man without feet, yet he surpasses even a king. How far he stands above the common! If this is so, how does he employ his mind?"

Confucius said, "Life and death are great matters, yet they do not alter him. Though heaven and earth should collapse, he would not be lost with them. He examines what is without artifice and does not shift with things. He governs the transformations of things while holding fast to their root."

Chang Ji said, "What do you mean?"

Confucius said, "Viewed from what makes them different, the liver and gallbladder are as distant as Chu and Yue. Viewed from what makes them the same, the myriad things are all one. One who is thus does not know what the ears and eyes deem fitting but roams the mind in the harmony of virtue. He sees what unites things and does not see what they lose. To him, the loss of a foot is like the shedding of dust."

Chang Ji said, "He cultivates himself through his understanding, attains his heart through his heart. Having attained his constant heart, why do things gather to him?"

Confucius said, "Men do not mirror themselves in flowing water but in still water. Only stillness can still the many. Endowed by earth, only the pine and cypress remain green in winter and summer. Endowed by heaven, only Shun was upright. Fortunate to have upright life, he set others upright. The proof of preserving the beginning lies in fearlessness. A single brave warrior can charge into nine armies. Those who seek fame and bind themselves to it are like this—how much more one who governs heaven and earth, stores the myriad things, treats the six senses as mere lodging, takes the ears and eyes as mere images, unifies knowledge with what is known, and whose heart never dies! He will choose a day to ascend to the heights, and men will follow him. How could he deign to make things his concern?"

Shentu Jia, a man without feet, studied with Zheng Zichan under Bohun Wuren. Zichan said to Shentu Jia, "If I go out first, you stay; if you go out first, I will stay." The next day, they again sat together in the same hall. Zichan said to Shentu Jia, "If I go out first, you stay; if you go out first, I will stay. Now I am about to go out—can you stay, or not? Moreover, when you see the one in power, you do not avoid him. Do you consider yourself his equal?"

Shentu Jia said, "In the Master’s gate, are there truly those in power like this? Do you delight in your position of power and look down on others? I have heard that when a mirror is bright, dust does not settle; if it settles, it is not bright. When one dwells long with the worthy, one commits no faults. Now, what you value most is the Master, yet you still speak like this—is this not a fault?"

Zichan said, "You are already like this, yet you still vie with Yao in goodness. Do you not reflect that your virtue is insufficient?"

Shentu Jia said, "Those who justify their faults and claim they should not have lost are many; those who do not justify their faults and yet should not have perished are few. Knowing one cannot help but accept fate, only those with virtue can do so. Roaming within Yi’s bow casing, the center is the center of the earth, yet one may still miss—this is fate. Many are those with whole feet who laugh at my lack of feet. I would have raged, but when I came to the Master’s place, I was cast down and returned. Did the Master not wash me with goodness? I have followed you for nineteen years and never knew I was a man without feet. Now you roam with me within the form, yet you seek me outside the form—is this not a fault?"

Zichan, startled, changed his expression and said, "Say no more."

In Lu there was a man without toes named Shushan Wuzhi, who came to see Confucius on his heels. Confucius said, "You were not careful and have already suffered such misfortune. Though you come now, what can be done?"

Wuzhi said, "I was ignorant of my duties and carelessly used my body, which is why I lost my foot. Now I come because what is more honorable than the foot still remains, and I strive to preserve it. Heaven covers all without exception; earth bears all without exception. I took you, Master, to be heaven and earth—how could I have known you would be like this?"

Confucius said, "I am indeed narrow-minded. Why do you not enter? Let me expound what I have heard."

Wuzhi departed. Confucius said to his disciples, "Strive! This Wuzhi, a man without feet, still strives to learn to make up for his past misdeeds—how much more should a man of full virtue!"

Wuzhi said to Lao Dan, "Is Confucius not far from the perfect man? Why does he persistently take learning as his guest? He seeks to be known for strange and fantastical fame, unaware that the perfect man regards this as fetters and shackles."

Lao Dan said, "Why not simply lead him to see life and death as a single thread, the permissible and impermissible as one continuum, and thus free him from his fetters? Would that not suffice?"

Wuzhi said, "Heaven has punished him—how can it be undone?"

Duke Ai of Lu asked Confucius, "In Wei there is an ugly man named Jifu Tuo. Men who live with him grow attached and cannot leave. Women who see him beg their parents, saying, ‘Rather than be another man’s wife, I would be this man’s concubine’—there are more than ten such cases. He never initiates speech but always harmonizes with others. He holds no lordly position to save men from death, nor wealth to fill men’s bellies. Moreover, he is ugly enough to startle all under heaven. He harmonizes but does not lead, and his knowledge does not extend beyond his domain. Yet men and women gather before him—he must possess something extraordinary."

Duke Ai summoned him and observed him, and indeed his ugliness startled all under heaven. Before even a month had passed in his company, the Duke took interest in his character; before a full year had elapsed, the Duke trusted him. When the state had no minister, the Duke offered him the throne. He responded with hesitation, and when he spoke, his words were vague. The Duke felt ashamed, yet ultimately entrusted him with the state. Soon after, he left the Duke and departed. The Duke grieved as though bereft, as if the state held no joy without him. What manner of man was this?

Confucius said, "I once traveled to Chu and happened upon a piglet nursing from its dead mother. After a moment, it shrank back in alarm and fled. It saw no self in her, no likeness to itself. What it loved in its mother was not her form but that which animated her form. Warriors who die in battle are buried without ritual finery; a man who loses his feet is no longer cherished for his shoes—both have lost their root. When the Son of Heaven selects his consorts, they are not to pare their nails or pierce their ears; once married, they are not sent on further errands. Even those whose forms remain whole are thus esteemed—how much more one whose virtue is whole? Now, this Jifu Tuo has won trust without speaking, affection without merit, and been offered a state, only fearing he might refuse. He must be one whose talents are complete and whose virtue remains unmanifest."

Duke Ai asked, "What does it mean for talents to be complete?"

Confucius said, "Life and death, survival and ruin, poverty and wealth, worthiness and unworthiness, slander and praise, hunger and thirst, cold and heat—these are the changes of affairs, the workings of fate. They alternate day and night before us, yet knowledge cannot fathom their beginnings. Thus, they should not disturb harmony or enter the mind’s sanctuary. One who makes others joyful and at ease, who remains open without losing balance, who ensures day and night flow without stagnation, bringing springtime to all things—this is one whose heart connects and gives rise to the seasons. This is what it means for talents to be complete.

"What does it mean for virtue to remain unmanifest? Stillness is the fullness of settled water. It may serve as a model, for it preserves within while remaining undisturbed without. Virtue is the cultivation of perfect harmony. One whose virtue remains unmanifest cannot be separated from things."

Another day, Duke Ai told Minzi what he had heard: "At first, I thought ruling the world from the southern throne, holding the people’s guiding principles and fearing their deaths, was the height of understanding. Now, hearing the words of a perfected person, I fear I lack true substance, that I have lightly used my body and lost my state. Between Confucius and me, there is no lord and minister—only a bond of virtue."

Minjiao, the hunchbacked and lipless, advised Duke Ling of Wei, who delighted in him; yet when he saw whole men, their necks seemed frail. The potbellied and emaciated advised Duke Huan of Qi, who delighted in him; yet when he saw whole men, their necks seemed frail. Thus, virtue may excel while form is forgotten. Do men forget what should be forgotten and remember what should not? This is called true forgetting.

Therefore, the sage wanders freely, where knowledge becomes a snare, covenants become glue, virtue becomes a bond, and skill becomes trade. The sage does not scheme—why use knowledge? Does not sever—why use glue? Suffers no loss—why use virtue? Engages in no commerce—why trade? These four are the fasting of heaven. Heaven’s fasting is heaven’s nourishment. Having received nourishment from heaven, why rely on man?

One may have the form of a man but lack human emotions. Having the form of a man, one mingles with others; lacking human emotions, right and wrong do not cling to one. How small and insignificant—this is how one belongs to mankind. How vast and great—this is how one completes heaven alone.

Huizi said to Zhuangzi, "Do men truly have no emotions?"

Zhuangzi said, "Yes."

Huizi said, "If a man has no emotions, how can he be called a man?"

Zhuangzi said, "The Dao gives him appearance, heaven gives him form—how can he not be called a man?"

Huizi said, "If he is called a man, how can he have no emotions?"

Zhuangzi said, "This is not what I mean by emotions. What I call having no emotions is that a man does not inwardly harm his body with likes and dislikes, but constantly follows nature without adding to life."

Huizi said, "Without adding to life, how can one preserve one’s body?"

Zhuangzi said, "The Dao gives him appearance, heaven gives him form—do not inwardly harm your body with likes and dislikes. Now you exhaust your spirit externally, weary your essence, lean against a tree and chant, rest on a withered catalpa and doze. Heaven chose your form, yet you clamor about ‘hard’ and ‘white.’"

*The Classic of Southern Florescence, Scroll One ends here.*

*The Classic of Southern Florescence, Scroll Two: Inner Chapters*
**Chapter Six: The Teacher of the Dao**

To know what heaven does and what man does—this is perfection. To know what heaven does is to be born of heaven; to know what man does is to use what one’s knowledge knows to nourish what one’s knowledge does not know, thus fulfilling one’s heavenly years without dying prematurely. This is the fullness of knowledge.

Yet there is a danger. Knowledge depends on something to be correct, but what it depends on is inherently uncertain. How can we know that what I call heaven is not man, or what I call man is not heaven? Only when there is a true person is there true knowledge.

What is a true person? The true persons of old did not resist the few, did not boast of success, did not scheme for office. Thus, they erred without regret and succeeded without self-satisfaction. Thus, they climbed heights without trembling, entered water without getting wet, entered fire without feeling heat. Their knowledge could ascend to the Dao in this way.

The true persons of old slept without dreaming, woke without worry, ate without relishing, and breathed deeply. True persons breathed from their heels, while ordinary people breathe from their throats. Those who are forced to submit speak as though choking. Those whose desires run deep have shallow heavenly mechanisms.

The true persons of old knew neither delight in life nor aversion to death. Their departure brought no joy, their return no resistance; they simply went lightly away and came lightly back. They did not forget their beginning nor seek their end. They received life with delight and forgot it to return—this is what it means not to use the mind to cast aside the Dao, not to use man to aid heaven. This is called the true person.

Such a one’s mind is serene, their countenance still, their brow tranquil. Cold as autumn, warm as spring, their joy and anger flow with the four seasons, harmonizing with things yet no one knows their limit. Thus, when the sage employs arms, they may destroy a state yet lose no hearts. Their beneficence extends through ten thousand generations, yet they do not act out of love for others. Therefore, one who delights in harmonizing with things is no sage; one who has partiality is not benevolent; one who follows the seasons is not worthy; one who does not comprehend profit and harm is no gentleman; one who acts for fame and loses the self is no scholar; one who destroys the self yet is not true is no servant of others.

Those like Hu Buxie, Wu Guang, Boyi, Shuqi, Jizi, Xuyu, Ji Ta, and Shentu Di—these are servants of others’ service, followers of others’ paths, unable to follow their own. The true persons of old bore righteousness yet did not form cliques, seemed insufficient yet did not seek fulfillment. They were yielding yet not weak, expansive yet not ostentatious. Their demeanor was joyful yet solemn, their actions seemed compelled. Their presence softened my countenance, their virtue stilled my power. They were stern as the world, yet untamable; connected as though fond of seclusion, yet their words were forgotten. They took punishment as their body, ritual as their wings, knowledge as their timing, and virtue as their guide.

Taking punishment as their body, they were resolute in execution. Taking ritual as their wings, they moved through the world. Taking knowledge as their timing, they acted only when compelled. Taking virtue as their guide, they spoke as though reaching hills with those who have feet—yet others truly believed them to be diligent in conduct. Thus, their preferences were unified, and their aversions were unified. Their unity was one, and their disunity was one. Those who were one followed heaven; those who were not one followed man. Heaven and man do not overcome one another—this is called the true person.

Life and death are fate, as constant as night and day—this is heaven. What man cannot partake in is the nature of things. They take heaven as their father and love it still—how much more that which transcends it? They take having a lord as better than themselves and die for it still—how much more that which is true?

When springs dry, fish find themselves on land, wetting one another with their breath, moistening one another with their foam—better to forget one another in rivers and lakes. Rather than praising Yao and condemning Jie, it is better to forget both and be transformed by the Dao.

The great clod burdens me with form, labors me with life, eases me with old age, and rests me with death. Thus, to be good to my life is to be good to my death. Hiding a boat in a ravine or a mountain in a marsh seems secure, yet in the dead of night, a strong man may carry them away, and the unaware do not know. Hiding the small and large appropriately still leaves room for delay. But to hide the world within the world leaves no room for delay—this is the great truth of things.

Even those who cherish their human form—if the human form is but one of ten thousand transformations yet to reach their limit, how immeasurable must their joy be? Thus, the sage wanders where things cannot delay and all endure. To be good at early death and old age, at beginnings and endings—people emulate this, let alone that on which the ten thousand things depend and the one transformation awaits.

The Dao has essence and trustworthiness, yet is without action or form. It can be transmitted but not received, attained but not seen. It is self-originating, self-rooted, existing before heaven and earth, enduring since ancient times. It spirits forth ghosts and gods, gives birth to heaven and earth. It is before the Supreme Ultimate yet not high, below the Six Directions yet not deep. It preceded heaven and earth yet is not ancient, older than antiquity yet not aged.

Xiwei obtained it and thereby lifted heaven and earth. Fuxi obtained it and thereby embraced the mother of qi. The Northern Dipper obtained it and has never faltered since. The sun and moon obtained it and have never ceased. Kanpi obtained it and thereby embraced Kunlun. Feng Yi obtained it and thereby roamed the great rivers. Jian Wu obtained it and thereby dwelt on Mount Tai. The Yellow Emperor obtained it and thereby ascended to the cloudy heavens. Zhuanxu obtained it and thereby dwelt in the Dark Palace. Yuqiang obtained it and stood at the North Pole. The Queen Mother of the West obtained it and sat in Shaoguang. None know its beginning, none know its end.

Pengzu obtained it, reaching from the time of Yu to the Five Hegemons. Fu Yue obtained it and served Wu Ding, ruling all under heaven, riding the tail of the Eastern Well and the handle of the Dipper, ranking among the stars.

Nanbo Zigui asked Nü Ou, “You are advanced in years, yet your complexion is like a child’s. How is this?” He replied, “I have heard the Dao.” Nanbo Zigui said, “Can the Dao be learned?” He said, “No. How could it? You are not the right person. Bu Liangyi has the talent of a sage but not the Dao of a sage. I have the Dao of a sage but not the talent of a sage. I wished to teach him, hoping he might truly become a sage. If not, teaching the Dao of a sage to one with the talent of a sage would be easy. I still guarded and taught him. After three days, he was able to put the world outside himself. Once he had put the world outside, I guarded him again. After seven days, he was able to put things outside himself. Once he had put things outside, I guarded him again. After nine days, he was able to put life outside himself.”

Once he had put life outside himself, only then was he able to attain clarity at dawn. Having attained clarity at dawn, only then was he able to perceive the One. Having perceived the One, only then was there no past and no present. Having transcended past and present, only then was he able to enter into that which is neither dead nor alive.

That which kills life does not die; that which gives life to life does not live. As a thing, it sends nothing away, welcomes nothing, destroys nothing, and completes nothing. Its name is Serene Repose. Serene Repose is that which attains completion through serenity.

Nanbo Zigui said, “From whom did you alone hear this?”
He replied, “I heard it from the disciple of Fu Mo, the disciple of Fu Mo heard it from the grandson of Luo Song, the grandson of Luo Song heard it from Zhan Ming, Zhan Ming heard it from Nie Xu, Nie Xu heard it from Xu Yi, Xu Yi heard it from Yu Shu, Yu Shu heard it from Xuan Ming, Xuan Ming heard it from Can Liao, and Can Liao heard it from Yi Shi.”

Zisi, Ziyu, Zili, and Zilai said to one another, “Who can take non-being as the head, life as the spine, and death as the tail, and who knows that life, death, existence, and extinction are one body? That one I would befriend.” The four looked at one another and smiled, their hearts in perfect accord, and so they became friends.

Soon after, Ziyu fell ill, and Zisi went to inquire about him.
He said, “How magnificent!”

The Creator would make you thus confined and deformed?
Your spine curves like a bow, your back humps, your five organs press upward, your navel sinks into your belly, your shoulders tower above your crown, and your chin points to the sky. The imbalance of yin and yang qi has thrown you into disorder, yet your heart remains at ease, untroubled. Leaning on your staff, you gaze into the well and sigh, “Ah! Would the Creator make me thus confined and deformed?”

Zisi said, “Do you resent this?”
He replied, “No, why should I resent it? If, little by little, my left arm were transformed into a rooster, I would use it to herald the dawn. If, little by little, my right arm were transformed into a crossbow, I would use it to hunt for roasted magpie. If, little by little, my body were transformed into a wheel, and my spirit into a horse, I would mount it—why ever change steeds? Moreover, what comes is timely; what goes is fitting. To rest in timeliness and abide in fittingness, neither joy nor sorrow can enter. This is what the ancients called ‘suspended release,’ yet those who cannot release themselves are bound by things. For long now, things have not overcome Heaven—why should I resent it?”

Soon after, Zilai fell ill, gasping as if near death. His wife and children gathered around, weeping. Zili went to inquire after him and cried, “Shoo! Away! Do not disturb his transformation.” Leaning against the doorframe, he said to him, “How wondrous is the Creator! What will it make of you next? Where will it send you? Will it make you into a rat’s liver? A bug’s arm?”

Zilai replied, “Wherever a child goes—east, west, south, or north—he follows his parents’ command. Yin and yang are to man no less than parents. If they press me toward death and I resist, I would be obstinate—what fault would be theirs? The great earth burdens me with form, labors me with life, eases me with old age, and rests me with death. Thus, to make my life good is to make my death good. Now, if a great smith were casting metal, and the metal leapt up, crying, ‘I must become a Moye sword!’ the smith would surely deem it ill-omened metal. Now, if one were to assume human form and cry, ‘A human! A human!’ the Creator would surely deem him an ill-omened man. If we take heaven and earth as a great furnace and the Creator as a great smith, where could we go and not be well?”

Then he fell asleep as if in completion and awoke as if in bloom.

Zisang Hu, Mengzi Fan, and Zi Qin Zhang became friends, saying to one another, “Who can join in joining, act in non-acting? Who can ascend the heavens, roam the mists, rove without limit, forget life, and never reach an end?” The two looked at one another and smiled, their hearts in perfect accord, and so they became friends.

By and by, Zisang Hu died. Before he was buried, Confucius, hearing of it, sent Zigong to assist with the rites. One wove a song, another strummed the zither, and together they sang in harmony:
“Ah, Zisang Hu!
Ah, Zisang Hu!
You have returned to your true nature,
While I remain a mere man.”

Zigong hurried forward and said, “May I ask whether singing before the corpse accords with ritual?”
The two looked at each other and smiled, saying, “What does he know of the meaning of ritual?”

Zigong returned and reported to Confucius, saying, “What sort of men are they? They cultivate no conduct, treat their bodies as external, and sing before the corpse without changing countenance—there is no name for them. What sort of men are they?”

Confucius said, “They roam beyond the realm; I roam within it. Beyond and within do not meet, yet I sent you to offer condolences—how boorish I was! They are now companions to the Creator, roaming as one with the qi of heaven and earth.”

They regard life as an excrescent wart, death as the bursting of a boil—how could such men know where life and death, before and after, truly lie? Borrowing from other things, they lodge in the same body; they forget liver and gall, disregard ears and eyes. Reversing and repeating, beginning and ending, they know not head nor tail. Vagrant beyond the dust and grime, they wander carefree in the work of non-action. How could they trouble themselves with the fuss of worldly ritual, seeking to impress the eyes and ears of the crowd?

Zigong said, “Then what Way do you, Master, rely upon?”
Confucius said, “I am one whom Heaven has punished. Yet even so, I share it with you.”
Zigong said, “May I ask what that Way is?”
Confucius said, “Fish are made for water, as men are made for the Dao. Those made for water thrive when they carve out ponds; those made for the Dao find life settled without need for action. Thus it is said: fish forget one another in rivers and lakes, men forget one another in the arts of the Dao.”

Zigong said, “May I ask about the *qi*-man?”
Confucius said, “The *qi*-man is *qi* to men but a companion to Heaven. Thus it is said: the petty man of Heaven is the gentleman of men; the gentleman of men is the petty man of Heaven.”

Yan Hui asked Confucius, saying, “When Mengsun Cai’s mother died, he wept without tears, felt no grief in his heart, and showed no sorrow in mourning. Lacking these three, he was yet praised throughout Lu for his mastery of mourning. Is it truly possible to gain a name without the reality? I find it most strange.”
Confucius said, “Mengsun has exhausted the matter—he has advanced beyond knowledge. Though he sought to simplify, he could not; yet he has already simplified. Mengsun does not know why he lives, nor why he dies; he does not know what to pursue first, nor what to pursue later. Transformed into a thing, he awaits whatever transformations he does not yet know. And even as he is about to transform, how could he know that he will not transform?
