Even as he is about to remain unchanged, how could he know that he has not already transformed? You and I are but dreamers who have not yet awakened. Moreover, he may be startled by a change in form, but his heart remains unharmed; he may dwell in a new abode, but his essence does not die. Mengsun alone is awake—others weep, so he weeps too; that is simply his nature. Besides, we all call this ‘I’ of ours ‘I,’ but how do we know what we mean by ‘our I’? You may dream you are a bird soaring in the sky, or a fish diving into the deep. But do you know whether the one speaking now is awake or dreaming? When joy comes, you cannot laugh in time; when laughter comes, you cannot arrange it. Accept the arrangement and follow transformation—then you enter the vast oneness of Heaven.”

Yi’erzi went to see Xu You.
Xu You said, “What did Yao give you?”
Yi’erzi said, “Yao told me, ‘You must personally practice benevolence and righteousness and clearly distinguish right and wrong.’”
Xu You said, “Then why have you come? Yao has already branded you with benevolence and righteousness and mutilated you with right and wrong—how will you wander the paths of carefree, uninhibited, and ever-shifting freedom?”
Yi’erzi said, “Even so, I wish to roam within their bounds.”
Xu You said, “Not so. The blind cannot appreciate the beauty of brows and eyes; the colorblind cannot distinguish blue and yellow. Yi’erzi said, “Yet Wu Zhuang lost her beauty, Ju Liang lost his strength, and the Yellow Emperor lost his knowledge—all in the space of the forge and hammer. How do we know the Creator will not remove my brands and mend my mutilations, allowing me to ride perfection and follow you, Master?”
Xu You said, “Ah, who can say? Let me tell you the general idea. My teacher! My teacher! He slays the myriad things yet is not cruel; his blessings reach ten thousand generations yet he is not benevolent. Older than high antiquity, he is not aged; he covers and bears heaven and earth, carves and shapes all forms, yet is not skillful. This is the realm in which he wanders.”

Yan Hui said, “I have made progress.”
Confucius said, “What do you mean?”
Yan Hui said, “I have forgotten benevolence and righteousness.”
Confucius said, “Good, but not yet enough.”
Another day, he saw Confucius again and said, “I have made progress.”
Confucius said, “What do you mean?”
Yan Hui said, “I have forgotten ritual and music.”
Confucius said, “Good, but not yet enough.”
Another day, he saw Confucius again and said, “I have made progress.”
Confucius said, “What do you mean?”
Yan Hui said, “I sit and forget.”
Confucius started and said, “What do you mean by ‘sit and forget’?”
Yan Hui said, “I cast off my limbs and trunk, dim my faculties and intelligence, leave my form and abandon knowledge, and merge with the Great Pervasion. This is what I mean by ‘sit and forget.’”
Confucius said, “To merge is to have no preferences; to transform is to have no constancy. You are indeed worthy! I beg to follow after you.”

Yan Yu and Zi Sang were friends. After ten days of soaking rain, Yan Yu said, “Zi Sang must be ill.” He wrapped some rice and went to feed him. Arriving at Zi Sang’s gate, he heard something between singing and weeping, as Zi Sang plucked his zither and cried:

“Father? Mother?
Heaven? Man?
Ah—”

His voice could not bear the strain, and he hurriedly struck the strings in time. Yan Yu entered and asked, “Why do you sing like this?”

Zi Sang replied, “I pondered who brought me to this extremity, but I could not find the answer. Would my parents wish me to be poor? Heaven covers all without partiality; Earth bears all without partiality. Would Heaven and Earth single me out for poverty? I sought the one who caused this, but I could not find him. Yet here I am at this extremity—it must be fate!”

---

*The Nanhua True Classic, Scroll Two: Inner Chapters, “Fit for Emperors,” Chapter Seven*

Nie Que questioned Wang Ni four times, and four times Wang Ni professed ignorance. Nie Que leapt up in great joy and went to tell Pu Yi Zi.

Pu Yi Zi said, “Now you have truly come to know it? The Lords of Yu do not compare to the Lord of Tai. The Lords of Yu, though they hoarded virtue to win men’s hearts, did gain men’s hearts, yet they never escaped the realm of the unnatural. The Lord of Tai, in his outward demeanor, was unhurried; in his awakening, he was all-pervasive. At times he took himself for a horse, at times for an ox. His understanding transcended form and abandoned knowledge, merging with the Great Pervasion—this is what is called ‘sitting in forgetfulness.’”

Confucius said, “To merge is to have no preferences; to transform is to have no constancy. You are indeed worthy! I beg to follow after you.”

---

Yan Yu and Zi Sang were friends. After ten days of soaking rain, Yan Yu said, “Zi Sang must be ill.” He wrapped some rice and went to feed him. Arriving at Zi Sang’s gate, he heard something between singing and weeping, as Zi Sang plucked his zither and cried:

“Father? Mother?
Heaven? Man?”

“Man, then? Yet his voice could not bear the strain, and so he hastened to recite his verse.”

Yan Yu entered and asked, “Why do you sing your poem in this manner?”

Zi Sang replied, “I ponder who has brought me to this extremity, yet I cannot find the answer. Would my parents wish me to be poor? Heaven covers all without partiality; earth bears all without favor. Would heaven and earth single me out for poverty? I seek the one who has done this, yet I cannot find him. Nevertheless, to reach this extremity—this is fate, is it not?”

Nie Que questioned Wang Ni four times, and four times Wang Ni professed ignorance. Thereupon Nie Que leapt up in great joy and went to tell Master Pu Yi.

Master Pu Yi said, “Have you only now come to know this? The Lord of Yu fell short, yet in his awakening he was all-pervasive, at times taking himself for a horse, at times for an ox. His knowledge was true to his nature, his virtue most genuine, and he had never entered the realm of man.”

Jian Wu met Jie Yu the Madman. Jie Yu asked, “What did Ri Zhongshi tell you at noon?”

Jian Wu replied, “He told me that a ruler of men must issue decrees, standards, principles, and measures from himself—who would dare not obey and be transformed?”

Jie Yu said, “This is deceptive virtue. In governing the world, it is like crossing the sea by digging a river or making a flea carry a mountain. Does the sage’s governance concern externals? He rectifies himself and then acts, merely ensuring that those capable of the task can fulfill it. Moreover, the bird flies high to avoid the harm of nets and arrows, while the mole digs deep beneath the sacred mound to escape the calamity of pitfalls—yet you would call these two creatures ignorant?”

Tian Gen was wandering at Yin Yang when he came to the banks of the Liao River. There he encountered an unnamed man and asked him, “May I inquire about governing the world?”

The unnamed man said, “Begone! You are a vulgar man—why do you ask such an inopportune question? I am about to become one with the Creator. When weary, I shall ride the nebulous bird beyond the six directions and wander in the land of Nothing Whatever, dwelling in the boundless wilds. Why do you disturb my heart with talk of governing the world?”

Tian Gen asked again.

The unnamed man replied, “Let your mind wander in simplicity, blend your qi with the vastness, follow things as they are without imposing your private will—and the world will be governed.”

Yang Ziju met Lao Dan and said, “Here is a man swift, strong, and resolute, with keen perception and clear understanding, who studies the Dao tirelessly. Could such a one be compared to an enlightened king?”

Lao Dan replied, “In the eyes of the sage, such a man is like a clerk or artisan, bound by skill and toil, exhausting his body and straining his mind. Moreover, the patterns of tigers and leopards attract hunters, while the agility of monkeys and the cunning of dogs trained to catch rats bring them to the leash. Could such a one be compared to an enlightened king?”

Yang Ziju, startled, asked, “May I inquire about the governance of an enlightened king?”

Lao Dan said, “The achievements of an enlightened king cover the world yet seem not his own; he transforms and bestows upon the ten thousand things, yet the people do not rely on him. No name is raised, and he allows things to rejoice in themselves. He stands in the unfathomable and wanders in the realm of Nothing.”

In Zheng there was a divine diviner named Ji Xian, who knew of men’s deaths and lives, survivals and perishings, fortunes and misfortunes, long lives and early deaths, predicting them by the year, month, ten-day week, or day, as if by spirit. When the people of Zheng saw him, they all fled in haste. Liezi saw him and was enchanted in heart. Returning, he told Master Hu, saying, “At first I took your Dao as the ultimate, but now there is something even more ultimate.”

Master Hu said, “I have shown you only its form, not yet its reality—do you truly possess the Dao? Though many hens may gather, without a rooster how can they lay fertile eggs? You take your seclusion and contend with the world, so you must be genuine, which is why others can read your fortune. Try bringing him here to show him me.”

The next day, Liezi brought Ji Xian to see Master Hu. When they came out, Ji Xian said to Liezi, “Alas! Your master is dead—he will not live. Not even ten days remain. I saw something strange in him, like damp ashes.”

Liezi entered and wept, his tears soaking his lapels, as he told Master Hu. Master Hu said, “I showed him the pattern of earth—still and unshaken, neither moving nor upright. He likely saw my blocked virtue-mechanism. Try bringing him again.”

The next day, Liezi brought Ji Xian to see Master Hu again. When they came out, Ji Xian said to Liezi, “Fortunate that your master met me! He is restored—fully alive. I saw his blocked potential.”

Liezi entered and told Master Hu. Master Hu said, “I showed him the expanse of heaven and earth—names and realities did not enter, yet the mechanism arose from his heels. He likely saw my mechanism of goodness. Try bringing him again.”

The next day, Liezi brought Ji Xian to see Master Hu again. When they came out, Ji Xian said to Liezi, “Your master is unsettled—I cannot read his fortune. If he settles, I will try again.”

Liezi entered and told Master Hu. Master Hu said, “I humbly showed him the Great Thoroughfare where nothing prevails. He likely saw my balanced qi-mechanism. The stillness of a deep ravine forms a profound abyss; the stillness of water forms a profound abyss; the flow of water forms a profound abyss. There are nine names for abyss—here he saw three. Try bringing him again.”

The next day, Liezi brought Ji Xian to see Master Hu again. Before he could stand steadily, Ji Xian lost himself and fled. Master Hu said, “Pursue him.”

Liezi chased him but could not catch up. Returning, he reported to Master Hu, “He has vanished—he is gone. I could not reach him.”

Master Hu said, “I showed him what had never yet departed from my origin. I met him with emptiness and compliance, not knowing who or what he was. He took me for a drifting gourd, for a flowing wave—hence his flight.”

After this, Liezi considered himself as one who had never yet begun to learn. He returned home and for three years did not go out. He cooked for his wife, fed pigs as if feeding people, and in affairs kept no special closeness. He carved away excess and returned to simplicity, standing alone in his form like an unworked clod. Entangled yet sealed, he ended thus. He became no vessel for fame, no repository for schemes, no bearer of tasks, no master of knowledge. His body embraced the boundless, and he wandered without trace. He exhausted what he received from heaven yet saw no gain—he was merely empty. The mind of the perfected man is like a mirror: it does not send forth, does not welcome, responds yet does not store, and thus can master things without harm.

The emperor of the Southern Sea was Shu, the emperor of the Northern Sea was Hu, and the emperor of the Central Sea was Hundun. Shu and Hu often met in Hundun’s land, and Hundun treated them very well. Shu and Hu plotted to repay Hundun’s kindness, saying, “All people have seven openings for seeing, hearing, eating, and breathing—only he has none. Let us try to bore them for him.” Each day they bored one opening, and on the seventh day, Hundun died.

*Chapter Eight of the Outer Chapters of the True Scripture of Southern Florescence: "Webbed Toes"*

Webbed toes and extra fingers arise from nature yet exceed in virtue; hanging tumors and dangling warts arise from form yet exceed in nature. Those who multiply methods of benevolence and righteousness and apply them are arrayed among the five organs, yet this is not the true standard of the Dao. Thus, webbing in the feet is but connected useless flesh; branching in the hands is but an extra useless finger. Those who multiply methods and branch out in the passions of the five organs indulge in perverse practices of benevolence and righteousness and multiply the uses of intelligence. Thus, those with webbing in their vision confuse the five colors, indulge in ornate patterns, and dazzle with blues, yellows, and fish-scale brilliance—is this not Li Zhu? Those with branching in their hearing confuse the five sounds, indulge in the six pitches, and resound with metal, stone, silk, bamboo, the Yellow Bell, and the Great Regulator—is this not Shi Kuang? Those who branch in benevolence pluck at virtue and block their nature to gather fame, making the world drum up support for unattainable laws—are these not Zeng and Shi? Those with webbing in their eloquence weave tangled knots of words, roaming their minds among the questions of hardness and whiteness, sameness and difference, and exhaust jade-like reputations with useless speech—are these not Yang and Mo? Thus, all these are paths of excess webbing and branching, not the ultimate standard of the world. That which is truly upright does not lose the truth of its nature and life. Therefore, what is joined is not called webbing, and what branches is not called divergence; what is long is not called excess, and what is short is not called deficiency. Thus, the duck’s legs are short, but lengthening them would cause sorrow; the crane’s legs are long, but cutting them would cause grief. Therefore, what is long by nature should not be cut, and what is short by nature should not be lengthened—there is no need to remove sorrow. Do benevolence and righteousness truly not accord with human nature? Why do those benevolent people have so many worries? Moreover, those with webbed toes feel pain when separated, and those with extra fingers cry when pinched. These two—whether in excess or deficiency in number—share the same sorrow. The benevolent people of today gaze with concern at the world’s calamities; the unbenevolent people harm their nature and life to glut themselves on wealth and honor.

Thus, do benevolence and righteousness truly not accord with human nature? Since the Three Dynasties and below, why has the world been so clamorous? Moreover, those who rely on hooks, cords, compasses, and squares to make things straight cut away at their nature; those who depend on ropes, glue, and lacquer to make things firm encroach upon their virtue. Bending and folding with rites and music, mouthing benevolence and righteousness to soothe the hearts of the world—this is to lose what is constant and natural. The world has its constant and natural state. In this state, the crooked need no hook, the straight need no cord, the round need no compass, the square need no square, adhesion requires no glue or lacquer, and binding needs no rope. Thus, the world is born in harmony, all living yet not knowing why they live, all attaining yet not knowing why they attain. From ancient times to the present, this has never changed—it cannot be diminished. Then why do benevolence and righteousness cling like glue and lacquer, entangle like ropes, and wander between the Dao and virtue? They only confuse the world.

Minor delusions distort direction; great delusions distort nature. How do we know this is so? Since the time of Yu’s dynasty, when benevolence and righteousness were promoted to disturb the world, all under heaven have rushed to answer the call of benevolence and righteousness—is this not using benevolence and righteousness to alter their nature? Let us examine this: since the Three Dynasties and below, has there been anyone in the world who has not exchanged their nature for external things?

The petty man sacrifices himself for profit; the scholar sacrifices himself for fame; the grandee sacrifices himself for his family; the sage sacrifices himself for the world. Though these several types differ in their undertakings and their reputations bear different names, in harming their nature and sacrificing themselves, they are one.

Zang and Gu were two men who herded sheep together, yet both lost their sheep. When asked what Zang was doing, he was holding books and reading; when asked what Gu was doing, he was gambling for sport. Though their occupations differed, in losing their sheep they were the same.

Boyi died for fame beneath Shouyang Mountain, while Robber Zhi died for profit atop Dongling. Though their deaths differed, in harming their lives and injuring their natures they were the same. Why must Boyi be right and Robber Zhi wrong? The world is entirely given to sacrifice: those who sacrifice for benevolence and righteousness are called gentlemen by custom; those who sacrifice for wealth and property are called petty men. In their sacrifice, they are one—some are gentlemen, some are petty men. But if we consider the harm to their lives and the injury to their natures, then Robber Zhi is no different from Boyi. How then can we distinguish between gentleman and petty man?

Moreover, those who bind their nature to benevolence and righteousness, though as learned as Zeng and Shi, are not what I call good; those who bind their nature to the five flavors, though as skilled as Yu’er, are not what I call good; those who bind their nature to the five sounds, though as perceptive as Master Kuang, are not what I call keen; those who bind their nature to the five colors, though as discerning as Li Zhu, are not what I call clear-sighted.

What I call good is not the so-called benevolence and righteousness, but preserving one’s virtue alone. What I call good is not the so-called benevolence and righteousness, but preserving one’s virtue alone. What I call good is not the so-called benevolence and righteousness, but following the true nature of one’s life. What I call keen is not hearing others, but hearing oneself; what I call clear-sighted is not seeing others, but seeing oneself.

Those who do not see themselves but see others, who do not attain for themselves but attain for others—these attain what others attain yet do not attain what they themselves attain, they follow what others follow yet do not follow what they themselves follow. Whether Robber Zhi or Boyi, those who follow what others follow and do not follow what they themselves follow are equally excessive and perverse.

I am ashamed before the Dao and virtue, and so above I dare not practice the conduct of benevolence and righteousness, and below I dare not engage in excessive and perverse actions.

*Southern Florescence Perfected Scripture, Scroll Two, Outer Chapters: "Horse’s Hooves" (Chapter Nine)*

A horse’s hooves can tread upon frost and snow; its hair can resist wind and cold. It grazes on grass and drinks water, lifts its feet and gallops—this is the true nature of the horse. Though there be grand halls and towering chambers, they are of no use to it. But when Bo Le arrived and said, “I am skilled in managing horses...”

To burn and brand them, to pare and carve them, to bind them with bridles and reins, to confine them with early-morning stalls—of horses thus treated, twelve or thirteen in every twenty die. To starve them, to parch them, to drive them at a gallop, to spur them to a run, to marshal them in ranks, to align them in formation, with the peril of elaborate trappings before them and the threat of whips and lashes behind—of horses thus treated, more than half die.

The potter says, “I am skilled in shaping clay: round vessels meet the compass, square ones meet the carpenter’s square.” The craftsman says, “I am skilled in shaping wood: curved pieces meet the hook, straight ones meet the plumb line.” But do clay and wood by nature desire to meet compass, square, hook, and plumb line? Yet generation after generation praises them, saying, “Bo Le is skilled in managing horses, and potters and carpenters are skilled in shaping clay and wood.” This is also the error of those who govern the world.

In my view, those who are truly skilled in governing the world do not act thus. The people have a constant nature: they weave to clothe themselves, they plow to feed themselves—this is called shared virtue. Unified yet not partisan, this is called “Heaven’s release.” Thus in the age of utmost virtue, their movements were unhurried, their gazes were steady. At that time, mountains had no paths or tunnels, marshes had no boats or bridges. The myriad things thrived in flocks, linked to their native lands. Birds and beasts gathered in herds, grasses and trees grew to fullness. Thus one could lead birds and beasts by the halter and wander with them, or climb to the nests of crows and magpies and peer inside. In the age of utmost virtue, people dwelled together with birds and beasts, lived in kinship with the myriad things. How would they know of gentlemen and petty men? Alike in their lack of knowledge, their virtue did not depart; alike in their lack of desire, they were called “plain and unhewn.” In plainness and unhewn simplicity, the people’s nature was preserved.

But when the sages came, they trod and trampled to create benevolence, they lifted and prodded to create righteousness—and the world began to doubt. They spread and scattered to create music, they picked and selected to create ritual—and the world began to divide. Thus if the unhewn block is not carved, who would make sacrificial goblets? If white jade is not cut, who would make ritual scepters? If the Dao and virtue are not abandoned, why would one need benevolence and righteousness? If nature and feeling are not estranged, why would one need ritual and music? If the five colors are not confused, who would create patterned embroidery? If the five notes are not disordered, who would tune them to the six pitch-pipes? To carve the unhewn into vessels is the fault of the craftsman; to destroy the Dao and virtue to create benevolence and righteousness is the error of the sage.

The horse, dwelling on land, eats grass and drinks water; when pleased, it nuzzles necks with its companions; when angry, it turns its back and kicks. This is all the horse knows. But when one adds a crossbar and yoke, aligns it with blinkers, the horse learns to feign submission, to balk at the bit, to startle and bolt, to cheat the bridle, to steal the reins. Thus the horse’s knowledge, able to reach the point of theft, is the fault of Bo Le.

In the time of the Lord of Brightness, the people dwelled without knowing what to do, wandered without knowing where to go. They carried food in their cheeks and played, patted their bellies and roamed—this was all the people could do. But when the sages came, they bent and twisted ritual and music to correct the world’s form, they hung up benevolence and righteousness to console the world’s heart—and the people then began to tread the byways of cunning and love of knowledge, to contend in pursuit of profit, unable to stop. This, too, is the error of the sages.

*Southern Florescence Perfected Scripture, Scroll Two, Outer Chapters: "The Collar-Basket" (Chapter Ten)*

If one prepares locks, cords, and sturdy bolts to guard against thieves who would pry open baskets, rummage through bags, or break into chests, this is what the world calls wisdom. Yet when the great thief comes, he shoulders the chest, lifts the basket, carries the bag, and flees—fearing only that the cords, locks, and bolts are not secure enough. Thus what was formerly called wisdom is nothing but amassing goods for the great thief.

Let me try to discuss this: Is there anything in what the world calls wisdom that does not amass for the great thief? Is there anything in what it calls sagacity that does not guard for the great thief? How do I know this is so? In ancient times, the state of Qi had villages so close they could see one another, and the sounds of their chickens and dogs could be heard across the land. The area where nets and traps were spread, where plows and hoes were used, extended over three thousand *li* square. Within its four borders, in all its ancestral temples, altars of soil and grain, cities, towns, districts, and villages—was there any place where they did not model themselves on the sages? Yet one morning, Tian Chengzi killed the ruler of Qi and stole his state. What he stole was not merely the state—he also stole the sagely laws along with it. Thus though Tian Chengzi bore the name of a thief and bandit, he lived in the security of Yao and Shun. Small states dared not criticize him, great states dared not punish him, and for twelve generations he held the state of Qi. Was this not stealing the state of Qi along with its sagely laws to guard his thieving body?

Let me try to discuss this: Is there anything in what the world calls supreme wisdom that does not amass for the great thief? Is there anything in what it calls supreme sagacity that does not guard for the great thief? How do I know this is so? In ancient times, Guan Longpeng was beheaded, Bi Gan was disemboweled, Chang Hong was torn apart, and Wu Zixu was torn to shreds. Thus though these four were worthy men, they could not escape execution.

A disciple asked Robber Zhi, “Do thieves also have a Dao?” Robber Zhi said, “Where can one go without a Dao? To guess the treasures hidden in a room is sagacity; to enter first is courage; to leave last is righteousness; to know whether it can be done is knowledge; to divide the spoils equally is benevolence. Without these five, no one in the world has ever become a great thief.”

From this we see that the good man cannot stand without the Dao of the sage, and Robber Zhi cannot act without the Dao of the sage. Since there are few good men in the world and many who are not good, the sage brings little benefit to the world and much harm. Thus it is said: When the lips are gone, the teeth grow cold; when the wine of Lu was weak, the city of Handan was besieged; when sages arise, great thieves appear. Strike down the sages, release the thieves and bandits—and the world will begin to be ordered.

When rivers run dry, valleys empty; when hills are leveled, deep pools fill. When the sages die, great thieves will not arise, and the world will be at peace, without trouble.

If the sage does not die, great thieves will not cease. Though one may exalt the sage to govern the world, this is but to heap profit upon Robber Zhi. When measures and pecks are made to gauge, he steals both measure and peck; when scales and balances are made to weigh, he steals both scale and balance; when tallies and seals are made to authenticate, he steals both tally and seal; when benevolence and righteousness are made to rectify, he steals both benevolence and righteousness. How do we know this is so? He who steals a hook is executed, but he who steals a state becomes a feudal lord—benevolence and righteousness abide at the gates of such lords. Is this not the theft of benevolence, righteousness, sagacity, and wisdom? Thus, those who pursue great thieves, usurp the ranks of feudal lords, and seize the profits of benevolence, righteousness, measures, scales, tallies, and seals—even with the rewards of high office, none can be encouraged; even with the threat of the executioner’s axe, none can be restrained. This heaping of profit for Robber Zhi, making him unstoppable, is the fault of the sage.

Thus it is said: The fish must not leave the deep; the state’s sharpest tools must not be shown to others. The sage is the world’s sharpest tool—he is not what should be displayed to the world. Therefore, cut off sagacity and cast aside wisdom, and great thieves will cease; smash jade and shatter pearls, and petty thieves will not arise; burn tallies and break seals, and the people will return to simplicity; discard measures and snap balances, and the people will know no contention. Only after destroying the world’s sagely laws can the people be fit for discussion. Throw the six pitch-pipes into chaos, melt down flutes and zithers, deafen the ears of Master Kuang, and the world will begin to set aside its hearing; erase patterns, scatter the five colors, blind the eyes of Li Zhu, and the world will begin to set aside its sight; break hooks and cords, discard compass and square, cripple the fingers of the artisan Chui, and the world will begin to possess its skill. Thus it is said: Great skill appears clumsy.

Erase the conduct of Zeng and Shi, silence the mouths of Yang and Mo, cast aside benevolence and righteousness, and the world’s virtue will begin to attain profound unity. When people contain their brightness, the world will not be dazzled; when people set aside their hearing, the world will not be burdened; when people contain their knowledge, the world will not be deluded; when people contain their virtue, the world will not be perverse. Those like Zeng, Shi, Yang, Mo, Master Kuang, the artisan Chui, and Li Zhu—all externally establish their virtue and thereby confuse the world—are of no use to the law.

Do you alone not know of the age of perfect virtue? In ancient times, under the reigns of Rongcheng, Dating, Bohuang, Zhongyang, Lilu, Lixu, Xuanyuan, Hexu, Zunlu, Zhurong, Fuxi, and Shennong, the people knotted cords to keep records. They savored their food, admired their clothing, delighted in their customs, and rested content in their dwellings. Neighboring states were within sight of one another, the sounds of chickens and dogs carried between them, yet the people grew old and died without ever traveling beyond their lands. In such an age, perfect governance was already achieved.

But now, it has come to this: the people crane their necks and stand on tiptoe, saying, “In such-and-such a place there is a worthy one,” and they carry provisions to flock there—abandoning their parents at home and forsaking their lords’ service abroad.

Their footprints tread upon the borders of the lords’ domains, and the ruts of their chariots entangle a thousand *li* beyond—all because those above are excessively fond of knowledge. If those above truly cherish knowledge while lacking the Dao, the world will fall into great chaos.

How do we know this is so? When the cunning of bows, crossbows, snares, and traps multiplies, birds are thrown into turmoil above. When the schemes of hooks, baits, nets, and weirs proliferate, fish are thrown into turmoil in the waters. When the devices of fences, nets, and pitfall traps grow numerous, beasts are thrown into turmoil in the marshes. When the deceptions of crafty deceit, poisonous sophistry, and the disputes over "hard and white" or "same and different" abound, the common folk are bewildered by rhetoric.

Thus, the world repeatedly falls into great chaos, and the fault lies in the love of knowledge. For the world all knows to seek what it does not know, yet none knows to seek what it already knows; all knows to condemn what it deems unworthy, yet none knows to condemn what it already deems worthy. This is why great chaos arises.

Above, it eclipses the brightness of the sun and moon; below, it scorches the essence of mountains and rivers; in between, it disrupts the unfolding of the four seasons. Even the creatures that crawl and flutter—none retain their true nature. How severe is the disorder wrought by the love of knowledge upon the world! This has been the case since the Three Dynasties and beyond: abandoning the simple and unadorned people, they delight in the toiling flattery of the obsequious. Forsaking tranquil non-action, they take pleasure in the restless clamor of cleverness—this restless clamor has thrown the world into chaos.

*The Southern Florescence Scripture*, Scroll Two, Outer Chapters: "Maintaining and Accommodating" (Chapter Eleven)

We hear of maintaining and accommodating the world, but never of governing the world. What does "maintaining" mean? It is to fear that the world will corrupt its nature. What does "accommodating" mean? It is to fear that the world will alter its virtue. If the world neither corrupts its nature nor alters its virtue, is there any need to govern it?

In ancient times, when Yao governed the world, he made the people joyful and content in their nature—this was not tranquility. When Jie governed the world, he made the people weary and distressed in their nature—this was not happiness. Neither the absence of tranquility nor the absence of happiness is virtue. And that which is not virtue cannot endure for long.

When people are excessively joyful, they harm the yang; when excessively angry, they harm the yin. When yin and yang are both harmed, the four seasons fail to arrive, the harmony of cold and heat is lost, and in turn, this injures the human form. It causes people’s joy and anger to lose their proper place, their dwellings to lack constancy, their thoughts to find no fulfillment, and their endeavors to remain incomplete. From this, the world first becomes restless and unruly, and only then do the conducts of thieves like Zhi and sages like Zeng and Shi arise.

Thus, if one were to reward all the good in the world, it would not suffice; if one were to punish all the evil, it would not be enough. Therefore, the vastness of the world is insufficient for rewards and punishments. Since the Three Dynasties and beyond, they have clamored endlessly over rewards and punishments—when would they have had leisure to preserve the true essence of their natures and lives?

Moreover, to delight in cleverness is to be seduced by appearances; to delight in sharpness is to be seduced by sounds; to delight in benevolence is to disorder virtue; to delight in righteousness is to pervert principle; to delight in ritual is to indulge in artifice; to delight in music is to indulge in excess; to delight in sagacity is to indulge in skill; to delight in knowledge is to indulge in fault-finding. If the world seeks to preserve the true essence of its nature and life, these eight may exist or vanish—it matters not. But if the world does not preserve the true essence of its nature and life, these eight become twisted, entangled, and knotted, throwing the world into chaos. And yet, the world begins to revere and cherish them—how profound is its delusion!

Is it merely a matter of recognizing their excess and discarding them? No, they fast and purify themselves to speak of them, kneel and sit to present them, drum and sing to dance for them—what is to be done about this?

Thus, when the noble person is compelled to oversee the world, nothing is better than non-action. Only through non-action can the true essence of nature and life be preserved. Therefore, one who values their own person over governing the world may be entrusted with the world; one who cherishes their own person over governing the world may be charged with the world.

If the noble person can refrain from disturbing their five viscera and from flaunting their intelligence; if they dwell like an ancestral tablet yet manifest like a dragon, remain as still as an abyss yet resound like thunder, move with the spirit and the heavens follow, and act with ease and non-action so that the myriad things rise like steam—then what leisure would they have to govern the world?

Cui Qu asked Lao Cong, "If one does not govern the world, how can the hearts of the people be settled?"
Lao Cong replied, "Be careful not to agitate the human heart. The human heart presses downward yet strives upward, trapped between above and below, now yielding, now unyielding, now soft, now hard. It is sharp and carving, its heat scorching like fire, its cold congealing like ice. Its swiftness spans the four seas in the blink of an eye; when at rest, it is deep and still; when in motion, it soars to the heavens. Fierce and proud, it cannot be bound—is there anything like the human heart?

In ancient times, the Yellow Emperor first used benevolence and righteousness to agitate the human heart. Yao and Shun then wore their thighs bare and their shins hairless in toiling to nourish the forms of the world. They exhausted their five viscera to establish benevolence and righteousness, strained their blood and qi to model laws and standards. Yet even so, they could not prevail. Thus, Yao banished Huan Dou to Mount Chong, exiled the Three Miao to Sanwei, and sent Gong Gong to Youdu—this was their failure to overcome the world.

By the time this reached the Three Kings, the world was thrown into great alarm. Below, there were tyrants like Jie and Robber Zhi; above, there were sages like Zeng and Shi; and the Confucians and Mohists all arose. From this, joy and anger suspected one another, the foolish and the wise deceived one another, the good and the bad condemned one another, the truthful and the deceitful mocked one another—and the world declined. Virtue diverged, and the nature and lives of the people scattered like wildflowers.

The world loved knowledge, and the common people were driven to exhaustion. Thus, saws and axes were fashioned, inked cords and knives were wielded, chisels and mallets were employed. The world was thrown into great disorder—all because of the agitation of the human heart."

Thus, the worthy withdrew to dwell beneath the great mountains and towering cliffs, while the lords of ten thousand chariots trembled with anxiety in their ancestral halls. In this age, those condemned to death lie heaped upon one another; those in stocks press shoulder to shoulder; and the executed stretch as far as the eye can see. Yet the Confucians and Mohists still raise their arms in contention amidst the fetters and cangues—how extreme is their shamelessness! How utterly devoid of remorse! I know not whether the wisdom of sages does not in truth become the stocks and pillories that break men’s limbs, or whether benevolence and righteousness do not become the fetters and cangues that bore through flesh. How can we know that Zeng and Shi were not the arrow-tips of Jie and Robber Zhi?

Therefore it is said: "Cut off sageliness, cast aside wisdom, and the world will be greatly ordered."

After Yellow Emperor had reigned as Son of Heaven for nineteen years, his decrees reached throughout the land. Hearing that Guangchengzi dwelt upon Mount Kongtong, he went to see him and said: "I have heard that you have attained the perfect Dao. I venture to ask: what is the essence of the perfect Dao? I wish to draw forth the essence of Heaven and Earth to aid the five grains and nourish the people. I also wish to regulate the Yin and Yang to bring all living things to fulfillment. How may this be done?"

Guangchengzi replied: "What you wish to inquire about is the substance of things; what you wish to regulate is the ruin of things. Since you have governed the world, the clouds have rained without gathering, the grasses and trees have withered without yellowing, and the light of the sun and moon has grown ever more dim. Your heart, so full of cunning and flattery, is unfit to speak of the perfect Dao."

The Yellow Emperor withdrew, relinquished the world, built a solitary chamber, spread a mat of white cogon grass, and lived in seclusion. Then he returned to seek Guangchengzi again. Guangchengzi lay with his head to the south, and the Yellow Emperor approached from the north, crawling forward on his knees. Bowing twice, he touched his forehead to the ground and asked: "I have heard that you have attained the perfect Dao. I venture to ask: how may one cultivate the body to attain long life?"

Guangchengzi rose with a start and said: "An excellent question! Come, and I will tell you of the perfect Dao. The essence of the perfect Dao is profound and obscure; its utmost reaches are dark and silent. See nothing, hear nothing, hold fast to the spirit in stillness, and the body will right itself. Be still, be pure; do not weary your body, do not agitate your essence, and you may attain long life. Let your eyes see nothing, your ears hear nothing, your heart know nothing, and your spirit will guard your body, and your body will attain long life. Guard your inner self, restrain your outer self, and excess knowledge will bring ruin. I have guided you to the source of great brightness, to the origin of utmost Yang. I have led you through the gate of profound obscurity, to the origin of utmost Yin. Heaven and Earth have their offices, Yin and Yang have their storehouses. Guard your body well, and things will flourish of themselves. I preserve the One and abide in harmony; thus I have cultivated my body for one thousand two hundred years, and my form has never decayed."

The Yellow Emperor bowed twice, touching his forehead to the ground, and said: "Guangchengzi is like Heaven itself!"

Guangchengzi said: "Come, and I will tell you more. Those things are inexhaustible, yet men all take them to be finite; those things are unfathomable, yet men all take them to be limited. Those who attain my Dao may become emperors above and kings below; those who lose my Dao may see light above yet become earth below. Now all the myriad creatures are born from the earth and return to the earth. Therefore I will leave you and enter the gate of the inexhaustible, to wander in the boundless wilds. I share the light of the sun and moon, I endure with Heaven and Earth. Let those who come near me be enlightened; let those who depart from me be benighted. Let all men perish, yet I alone endure."

Cloud General was roaming eastward when he passed the branches of the whirlwind tree and happened upon Hong Meng. Hong Meng was just then slapping his thighs and leaping about in play. When Cloud General saw him, he stopped in astonishment and stood in awe, saying: "Old sir, who are you? What are you doing here?"

Hong Meng continued slapping his thighs and leaping without pause. He replied to Cloud General: "Roaming."

Cloud General said: "I wish to ask you something."

Hong Meng looked up at Cloud General and said: "Ah!"

Cloud General said: "The qi of Heaven is not harmonious, the qi of Earth is pent up, the six qi are not balanced, the four seasons are out of order. Now I wish to blend the essence of the six qi to nurture all living things. How may this be done?"

Hong Meng continued slapping his thighs and leaping, shaking his head and saying: "I do not know! I do not know!" Cloud General could not press his question.

Three years later, roaming again, Cloud General passed through the wilds of Song and once more encountered Hong Meng. Overjoyed, Cloud General hastened forward and said: "Has Heaven forgotten me? Has Heaven forgotten me?" He bowed twice, touching his head to the ground, and entreated Hong Meng for instruction.

Hong Meng replied: "Roaming freely, knowing not what I seek; wandering wildly, knowing not where I go. The roamer is carefree, observing the uncontrived. What do I know?"

Cloud General said: "I too believed myself to be wandering wildly, yet the people follow wherever I go. I cannot be free of the people—now they have become my shackles. I wish to hear but a single word."

Hong Meng said: "To disrupt the warp of Heaven, to violate the nature of things, even the mysterious workings of Heaven cannot bring them to completion. To scatter the herds of beasts, so that birds all cry at night; to bring calamity upon grasses and trees, disaster upon insects—alas, these are the faults of those who govern men."

Cloud General said: "Then what am I to do?"

Hong Meng said: "Alas, how poisonous! Return in bewildered exhaustion."

Cloud General said: "I have met with Heaven’s difficulty. I wish to hear but a single word."

Hong Meng said: "Alas, nurture the heart. Abide in non-action, and things will transform of themselves. Cast off your form, expel your cleverness, forget both self and things, and merge with the great sameness of the marshlands. Release the heart, liberate the spirit, and become as though soulless. The ten thousand things, teeming and indistinct, each return to their root, each return to their root without knowing it. Vast and undifferentiated, they remain thus all their lives. If one claims to know it, one has already departed from it. Do not inquire into their names, do not scrutinize their natures—things arise of themselves."

Cloud General said: "Heaven has bestowed virtue upon me, shown me the way of silence. Through personal seeking, I have now attained it." He bowed twice, touching his head to the ground, then rose and took his leave.

The common people all delight in those who are like themselves and despise those who differ. Those who desire likeness and reject difference do so with the intent of standing out from the crowd. Yet how can one who seeks to stand out from the crowd ever truly do so? Relying on the multitude for tranquility, what one hears will not surpass the skills of the many. Yet those who would govern the realm of men grasp at the benefits of the Three Kings while blind to their perils. This is to gamble with the realm of men—how often can one gamble and not lose it? The chance of preserving the realm is less than one in ten thousand, while the risk of losing it is such that a single failure brings ten thousandfold ruin. Alas, how ignorant are those who hold land!

Those who hold land possess a great thing. Possessing a great thing, one must not be constrained by it. Only by not being constrained by things can one master things. He who understands that the master of things is not a thing himself—how could his governance be limited to the realm and its people? Moving in and out of the six directions, roaming the nine provinces, coming and going alone—this is called possessing singularity. One who possesses singularity is called most noble.

The teaching of the great person is like the shadow following form, the echo responding to sound. When questioned, they answer, exhausting all that is in their heart, becoming the counterpart of all under Heaven. They dwell in the realm of no echo, move in the realm of no direction. They grasp you and lead you through the bustling throng, roaming without end. Entering and exiting without hindrance, they share no beginning with the sun. They praise and discuss the forms of the body, merging with the great sameness, the great sameness without self. Without self, how can there be possession? Those who perceive possession are the gentlemen of old; those who perceive non-possession are friends of Heaven and Earth.

That which is lowly yet cannot be shunned is things; that which is humble yet cannot be disregarded is the people; that which is hidden yet cannot be left undone is affairs; that which is coarse yet cannot be set forth is law; that which is distant yet cannot be neglected is righteousness; that which is intimate yet cannot be narrow is benevolence; that which is measured yet cannot be unaccumulated is ritual; that which is centered yet cannot be unexalted is virtue; that which is one yet cannot be unchanging is the Dao; that which is spirit yet cannot be left undone is Heaven.

Thus, the sage observes Heaven yet does not assist, completes virtue yet is not burdened, emerges from the Dao yet does not scheme, meets benevolence yet does not rely on it, approaches righteousness yet does not accumulate it, responds to ritual yet does not conceal, engages with affairs yet does not decline, aligns with law yet is not disordered, depends on the people yet does not belittle them, follows things yet does not abandon them. Things are not worth acting upon, yet they cannot be left undone. One who does not understand Heaven is not pure in virtue; one who does not comprehend the Dao has no means to proceed. Alas, those who do not understand the Dao!

What is the Dao? There is the Dao of Heaven and the Dao of Man. That which is revered through non-action is the Dao of Heaven; that which is burdened through action is the Dao of Man. The ruler follows the Dao of Heaven; the minister follows the Dao of Man. The Dao of Heaven and the Dao of Man are vastly different—this must not go unexamined.

* * *

Though Heaven and Earth are vast, their transformations are uniform; though the ten thousand things are many, their governance is one; though the masses of people are numerous, their lord is the ruler. The ruler originates in virtue and is completed by Heaven. Thus it is said: The ancient rulers of the world under Heaven practiced non-action, embodying only the virtue of Heaven.

Viewing words through the Dao, the rulers of the world are rectified; viewing distinctions through the Dao, the duties of ruler and minister are clarified; viewing abilities through the Dao, the offices of the world are well-ordered; viewing broadly through the Dao, the responses of the ten thousand things are complete. Thus, that which penetrates Heaven and Earth is virtue; that which moves through the ten thousand things is the Dao; that which governs people is affairs; that which involves skill is technique.

The arts are encompassed by affairs, affairs by righteousness, righteousness by virtue, virtue by the Dao, and the Dao by Heaven. Thus it is said: The ancients who nurtured the world under Heaven had no desires, and the world was sufficient; they practiced non-action, and the ten thousand things were transformed; they were profound and still, and the hundred clans were settled.

The *Record* states: "Penetrate the One, and the ten thousand affairs are complete; attain effortless mastery, and even ghosts and spirits submit." The Master said: "The Dao—it covers and sustains the ten thousand things, vast and boundless! How great it is! The noble person cannot but examine his heart in this regard."

Non-action in doing is called Heaven; non-action in speaking is called virtue; loving people and benefiting things is called benevolence; uniting differences in sameness is called greatness; acting without eccentricity is called magnanimity; possessing the ten thousand differences is called wealth. Thus, holding fast to virtue is called discipline; the completion of virtue is called establishment; following the Dao is called perfection; not allowing things to thwart one’s will is called integrity.

When the noble person is enlightened regarding these ten, his mind’s capacity for affairs is profound, and his engagement with the ten thousand things is effortless. Such a one hides gold in the mountains and pearls in the depths, neither valuing wealth nor seeking nobility and riches. He does not rejoice in long life nor grieve over early death; he is not honored by success nor ashamed by poverty. He does not claim the world’s benefits as his private share, nor does he regard ruling the world as a position of glory. When one is illustrious, one is luminous—all things are as a single storehouse, and death and life share the same form.

The Master said: "The Dao—profound is its abode, limpid is its clarity. Even metal and stone cannot sound without it. Thus, metal and stone have voices, yet without being struck, they do not ring. Who can determine the ten thousand things?"

The person of royal virtue moves plainly and feels shame at being entangled in affairs; he establishes the root and origin and knows how to commune with the spirit. Thus, his virtue is vast, and his heart’s emanations are gathered by things. Therefore, form does not arise without the Dao, nor is life illuminated without virtue. Preserving form and exhausting life, establishing virtue and illuminating the Dao—is this not the person of royal virtue?

Vast and unbounded, suddenly emerging, dynamically moving—when the ten thousand things follow him, this is called the person of royal virtue. He sees in darkness and hears in silence. Within darkness, he alone perceives the dawn; within silence, he alone hears harmony. Thus, the deeper one delves, the more one can comprehend things; the more spiritually attuned one becomes, the more one can grasp the essence.

Therefore, in his interaction with the ten thousand things, he is utterly non-substantial yet meets their needs; he roams freely yet binds them to their origins. Great and small, long and short, distant and near.

The Yellow Emperor roamed north of the Red Water and ascended the heights of Mount Kunlun to gaze southward. Upon returning, he lost his dark pearl. He sent Knowledge to seek it, but it was not found; he sent Li Zhu to seek it, but it was not found; he sent Chi Gou to seek it, but it was not found. Only Xiang Wang obtained it. The Yellow Emperor said: "How strange! Only Xiang Wang could retrieve it."

Yao’s teacher was Xu You; Xu You’s teacher was Nie Que; Nie Que’s teacher was Wang Ni; Wang Ni’s teacher was Pi Yi.

Yao asked Xu You: "Could Nie Que be matched with Heaven? I would rely on Wang Ni to assess him." Xu You said: "How perilous it would be to burden the world with him! Nie Que’s nature is keenly intelligent, quick-witted, and surpasses others, yet he still takes humanity as his measure of Heaven. He is strict in preventing transgressions but does not understand their source. Could he be matched with Heaven? He would ride upon people and lose Heaven. He would prioritize the self and differentiate forms, exalt knowledge and rush like fire, be driven by threads of affairs, be ensnared by things, look in all directions for responses, adapt to every contingency, and transform with things without constancy. How could he be matched with Heaven? Nevertheless, he has a lineage and ancestors; he could be a father to the masses but not the father of fathers. Governance under him would lead to disorder, inviting calamity from those who face north and treachery from those who face south."

Yao observed Hua. Hua addressed Yao, saying: "Ah! A sage! Let me pray for blessings upon the sage: may the sage live long." Yao declined. "May the sage be rich." Yao declined. "May the sage have many sons." Yao declined. Hua said: "Long life, wealth, and many sons—these are what people desire. Why do you alone not desire them?" Yao said: "Many sons bring many fears; wealth brings many affairs; long life brings many humiliations. These three are not means to cultivate virtue, so I decline."

Hua said: "At first, I took you for a sage, but now you are merely a gentleman. Heaven generates the ten thousand people and must assign them duties. If one has many sons and assigns them duties, what fear is there? If one is rich and distributes it among others, what affairs remain? The sage dwells like a quail and eats like a fledgling, moves like a bird without ostentation. When the world is ordered, he flourishes with all things; when the world is disordered, he cultivates virtue and retreats into seclusion. After a thousand years, weary of the world, he departs and ascends in weariness, riding the white clouds to the realm of the Emperor. The three calamities do not reach him, and his person remains free from misfortune. What humiliation is there?"

Hua departed. Yao followed him, saying: "May I ask a question?" Hua said: "Withdraw."

When Yao governed the world, Bo Cheng Zi Gao was enfeoffed as a lord. When Yao ceded the throne to Shun, and Shun to Yu, Bo Cheng Zi Gao resigned his lordship to farm. Yu went to see him and found him plowing in the fields. Yu hastened to the lower position, stood, and asked: "In the past, when Yao governed the world, you were enfeoffed as a lord. When Yao ceded the throne to Shun, and Shun to me, you resigned your lordship to farm. May I ask the reason?"

Bo Cheng Zi Gao said: "In the past, when Yao governed the world, he did not reward yet the people were encouraged; he did not punish yet the people were in awe. Now you reward and punish, yet the people lack benevolence. Virtue has declined from this point, and punishments have been established from this point—disorder for later generations begins here."

When you, sir, close your path and act perversely, do not implicate my affairs. He continued plowing, damp with sweat, without looking back.

In the Great Beginning, there was Nonbeing; there was no being, no name. From the One arose the One, yet it had no form. When things received what enabled them to live, this was called *virtue*; when the formless had divisions, yet remained thus without inquiry, this was called *ming*; when movement and stillness gave rise to things, and things took form and principle, this was called *shape*; when shape and body preserved spirit, each with its own pattern, this was called *nature*. Cultivating nature returns to virtue; when virtue reaches its fullness, it merges with the beginning. This merging is emptiness; emptiness is vastness. The beak joins in harmony; when the beak harmonizes, it unites with Heaven and Earth. This union is profound and subtle, seeming foolish and dull—this is called *mysterious virtue*, merging with the Great Compliance.

The Master turned to Lao Cong and said: "There is one who governs the Dao as if following another—what is permissible, what is not; what is so, what is not. A debater once said, 'Separating hardness and whiteness is like hanging them apart.' If this is so, can one be called a sage?"

Lao Cong replied: "This is the toil of clerks bound by skills, wearing out body and mind. A dog trained to catch raccoons grows anxious; a monkey brought from the mountains is skilled in climbing. Qiu, I tell you what you cannot hear and speak what you cannot say. Of those with heads and feet but no heart or ears, there are many; of those with form and those without form or shape who all exist, there are none. Their movement and stillness, their death and life, their decline and rise—these are not their true essence. When governance lies with man, forgetting things and forgetting Heaven, this is called forgetting oneself. One who forgets himself is said to enter into Heaven."

Jiang Wenqiao saw Ji Che and said: "The Lord of Lu told me, 'I beg to receive your instruction.' I could not decline his command. Having already told him, I do not know if it was fitting. Allow me to present it again. I said to the Lord of Lu, 'You must practice reverence and frugality, promote those who are publicly loyal and eliminate private favoritism—then who among the people would dare not be harmonious?'"

Ji Che laughed heartily and said: "If this is what you said, sir, then your words on the virtue of emperors and kings are like a mantis raising its arms to block a chariot wheel—certainly unequal to the task. Moreover, if this is so, then one who acts thus places himself in peril. His observation tower is high, and many things will come—those who throw themselves over the edge will be many."

Jiang Wenqiao was startled and said in alarm: "I am utterly lost in your words, sir. Nevertheless, I wish to hear the principles of your teaching."

Ji Che said: "When the Great Sage governs the world, he unsettles the people’s hearts, transforming their teachings and changing their customs. He eradicates their predatory hearts and advances their solitary aspirations, as if by nature’s own doing, and the people do not know why it is so. How could this be like the way Yao and Shun taught the people, laboring like a brother to guide them? He desires that all merge in virtue, and their hearts dwell in stillness."

Zigong traveled south to Chu and, on his return to Jin, passed through Hanyin. There he saw an old man working in his garden, digging a tunnel to enter a well, then hoisting a jar to draw water and irrigate his plots. He toiled laboriously, yet his results were meager.

Zigong said, "There is a device here that can irrigate a hundred plots in a day, requiring little effort yet yielding great results. Would you not wish to use it?"

The gardener looked up and asked, "How does it work?"

Zigong replied, "Carve wood into a machine—heavy at the rear, light at the front—drawing water as if by suction. It gushes forth like boiling water, and its name is the well-sweep."

The gardener flushed with anger, laughed, and said, "I have heard from my teacher that those who possess mechanical devices must engage in mechanical affairs, and those who engage in mechanical affairs must harbor mechanical minds. When a mechanical mind resides in the breast, purity and whiteness are incomplete. When purity and whiteness are incomplete, the spirit’s life is unsettled. One whose spirit’s life is unsettled is not borne by the Dao. I am not ignorant of such things—I am ashamed to do them."

Zigong, abashed, lowered his head and made no reply. After a moment, the gardener asked, "What are you doing here?"

Zigong said, "I am a disciple of Confucius."

The gardener replied, "Are you not one of those who broadly study to emulate the sages, striving to surpass the multitude, playing the zither and singing mournful songs to sell your reputation to the world? You are about to forget your spirit and qi, cast aside your bodily form, yet you barely manage yourself—how could you have leisure to govern the world? Be on your way and do not hinder my work."

Zigong, humbled and discomfited, lost his color and walked away crestfallen. After thirty li, he gradually recovered. His disciples asked, "Who was that man earlier? Why did you change countenance and lose composure, remaining unsettled for the rest of the day?"

Zigong said, "At first, I thought there was only one true person in the world, but I did not know there was another like him. I have heard from the Master that in affairs, one seeks what is possible; in achievements, one seeks completion. To expend little effort and gain much is the Way of the sage. Yet this is not so. One who holds to the Dao has complete virtue; one with complete virtue has complete form; one with complete form has complete spirit. One with complete spirit—this is the Way of the sage. Entrusted with life, he moves among the people yet does not know where he is going, so profound and whole is he. One must forget the mind that seeks merit, profit, and cleverness. As for such a person, if it is not his aspiration, he does not go; if it is not his heart, he does not act. Though the world praises him, if it aligns with his principles, he remains unmoved; though the world condemns him, if it contradicts his principles, he remains unshaken. The world’s praise or blame neither adds nor detracts—this is what is called a person of complete virtue. I am but a man tossed by the winds and waves."

Returning to Lu, he told Confucius what had happened. Confucius said, "He is one who temporarily practices the arts of Hundun’s school. He knows one aspect but not the other; he cultivates his inner self but neglects his outer conduct. One who is clear and enters simplicity, who acts without contrivance and returns to the uncarved block, who embodies nature and embraces the spirit, moving among the customs of the world—would you be so utterly astonished? Moreover, the arts of Hundun’s school—how could you or I truly understand them?"

Zhun Mang was traveling east to the Great Abyss when he encountered Yuan Feng on the shores of the Eastern Sea. Yuan Feng asked, "Where are you going?"

Zhun Mang replied, "To the Great Abyss."

Yuan Feng asked, "What will you do there?"

Zhun Mang said, "The Great Abyss is such a thing: pour into it, and it is never full; draw from it, and it is never exhausted. I will wander there."

Yuan Feng said, "Do you have no concern for the people with horizontal eyes? I wish to hear about sagely governance."

Zhun Mang said, "Sagely governance? Offices are bestowed without error, promotions are made without misjudgment, all circumstances are fully seen, and actions are taken accordingly. Words and deeds arise naturally, and the world is transformed. With a wave of the hand or a glance, the people of the four directions all come—this is called sagely governance. I wish to hear about the virtuous person."

Zhun Mang said, "The virtuous person dwells without thought, moves without calculation, and does not harbor right or wrong, beauty or ugliness. Within the four seas, all rejoice in shared benefit and find peace in shared provision. He is as forlorn as an infant who has lost its mother, as bewildered as a traveler who has lost his way. Wealth and resources are abundant, yet he does not know their source; food and drink are sufficient, yet he does not know their origin—this is the bearing of the virtuous person. I wish to hear about the spirit person."

Zhun Mang said, "The supreme spirit person rides the light, and form perishes with him—this is called illuminating the vast. He fulfills his mandate and exhausts his nature, delighting in heaven and earth, and all things dissolve. The myriad things return to their nature—this is called the undifferentiated darkness."

Men Wu Gui and Chi Zhang Manji observed the army of King Wu. Chi Zhang Manji said, "He does not measure up to the Yu clan—hence he has encountered this trouble."

Men Wu Gui said, "Was the world well-governed before the Yu clan governed it? Or did it fall into chaos and then they brought order?"

Chi Zhang Manji said, "The world being well-governed is the wish—why calculate whether it was the Yu clan? The Yu clan’s remedies are like treating sores by shaving the head and applying medicine, or curing illness by seeking a physician. A filial son administers medicine to his compassionate father with a pained expression—sages are ashamed of this. In the age of utmost virtue, worthies are not esteemed, abilities are not employed. Rulers are like the tips of branches, and the people are like wild deer. They are upright yet do not know it as righteousness, they love one another yet do not know it as benevolence, they are sincere yet do not know it as loyalty, they are trustworthy yet do not know it as faithfulness. They move and work together without considering it a favor. Thus, their actions leave no traces, and their affairs leave no record. Filial sons do not flatter their parents, loyal ministers do not fawn on their rulers—this is the height of ministerial conduct. If a son agrees with his parents’ words and finds their actions good, the world calls him an unworthy son. If a minister agrees with his ruler’s words and finds his actions good, the world calls him an unworthy minister. Yet how do we know this must be so?"

When the world calls right what is right and good what is good, it does not deem such people flatterers. Yet does this mean the world holds parents in stricter reverence than rulers? Call a man a flatterer, and he bristles with indignation; call him a sycophant, and his face darkens with anger. Yet all his life he flatters others, all his life he sycophants—gathering crowds with clever parables and polished words, his beginning and end, root and branch, never once held to account. He drapes himself in robes, adorns himself with colors, assumes the manners of a guest, and fawns upon his age, yet never calls himself a flatterer. He joins the company of such men, aligning right and wrong with them, yet never calls himself one of the crowd. This is the height of folly.

To recognize one’s folly is not the greatest folly; to recognize one’s confusion is not the greatest confusion. The truly confused never awaken in their lifetime; the truly foolish never gain wisdom. If three walk together and one is confused, the destination may still be reached, for confusion is in the minority. If two are confused, the journey is labored and the goal unattained, for confusion prevails. Now, with the whole world confused, though I have a direction I seek, how can it be attained? Is this not lamentable?

Grand music does not enter the ears of the village; but let the strains of *Zhe Yang* or *Huang Hua* sound, and they burst into laughter. Thus, lofty words do not lodge in the hearts of the crowd; profound speech remains unspoken, for common speech prevails. With two hobbling along in confusion, the destination is lost. Now, with the whole world confused, though I have a direction I seek, how can it be attained? Knowing it cannot be attained yet forcing the matter is yet another confusion. Better to let it go and not press further. If one does not press, who shares the worry?

A man afflicted with leprosy gives birth to a child in the dead of night. He hastily lights a torch to examine it, anxious lest it resemble himself. A hundred-year-old tree is felled to carve sacrificial goblets, painted in green and yellow with intricate designs—yet its offcuts lie discarded in the ditch. Compare the goblet to the offcut in the ditch: one is deemed beautiful, the other ugly, yet both have lost their original nature. Zeng and Shi may differ in their conduct of righteousness, yet their loss of nature is the same.

There are five ways in which nature is lost:
First, the five colors dazzle the eyes, blinding them;
Second, the five sounds deafen the ears, dulling them;
Third, the five odors cloy the nose, stupefying the senses;
Fourth, the five flavors cloy the palate, deadening it;
Fifth, preferences and aversions agitate the heart, scattering the spirit.
These five are all harms to life.

Yet Yang Zhu and Mo Di, having strayed from the Dao, claim to have attained it—this is not what I call attainment. If being trapped is called attainment, then the turtledove and crow in a cage may also be said to have attained it. Moreover, chasing after preferences and aversions, colors and sounds, to clutter the inner self; donning ceremonial caps and sashes to constrain the outer form; inwardly propped up by a cage of desires, outwardly bound by layers of restraint; standing entangled in these bonds, believing oneself to have attained—this is like a convict with bound arms and shackled fingers, or a tiger and leopard in a sack or cage, yet claiming to have attained.

---

The Dao of Heaven moves without accumulation, and so the myriad things are completed; the Dao of the Emperor moves without accumulation, and so the world submits; the Dao of the Sage moves without accumulation, and so the realm obeys. Those who understand Heaven and penetrate the Sage, whose six senses and four limbs align with the virtue of emperors and kings—their self-cultivation is so profound that nothing disturbs their tranquility.

The Sage’s stillness is not because stillness is good, but because nothing in the myriad things can disturb his heart, and so he is still. When water is still, it clearly mirrors the brows and beard, its level surface serving as a standard—even master craftsmen take it as their model. If still water can be so clear, how much more the spirit? The Sage’s heart is still, a mirror to Heaven and Earth, a reflection of the myriad things.

Emptiness, stillness, tranquility, silence, and non-action—these are the level of Heaven and Earth, the perfection of the Dao and virtue. Thus, emperors, kings, and sages rest in them. In rest, there is emptiness; in emptiness, there is fullness; in fullness, there is order. In emptiness, there is stillness; in stillness, there is movement; in movement, there is attainment. In stillness, there is non-action; in non-action, those who handle affairs are held responsible. In non-action, there is ease; in ease, worries and troubles cannot take hold, and one’s years are long.

Emptiness, stillness, tranquility, silence, and non-action are the root of the myriad things. Understanding this and ruling the south, one becomes a Yao as sovereign; understanding this and serving the north, one becomes a Shun as minister. Applying this in high office, it is the virtue of emperors, kings, and the Son of Heaven; applying this in lowly station, it is the Dao of the obscure sage and unadorned king. Retiring with this and wandering freely among rivers and seas, recluses of the mountains and forests submit to it; advancing with this to govern the world, one’s achievements are great, one’s name illustrious, and the world is unified.

In stillness, one is a sage; in action, one is a king. In non-action, one is revered; in simplicity, no one in the world can rival one’s beauty. Those who clearly understand the virtue of Heaven and Earth—this is called the great root, the great foundation, harmony with Heaven. This is how one harmonizes the world, how one harmonizes with others. Harmonizing with others is called human joy; harmonizing with Heaven is called Heavenly joy.

Zhuangzi said: "O my master! O my master! You order the myriad things yet are not tyrannical, your blessings extend to ten thousand generations yet are not called benevolence, you are older than high antiquity yet are not called long-lived, you cover and bear Heaven and Earth, carve and shape the myriad forms yet are not called skillful—this is called Heavenly joy. Thus, those who know Heavenly joy move with the Heavenly course in life and transform with things in death. In stillness, they share the virtue of yin; in movement, they flow with the waves of yang. Therefore, those who know Heavenly joy harbor no resentment from Heaven, no blame from others, no burden from things, no reproach from spirits.

Thus, in movement, they are like Heaven; in stillness, like Earth. With a single mind settled, they rule the world. Their spirits suffer no haunting, their souls no fatigue. With a single mind settled, the myriad things submit.

The ghost does not afflict them, the soul does not weary—with a single mind settled, the myriad things yield."

Speech that with emptiness and stillness extends to Heaven and Earth, penetrating the myriad things—this is called Heavenly joy. Heavenly joy is the heart of the sage, by which they nurture all under Heaven.

The virtue of emperors and kings takes Heaven and Earth as its model, the Dao and virtue as its master, and non-action as its constant. In non-action, they employ all under Heaven with abundance; in action, they are employed by all under Heaven yet remain insufficient. Thus, the ancients prized non-action.

When the superior practices non-action, the inferior also practices non-action—this is the inferior sharing the same virtue as the superior. When the inferior shares the same virtue as the superior, they do not serve as subjects. When the inferior acts, the superior also acts—this is the superior sharing the same path as the inferior. When the superior shares the same path as the inferior, they do not rule as lords. The superior must practice non-action to employ all under Heaven; the inferior must act to be employed by all under Heaven—this is the unchanging Dao.

Thus, the ancient kings of all under Heaven, though their knowledge embraced Heaven and Earth, did not rely on their own deliberation; though their eloquence adorned the myriad things, they did not rely on their own persuasion; though their abilities exhausted the world within the seas, they did not rely on their own efforts. Heaven does not produce, yet the myriad things transform; Earth does not grow, yet the myriad things are nurtured; emperors and kings practice non-action, yet all under Heaven achieves success.

Thus, nothing is more spirit-like than Heaven, nothing more abundant than Earth, nothing greater than emperors and kings. Therefore, the virtue of emperors and kings matches Heaven and Earth. This is the Dao of riding Heaven and Earth, driving the myriad things, and employing the hands of others.

The root lies with the superior, the branches with the inferior; the essential lies with the ruler, the details with the ministers. The deployment of the three armies and five weapons is the least of virtues; rewards and punishments, advantages and harms, the application of the five punishments—these are the least of teachings. Rituals, laws, measures, and standards, the comparison and elaboration of names—these are the least of governance. The sounds of bells and drums, the forms of feathered banners—these are the least of music. Mourning garments of varying degrees, the extremes of ritual attire—these are the least of returning to the root.

These five least things require the movement of spirit and the activation of the heart’s art before they can be followed. The lesser scholars of later times have had these, yet they are not what should come first.

The ruler leads and the minister follows; the father leads and the son follows; the elder brother leads and the younger brother follows; the elder leads and the younger follows; the man leads and the woman follows; the husband leads and the wife follows. This precedence of honor and sequence is the course of Heaven and Earth, and thus the sage takes it as their model.

Heaven is honored and Earth is lowly—this is the position of spirit and clarity. Spring and summer come first, autumn and winter follow—this is the sequence of the four seasons. The myriad things arise and take form, their flourishing and decline, their transformations and flows—all follow this pattern. If even Heaven and Earth, most spirit-like, have a sequence of honor and precedence, how much more so does the human Dao?

In ancestral temples, kinship is honored; in court, honor is honored; in villages, seniority is honored; in conduct, worthiness is honored—this is the sequence of the great Dao. To speak of the Dao yet violate its sequence is not the true Dao; to speak of the Dao yet not follow the Dao—how can one grasp the Dao?

Thus, the ancients who illuminated the great Dao first illuminated Heaven, then followed with the Dao and virtue. Once the Dao and virtue were illuminated, they followed with benevolence and righteousness. Once benevolence and righteousness were illuminated, they followed with the division of duties. Once the division of duties was illuminated, they followed with names and punishments. Once names and punishments were illuminated, they followed with delegation and responsibility. Once delegation and responsibility were illuminated, they followed with investigation and reflection. Once investigation and reflection were illuminated, they followed with right and wrong. Once right and wrong were illuminated, they followed with rewards and punishments.

Once rewards and punishments were clear, the foolish and the wise were placed appropriately, the noble and the base assumed their positions, the benevolent and the worthless followed their natures. Abilities were necessarily distinguished, names necessarily followed. With this, they served their superiors, nurtured their inferiors, governed things, and cultivated themselves. Without employing cunning schemes, they necessarily returned to the Heavenly. This is called Great Peace, the ultimate of governance.

Thus, the *Book* says: "There are punishments and names." Punishments and names existed among the ancients, yet they were not what should come first. Those who spoke of the great Dao in ancient times could raise the matter of punishments and names after five changes, and speak of rewards and punishments after nine changes.

To abruptly speak of punishments and names is to not know their root; to abruptly speak of rewards and punishments is to not know their beginning. To speak of the Dao and then discourse on it, to be within the Dao and then expound on it—these are what people govern, how can they govern others?
