To abruptly speak of punishments and names, rewards and punishments—this is to possess the tools of governance, not the Dao of governance. They can be employed in all under Heaven, but are insufficient to employ all under Heaven. These are called debaters, people of partial understanding.

Rituals, laws, measures, and standards, the comparison and elaboration of names—these existed among the ancients. They are how the inferior serve the superior, not how the superior nurture the inferior.

In ancient times, Shun asked Yao: "How does the Son of Heaven employ his heart?" Yao said: "I do not slight the bereft, do not abandon the destitute, grieve for the dead, delight in the young, and pity women. This is how I employ my heart." Shun said: "Admirable, yet not great." Yao said: "Then what should it be?" Shun said: "To embody Heavenly virtue and emerge in tranquility, with the sun and moon shining and the four seasons proceeding, like the constancy of day and night, with clouds moving and rain falling."

Yao said: "How entangled and troubled! You harmonize with Heaven; I harmonize with man." Now, Heaven and Earth are what the ancients deemed greatest, what the Yellow Emperor, Yao, and Shun all praised together. Thus, the ancient kings of all under Heaven—what did they do? They simply followed Heaven and Earth.

Confucius traveled west to deposit his books in the Zhou court. Zilu advised: "I have heard that the Zhou’s Keeper of Archives, an elder named Lao Dan, has resigned and returned home. If you wish to deposit your books, you might try visiting him." Confucius said: "Very well." He went to see Lao Dan, but Lao Dan did not consent. Thereupon, Confucius expounded the *Twelve Classics* to refute Lao Dan’s arguments, saying: "Great indeed! I wish to hear your thoughts." Lao Dan said: "Tell me, are benevolence and righteousness part of human nature?" Confucius said: "Yes."

The noble man cannot be without benevolence, for he would not be complete; he cannot be without righteousness, for he would not endure. Benevolence and righteousness are the true nature of the realized person—what more is there to be done?"

Lao Dan said: "May I ask, what do you mean by benevolence and righteousness?"
Confucius said: "Harmony with all things at heart, universal love without partiality—this is the essence of benevolence and righteousness."
Lao Dan said: "Alas! Nearly ruinous are such later words. Is not universal love impractical? Where there is no partiality, there is still partiality. If you, Master, wish to ensure that all under Heaven lose nothing of their natural guidance, then know this: Heaven and Earth have their constancy, the sun and moon their brightness, the stars and constellations their order, birds and beasts their flocks, trees and plants their uprightness. Follow virtue in your conduct, tread the Dao in your steps—this is already perfection. Why then do you clamorously brandish benevolence and righteousness like one beating a drum in search of a lost child? Ah! You, Master, confuse human nature."

Shi Chengqi, having met Laozi, asked: "I have heard that you, Master, are a sage. I did not hesitate to travel a great distance, wishing to see you. For a hundred days I journeyed, my calloused feet bearing the dust, never daring to rest. Now I observe you, and you are no sage."

The mouse burrows have surplus greens, yet you cast aside your younger sister—this is unkindness. Before you, food lies uneaten, both raw and cooked, yet you hoard without end."

Laozi remained silent, offering no response. The next day, Shi Chengqi returned and said, "Yesterday, I reproached you, but now my heart is settled. Why is this?"

Laozi replied, "Those who are cunning, wise, and spiritually sagacious—I believed myself free of such traits. In the past, when you called me an ox, I accepted the name of ox; when you called me a horse, I accepted the name of horse. If one truly possesses a nature, yet refuses the name others give, one invites calamity anew. My garb is ever my garb, yet I do not wear it because it is a garb."

Shi Chengqi stepped sideways to avoid his shadow, then advanced and asked, "How should one cultivate the self?"

Laozi said, "Your bearing is haughty, your gaze piercing, your brow knitted, your mouth tightly sealed, your demeanor rigid—like a horse reined in to a halt. Movement with restraint, action like a crossbow’s trigger, scrutiny with precision, cleverness that perceives excess—all this betrays a lack of trust. At the borderlands, there is a man known as a thief.

The Dao, in its vastness, does not end; in its minuteness, it does not omit. Thus, the ten thousand things are complete. Boundless is its capacity to embrace all; unfathomable is its depth. Forms, virtue, benevolence, and righteousness are but the remnants of spirit—who but the perfected person can stabilize them? The perfected person possesses the world, yet this is no great matter, nor does it burden him. Though the world strives and strains, he does not follow; though he discerns the unreal, he does not shift for gain. He exhausts the true nature of things and guards their root. Thus, he stands beyond Heaven and Earth, leaves the ten thousand things behind, and his spirit is never constrained.

In accord with the Dao, in harmony with virtue, he retreats from benevolence and righteousness, sets aside rites and music—the perfected person’s heart is thus settled. The world values the Dao as found in books. Yet books are but words, and words have their value. What is valued in words is their meaning, and meaning has its source. But the source of meaning cannot be conveyed in speech, and the world, mistakenly, prizes the transmission of words in books. Though the world values them, they are not truly valuable, for what they value is not true value.

What can be seen are forms and colors; what can be heard are names and sounds. Alas! The world believes that forms, colors, names, and sounds suffice to grasp the essence of things. Yet if forms, colors, names, and sounds truly do not suffice, then those who know do not speak, and those who speak do not know—how could the world recognize this?

Duke Huan was reading in the hall above. Below, Wheelwright Bian was carving a wheel. Setting aside his mallet and chisel, he ascended and asked the duke, "May I dare ask what words Your Grace is reading?"

The duke replied, "The words of a sage."

"Is the sage still alive?"

"No, he is already dead."

"Then what Your Grace reads are but the dregs of the ancients."

Duke Huan said, "I am reading—how can a wheelwright presume to criticize? If you have an explanation, speak; if not, you shall die."

Wheelwright Bian said, "Your servant observes this from his own craft. When carving a wheel, if done too slowly, it is smooth but not firm; if too swiftly, it is rough and does not fit. Neither too slow nor too fast—it is grasped by the hand and responds to the heart. The mouth cannot describe it, yet a certain skill resides in the process. I cannot make my son understand it, nor can my son receive it from me. Thus, at seventy, I am still carving wheels. If the ancients and what they could not transmit have perished, then what Your Grace reads are but the dregs of the ancients."

*The Southern Florescence Perfected Scripture, Scroll Three: "Outer Chapters" – "Heaven’s Revolutions" (Chapter Fourteen)*
Does Heaven revolve? Does Earth abide? Do the sun and moon contend for their places?

Who governs these movements? Who upholds their order? Who, without apparent cause, sets them in motion? Is there some hidden mechanism that compels them? Or do they revolve of themselves, unable to cease? Do clouds form rain, or does rain form clouds? Who regulates their rise and fall? Who, without cause, indulges in excess and urges them on? The wind rises from the north, now west, now east, drifting upward in restless motion—who exhales and inhales it? Who, without cause, stirs and scatters it? May I ask the reason?

Wu Xian summoned and said: "Come, let me tell you. Heaven has six poles and five constants; when emperors and kings follow them, there is order, but when they oppose them, there is calamity. The affairs of the Nine Rivers, when governed with perfected virtue, illuminate the earth below, and all under heaven bear them—this is called the Supreme August.

The Grand Steward Dang of Shang asked Zhuangzi about *ren* (benevolence). Zhuangzi said: "Tigers and wolves possess *ren*." Dang said: "What do you mean?" Zhuangzi said: "Father and son show affection to one another—how is this not *ren*?" Dang said: "May I ask about perfect *ren*?" Zhuangzi said: "Perfect *ren* has no partiality." The Grand Steward said: "I have heard that without partiality there is no love, and without love there is no filial piety. Can perfect *ren* be unfilial?" Zhuangzi said: "Not so. Perfect *ren* is loftier still; filial piety is insufficient to describe it. This is not a statement that opposes filial piety, but one that transcends it. One traveling south to Ying, though facing north, cannot see Mount Ming—why is this? Because it is too distant. Thus it is said: Reverent filial piety is easy; [lacuna] loving filial piety is difficult. Loving filial piety is easy, but forgetting one’s parents is difficult. Forgetting one’s parents is easy, but making one’s parents forget you is difficult. Making one’s parents forget you is easy, but forgetting all under heaven is difficult. Forgetting all under heaven is easy, but making all under heaven forget you is difficult. The virtue of Yao and Shun was abandoned, yet they did not act for its sake; their beneficence spread through ten thousand generations, yet none under heaven knew it—how could it be merely a matter of sighing over *ren* and filial piety? Filial piety, fraternal duty, *ren*, *yi* (righteousness), loyalty, trust, integrity, and incorruptibility—these are all self-exertions that enslave one’s virtue; they are not worthy of praise. Thus it is said: The most noble surpasses state rank; the most wealthy surpasses state wealth; the greatest aspiration surpasses fame and reputation. Therefore, the Dao does not change."

Bei Men Cheng asked the Yellow Emperor: "When Your Majesty performed the music of *Xianchi* in the wilds of Dongting, at first I heard it and was afraid; then I heard it and was calmed; finally, I heard it and was bewildered. Vast and silent, I could not find myself." The Emperor said: "You were nearly right to feel so."

I performed it through human affairs, harmonized it with Heaven, enacted it with ritual and righteousness, and established it in the Great Purity. The supreme music first responds to human affairs, aligns with Heaven’s principles, proceeds through the Five Virtues, and accords with nature—only then does it regulate the four seasons and bring perfect harmony to the myriad things.

The four seasons arise in succession, the myriad things follow their birth; now flourishing, now declining, patterned like the alternation of Wen and Wu; now clear, now turbid, yin and yang in balanced harmony, their sounds flowing like radiant light. When hibernating creatures first stir, I startle them with thunder. Its end has no tail, its beginning no head; now dying, now living, now sinking, now rising—ever constant yet never to be awaited. Thus you were afraid.

Then I performed it with the harmony of yin and yang, illuminating it with the brightness of sun and moon. Its sounds could be short or long, soft or hard; transforming uniformly, not bound to fixed forms. In valleys it fills the valleys, in pits it fills the pits; it seals off the spirit, taking things as its measure. Its sounds resound freely, its name lofty and luminous. Thus ghosts and spirits guard their hidden realms, while sun, moon, and stars follow their courses. I halt it at the finite, yet let it flow into the infinite. You wish to ponder it but cannot grasp it, gaze at it but cannot see it, pursue it but cannot reach it. Suddenly you stand on the path of the fourfold void, leaning against a withered catalpa and chanting. Your eyes strain to see what you desire, your strength fails to pursue what you seek—I have already left you behind. When form fills emptiness, it becomes pliable and yielding. You became yielding, and so were calmed.

Then I performed it with the sound of no-sound, attuning it to the mandate of nature. Thus it seemed to blend and surge like tangled growth, a forest of music yet without form; spreading and waving without trailing, dark and obscure yet soundless. Moving without direction, dwelling in profound obscurity—some call it death, some call it life; some call it substance, some call it glory. Flowing and scattering, shifting without fixed sound. The world doubts it, seeking answers from the sages. The sage is one who penetrates to reality and fulfills destiny. The celestial mechanism is not displayed, yet the five senses are all complete—this is called Heaven’s music, which delights the heart without words. Thus the clans of old praised it, saying: "Exhausted in listening, one hears no sound; gazing, one sees no form. It fills heaven and earth, enveloping the six directions." You wish to hear it but find no point of contact—thus you are bewildered.

Music begins with fear, and fear gives rise to reverence; then I followed it with calm, and complacency leads to escape; finally, it ends in confusion, and confusion gives rise to foolishness. Foolishness leads to the Dao, and the Dao can be borne and journeyed with as one.

Confucius traveled west to Wei. Yan Yuan asked Shi Jin, "What do you make of our Master’s conduct?" Shi Jin said, "Alas! Your Master is truly in dire straits." Yan Yuan said, "Why do you say that?" Shi Jin replied: "Before a straw dog is set out for sacrifice, it is placed in a fine basket, wrapped in embroidered cloth, and the invocator fasts and purifies himself to present it. But once it has been set out, passersby trample its head and spine, and fuel-gatherers take it to burn. If one were to gather it up again, place it in a fine basket, wrap it in embroidered cloth, and wander or rest beneath it, one would not dream well and would surely suffer nightmares. Now your Master has taken the already-discarded straw dogs of former teachers, and you disciples wander and rest beneath them. Thus he was hewn down in Song, had his traces erased in Wei, was impoverished in Shang and Zhou—was this not like a nightmare? Besieged between Chen and Cai, seven days without cooked food, life and death as close as neighbors—was this not like a nightmare?

For traveling on water, nothing is better than a boat; for traveling on land, nothing is better than a cart. To take a boat’s suitability for water and try to push it on land is to spend a lifetime moving no more than a few paces. Are not ancient and modern times like water and land? Are not Zhou and Lu like boat and cart? Now to drag Zhou’s practices into Lu is like pushing a boat on land—laborious and fruitless, and one’s person will surely suffer misfortune. He does not understand the transmission without fixed methods, which responds to things without exhaustion.

Moreover, have you alone not seen the well-sweep? Pull it and it bows; release it and it rises. What pulls it is people, not the sweep pulling people—thus it bows and rises without offending anyone. Therefore, the ritual, righteousness, laws, and institutions of the Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors were not prized for uniformity but for their efficacy in governance. Thus the ritual, righteousness, laws, and institutions of the Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors are like the hawthorn, pear, orange, and pomelo—their flavors differ yet all are pleasing to the mouth. Therefore, ritual, righteousness, laws, and institutions are things that change with the times.

Now if you take a macaque and dress it in the Duke of Zhou’s robes, it will surely gnaw and tear them, stripping them off entirely before it is satisfied. To observe the differences between past and present is like the difference between a macaque and the Duke of Zhou. Thus when Xi Shi suffered from heartache and knit her brows in her village, the ugly women of her village saw it and thought it beautiful, so they too clutched their chests and knit their brows in their village. The rich people of the village saw them and firmly shut their doors, refusing to come out; the poor people saw them, took their wives and children, and fled. They knew how to imitate beauty but did not know why Xi Shi’s knitting of brows was beautiful. Alas! Your Master is truly in dire straits."

Confucius had reached the age of fifty-one without hearing the Dao, so he went south to Pei to see Lao Dan. Lao Dan said, "Have you come? I have heard that you are a worthy man of the northern regions—have you attained the Dao?" Confucius said, "I have not." Lao Dan said, "How have you sought it?" Confucius said, "I sought it in measures and calculations, but after five years I had not attained it." Lao Dan said, "How else have you sought it?" Confucius said, "I sought it in yin and yang, but after twelve years I had not attained it." Lao Dan said, "Just so. If the Dao could be offered, there is no one who would not offer it to their lord..."

If the Dao could be advanced, there is no one who would not advance it to their parents; if the Dao could be told to others, there is no one who would not tell it to their brothers; if the Dao could be given to others, there is no one who would not give it to their children and grandchildren. Yet it cannot be so—there is no other reason: internally, there is no master to hold it, and externally, there is no standard to carry it forth. What issues from within is not received from without—thus the sage does not emerge; what enters from without finds no master within—thus the sage does not conceal himself.

Fame is a public vessel—it cannot be taken in excess. Benevolence and righteousness are the thatched huts of the former sages, where one may lodge for a single night but cannot dwell long, for to observe them is to demand much. The utmost persons of antiquity borrowed the Way of benevolence and lodged in the shelter of righteousness, roaming the wilds of carefree wandering, feeding in the fields of simplicity, and standing in the gardens of non-transaction. Carefree wandering is effortless action; simplicity is ease of nourishment; non-transaction is without expenditure. The ancients called this "the wandering of gathering authenticity."

Those who take wealth as right cannot relinquish emolument; those who take prominence as right cannot relinquish fame.

Those who cling to power cannot share its handle. Grasping it, they tremble; releasing it, they grieve—yet they see nothing clearly, ever driven by what they cannot cease. Such are the people punished by Heaven. Resentment and gratitude, taking and giving, admonition and instruction, life and death—these eight are the instruments of governance, and only those who follow great change without obstruction can wield them. Thus it is said: "To govern is to rectify." If one’s heart does not accept this, the Gate of Heaven remains closed.

Confucius visited Lao Dan and spoke of benevolence and righteousness. Lao Dan said: "When chaff fills the eyes, the four directions of heaven and earth seem to shift; when mosquitoes and gnats bite the skin, one cannot sleep through the night. Benevolence and righteousness, so intrusive, stir up my heart—no greater disorder exists! You would have the world not lose its simplicity, yet you yourself move with the wind and stand by accumulated virtue. Why then do you act so strenuously, as if carrying a drum in search of a lost child? The swan is not white from daily bathing, nor is the crow black from daily dyeing. The natural black and white of things are not worth debating; the spectacle of fame and reputation is not worth expanding. When the spring dries up, fish gather on dry land, wetting each other with their breath, moistening each other with foam—yet how much better to forget one another in the rivers and lakes!"

When Confucius returned from seeing Lao Dan, he did not speak for three days. His disciples asked: "Master, when you visited Lao Dan, what advice did you seek to give?" Confucius said: "Only now have I seen the dragon! The dragon coalesces into form, scatters into patterns, rides the clouds and qi, and nourishes itself in yin and yang. My mouth gaped open, yet I could not speak—what advice could I give Lao Dan?"

Zigong said: "Then are there truly people who dwell like corpses yet manifest like dragons, roar like thunder yet remain as silent as the abyss, and act with the power of heaven and earth? Might I observe such a one?" Thereupon, he went to see Lao Dan on Confucius’s behalf.

Lao Dan was sitting in his hall when he responded, softly: "My years have advanced, and I am passing away. What would you warn me of?" Zigong said: "The governance of the Three Kings and Five Emperors differed, yet their legacy of fame was the same. Why then do you alone deem them not sages?"

Lao Dan said: "Young man, come closer. Why do you say they differed?" Zigong replied: "Yao passed the throne to Shun, Shun to Yu, Yu relied on labor while Tang used force, King Wen submitted to Zhou without daring to oppose, and King Wu opposed Zhou without consenting to submit. Thus I say they differed."

Lao Dan said: "Young man, come closer. Let me tell you of the governance of the Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors. The Yellow Emperor governed the world by unifying the people’s hearts, so that when parents died, they did not mourn—and none found fault with this. Yao governed the world by fostering kinship, so that when people killed their kin, none found fault with this. Shun governed the world by inciting competition, so that pregnant women gave birth in ten months, children spoke at five months, and before they could crawl, they began to recognize others—thus people began to die young. Yu governed the world by altering the people’s hearts, so that each had their own intentions, and weapons followed suit. Killing thieves was not considered killing; people acted as their own kind, and the world was left to itself. Thus the world was greatly alarmed, and the Confucians and Mohists arose. Their beginnings had order, yet now—what can one say of women today? I tell you: the governance of the Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors was called ‘order,’ yet no greater disorder existed. The wisdom of the Three Sovereigns obscured the brightness of the sun and moon above, disrupted the essence of mountains and rivers below, and threw the seasons’ workings into chaos. Their knowledge exceeded the tail of the *qujiang* beast, yet creatures could not secure the natural state of their lives. And they still called themselves sages—is this not shameful? Truly, they knew no shame!"

Zigong stood uneasily, shifting his feet. Confucius said to Lao Dan: "I have studied the Six Classics—*The Odes*, *The Documents*, *The Rites*, *The Music*, *The Changes*, and *The Spring and Autumn*—and believed myself well-versed in their principles. I have advised seventy-two rulers, expounding the Way of the former kings and clarifying the governance of the Zhou and Shao, yet not a single ruler adopted them. How difficult it is to persuade people, and how hard to illuminate the Dao!"

Lao Dan said: "Fortunate indeed that you did not encounter a ruler of this age! The Six Classics are the worn tracks of the former kings—how could they be the very governance itself? What you speak of is still but the track. The track is what the shoe leaves behind—how could it be the shoe itself? When white egrets gaze at one another, their pupils do not move, yet they mate; insects, the male singing on the upwind, the female responding from downwind, yet they mate. Each kind has its own male and female, thus they mate. Nature cannot be altered, fate cannot be changed, time cannot be halted, the Dao cannot be blocked. If one attains the Dao, nothing is impossible; if one loses it, nothing is possible."

Three months later, Confucius visited Lao Dan again and said: "I have grasped it! Crows and magpies hatch their young, fish pass foam to one another, the slender-waisted wasp transforms, and the elder brother weeps for his younger sibling. For too long, I have not transformed with others. If I do not transform with others, how can I transform them?"

Lao Dan said: "Good. You have grasped it."

**Chapter Fifteen: Carving Intentions**
To carve intentions and uphold conduct, to depart from the world and differ from the common, to engage in lofty discourse and bitter reproach—this is but arrogance. Such are the scholars of mountains and valleys, not the people of this world, favored by those who wither and plunge into the abyss. To speak of benevolence, righteousness, loyalty, and trust, of reverence, frugality, and deference—this is but cultivation. Such are the scholars of a peaceful age, the teachers and instructors, favored by those who wander and dwell in learning. To speak of great achievements, to establish great fame, to order ruler and minister, to rectify high and low—this is but governance.

Such are the scholars of the court, the men who honor their lords and strengthen their states, favored by those who achieve merit and annex territory.

To dwell in marshes and wilds, to abide in seclusion and openness, to fish and idle—this is but nonaction. Such are the scholars of rivers and seas, the men who flee the world, favored by those who seek leisure. To practice breath control, to exhale the old and inhale the new, to stretch like a bear and crane—this is but longevity. Such are the practitioners of Dao-yin, the men who nurture the body, favored by those who, like Peng Zu, seek long life.

Yet one who does not carve intentions yet rises high, who is not bound by benevolence and righteousness yet cultivates virtue, who seeks no merit or fame yet governs, who does not flee to rivers and seas yet finds leisure, who does not practice Dao-yin yet attains longevity—such a one forgets nothing, yet lacks nothing. Serene and boundless, all beauties follow in their wake. This is the Dao of Heaven and Earth, the virtue of the sage.

Thus it is said: tranquility and stillness, emptiness and nonaction—these are the equilibrium of Heaven and Earth, the essence of the Dao and virtue. Thus it is said: when the sage rests in ease, he is level and yielding; being level and yielding, he is tranquil and expansive. Tranquil and expansive, then cares and troubles cannot enter, perverse qi cannot assail. Thus his virtue is whole and his spirit unharmed.

Thus it is said: the sage’s life follows the movement of Heaven; his death is the transformation of things. At rest, he shares the virtue of yin; in motion, he moves with the wave of yang. He does not precede fortune, nor initiate misfortune. He responds only when stimulated, moves only when pressed, rises only when compelled. He discards knowledge and precedent, following the principle of Heaven. Thus he suffers no heavenly disasters, no burdens from things, no human reproach, no blame from spirits. His life is like floating; his death, like repose. He neither schemes nor plans ahead. Radiant yet not dazzling, trustworthy yet not bound by expectation. In sleep, he does not dream; awake, he knows no care. His spirit is pure, his soul untiring. Emptiness and tranquility, ease and joy—these accord with Heavenly virtue.

Thus it is said: sorrow and joy are the perversions of virtue; delight and anger, the transgressions of the Dao; likes and dislikes, the failures of virtue. Therefore, a heart without sorrow or joy is the perfection of virtue; unchanging unity is the perfection of stillness; nonaction is the perfection of emptiness; noninterference with things is the perfection of indifference; nonresistance is the perfection of purity.

Thus it is said: if the body labors without rest, it wears out; if essence is used without cease, it exhausts; exhaustion leads to depletion. Water’s nature is such that if it is unmixed, it is clear; if undisturbed, it is level. If pent up and not flowing, it cannot remain clear—this is the image of Heavenly virtue.

Thus it is said: pure and unadulterated, still and unchanging, indifferent and nonactive, moving in accord with Heaven’s course—this is the Way of nurturing the spirit. As for the sword of Gan and Yue, though kept in a casket and never used, it is treasured to the utmost. When the spirit flows unimpeded in all four directions, reaching everywhere without limit, ascending to Heaven above, coiling upon the Earth below, transforming and nourishing the myriad things without leaving a trace—its name is "unity with the Emperor." The Way of purity and simplicity is to guard only the spirit. Guard it without loss, and you become one with the spirit. The essence of this unity penetrates, harmonizing with Heavenly order.

A rustic saying goes: the common man values profit; the incorruptible, fame; the worthy, aspiration; the sage, essence. Thus "simplicity" means having nothing mixed with it; "purity" means not harming the spirit. One who embodies purity and simplicity is called a "true person."

**Chapter Sixteen: Cultivating Nature**
To cultivate nature through custom is to use worldly learning to seek the recovery of one’s original state. To be entangled in desires by custom is to use thought to seek the attainment of clarity—these are called the benighted people.

The ancients who governed the Dao used tranquility to nurture knowledge. They lived without employing knowledge, which is called using knowledge to nurture tranquility. Knowledge and tranquility nurtured each other, and harmony and principle emerged from their nature. Virtue is harmony; the Dao is principle. Virtue admits all without exclusion—this is benevolence. The Dao orders all without exception—this is righteousness. Righteousness made manifest, things draw near—this is loyalty. Centered, pure, and returning to genuine feeling—this is joy. Trust in conduct, receptivity in bearing, and compliance with pattern—this is ritual. When ritual and music are practiced in a biased manner, the world falls into chaos. If one corrects others while obscuring one’s own virtue, virtue is no longer all-embracing. If it is not all-embracing, things inevitably lose their nature.

In ancient times, people dwelt in primordial chaos, yet with their age they attained indifference and stillness. In that era, yin and yang were harmonious and calm, ghosts and spirits did not disturb, the four seasons kept their rhythm, the myriad things were unharmed, the multitude of living beings did not die young. Though humans possessed knowledge, they had no use for it—this was called "perfect unity." In that time, none acted, yet all was constantly spontaneous.

When virtue declined, Sui Ren and Fu Xi began to govern the world, and thus compliance no longer achieved unity. When virtue declined further, Shen Nong and the Yellow Emperor began to govern the world, and thus peace no longer ensured compliance. When virtue declined still further, Yao and Shun began to govern the world, initiating the currents of governance and transformation. They diluted the rich and scattered the simple, departed from the Dao to pursue goodness, and risked virtue to enact policies. Then they abandoned nature and followed the heart. Hearts recognized one another through knowledge, yet this was insufficient to settle the world. Thus they supplemented it with culture and enriched it with erudition. Culture extinguished substance; erudition drowned the heart. Then the people began to be confused and disordered, unable to return to their nature and recover their original state.

From this perspective, the world has lost the Dao, and the Dao has lost the world. World and Dao mutually lose each other—how can the person of the Dao arise in the world, and how can the world arise in the Dao? If the Dao cannot arise in the world, and the world cannot arise in the Dao, then even if the sage does not dwell in the mountains and forests, his virtue is hidden. Hidden, yet not by his own concealment.

The so-called "recluses" of old did not crouch in concealment and refuse to appear, nor did they shut their mouths and refuse to speak, nor hide their knowledge and refuse to express it. The times and fate were greatly awry.

When the times and fate align and one’s Way flourishes throughout the world, one returns to unity without seeking nearness. When the times and fate do not align and one’s Way is utterly thwarted in the world, one deeply roots and calmly abides, awaiting the moment—this is the Way of preserving the self. The ancients who conducted themselves did not adorn their knowledge with eloquence, nor did they exhaust the world with their knowledge, nor did they exhaust virtue with their knowledge. Serene in their place, they returned to their nature—what more was there to do?

The Dao is not meant for petty conduct; virtue is not meant for petty understanding. Petty understanding harms virtue; petty conduct harms the Dao. Thus it is said: rectify the self and nothing more. To delight in wholeness is called "attaining one’s aspiration." The ancients who spoke of attaining aspiration did not mean attaining rank and emolument; they meant that nothing could augment their joy. Those today who speak of attaining aspiration mean attaining rank and emolument. When rank and emolument rest upon the self, they are not of one’s nature and fate—they are the fortuitous arrival of things, a lodging. As a lodging, their coming cannot be enclosed, nor their going halted. Therefore, one does not indulge one’s aspirations for rank and emolument, nor follow convention in poverty and hardship. One’s joy is the same in both, and so one is without sorrow. If one is unhappy when the lodging departs, then even in joy, one has never been free from distraction. Thus it is said: those who lose themselves in things and forfeit their nature to custom are called "the people of inverted priorities."

---

*Chapter Seventeen: "Autumn Floods" of the* Nan Hua True Classic

When the autumn floods arrive, the hundred streams pour into the Yellow River. The current swells so vast that between its banks and islets, one cannot distinguish ox from horse. Thereupon, the River Lord rejoiced, believing all the world’s beauty to be contained within himself. Following the current eastward, he came to the Northern Sea. Gazing east, he saw no end to the waters. Thereupon, the River Lord turned his face in dismay, looked toward Ruo, and sighed: "There is a common saying: 'Hearing the Dao a hundred times, one thinks none can compare to oneself.' That describes me. Moreover, I once heard of those who belittled Confucius’s learning and disparaged Boyi’s righteousness, and I did not believe it. Now that I have witnessed the boundlessness of your waters, had I not come to your gate, I would have been in peril—I would long have been ridiculed by those of great understanding."

The Lord of the Northern Sea said: "A well frog cannot speak of the sea, confined as it is by its pit; a summer insect cannot speak of ice, bound as it is by its season; a narrow scholar cannot speak of the Dao, constrained as he is by his teachings. Now that you have emerged from your banks and observed the great sea, you know your own inadequacy—you may now be spoken with about great principles. Of all the waters under heaven, none is greater than the sea. The ten thousand streams return to it, yet it never fills, though no one knows when it will cease; its tail drains, yet it never empties, though no one knows when it will end. Unchanged by spring or autumn, unmoved by flood or drought, it surpasses the flow of the Yangtze and Yellow Rivers beyond all measure. Yet I have never taken pride in this, for I compare my form to heaven and earth and receive qi from yin and yang. Within heaven and earth, I am but a pebble or a twig in a great mountain. Aware of my own smallness, why should I take pride? Measuring the four seas within heaven and earth, are they not like a molehill in a vast marsh? Measuring the Central States within the seas, are they not like a grain of millet in a granary? The number of things is called ten thousand, yet humans are but one among them. Of all the people gathered in the nine provinces, where grain is grown and boats and carts travel, humans are but one among them. Compared to the ten thousand things, are they not like the tip of a hair on a horse’s body? What the Five Emperors inherited, what the Three Kings contended for, what the humane worry over, what the devoted labor for—all of it is this. Boyi renounced it to gain fame; Confucius discoursed on it to display his erudition—this was their self-aggrandizement. Is it not like your former self-aggrandizement over your waters?"

The River Lord said: "Then may I consider heaven and earth great and the tip of a hair small?"

The Lord of the Northern Sea said: "No. Things have measure without end; time has no cessation; allotment has no constancy; beginning and end have no fixed pattern. Thus, the greatly wise observe both far and near, so that the small is not deemed little, nor the great deemed much. Knowing measure is without end, they verify the old and the new, so that though pressed they are not distressed, though assailed they are not divided. Knowing time has no cessation, they examine fullness and emptiness, so that though they gain they are not joyful, though they lose they are not sorrowful—knowing allotment has no constancy. Understanding the level path, though born they are not delighted, though dead they do not see it as misfortune—knowing beginning and end have no fixed pattern. Calculating what humans know, it is less than what they do not know; the time of their lives is less than the time before they were born. Seeking to exhaust the realm of the utmost great with what is utmost small, they become confused and cannot attain understanding. From this perspective, how can one know the tip of a hair is sufficient to determine the boundary of the utmost small? How can one know heaven and earth are sufficient to exhaust the realm of the utmost great?"

The River Lord said: "Those who discourse in the world all say: 'The utmost refined has no form; the utmost great cannot be encompassed.' Is this truly so?"

The Lord of the Northern Sea said: "From the small, one cannot see the great in its entirety; from the great, one cannot see the small clearly. The refined is the subtlest of the small; the vast is the most abundant of the great..."

Thus, their advantages differ—this is the nature of circumstances. The refined and the coarse are terms applied to what has form; what is formless cannot be divided by calculation; what cannot be encompassed cannot be exhausted by reckoning. What can be discussed in words is the coarse aspect of things; what can be grasped by intention is the refined aspect of things. That which words cannot discuss, and intention cannot fathom, does not fall within the scope of refined or coarse.

Therefore, the conduct of the great person does not extend to harming others, nor does it abound in benevolence and kindness. In action, they do not seek profit, nor do they despise gatekeepers and servants. They do not contend over goods and wealth, nor do they excessively defer; in affairs, they do not rely on others, nor do they overindulge in their own strength, nor do they despise the greedy and corrupt. Their conduct differs from the common, yet they do not excessively avoid eccentricity. They follow the multitude in their actions, yet do not despise flatterers. The world’s ranks and emoluments are insufficient to encourage them; disgrace and punishment are insufficient to shame them. They know that right and wrong cannot be definitively divided, nor can the fine and the vast be clearly demarcated.

It is said: The Daoist does not strive, the one of utmost virtue does not attain, the great person has no fixed self—this is the utmost in moderation.

The River Lord said: "Beyond things, within things—how does one determine nobility and baseness? How does one determine smallness and greatness?"

The Lord of the Northern Sea said: "From the perspective of the Dao, things have no nobility or baseness. From the perspective of things, each values itself and devalues others. From the perspective of custom, nobility and baseness do not reside in oneself. From the perspective of differentiation: if you magnify what is great in something, then nothing is not great; if you diminish what is small in something, then nothing is not small. Knowing that heaven and earth are like a grain of millet, and that the tip of a hair is like a mountain, then the distinctions of measurement become apparent.

From the perspective of function: if you affirm what something possesses, then nothing does not possess; if you negate what something lacks, then nothing does not lack. Knowing that east and west are opposites yet cannot be without each other, then the division of function is settled.

From the perspective of inclination: if you affirm what something affirms, then nothing is not affirmed; if you negate what something negates, then nothing is not negated. Knowing that Yao and Jie each affirmed their own way and negated the other, then the inclinations of conduct become clear.

In ancient times, Yao and Shun yielded the throne, yet Zhi and Qiang yielded and perished; Tang and Wu contended and became kings, while the Duke of Bai contended and was destroyed. From this, we see that the rites of contention and yielding, the conduct of Yao and Jie, nobility and baseness have their times—they cannot be taken as constant.

A battering ram can breach a city wall but cannot plug a hole—this speaks of differing implements. The swift horses Qi Ji and Luo Li can gallop a thousand *li* in a day, yet they are not as good as a wildcat at catching mice—this speaks of differing skills. The owl can snatch fleas at night and discern the tip of a hair, yet when it stares with wide eyes, it cannot see a mountain—this speaks of differing natures.

Thus, it is said: How can one take this as right and that as wrong, take order as order and chaos as chaos? This is to fail to understand the principles of heaven and earth and the conditions of the myriad things. It is like taking heaven as teacher without earth, taking yin as teacher without yang—its impossibility is clear. Yet if one still speaks of it without ceasing, they are either foolish or deceitful.

The succession of emperors and kings differs; the inheritance of the Three Dynasties differs. Those who go against their time and defy custom are called usurpers; those who align with their time and follow custom are called righteous followers.

Silent, O River Lord, how do you know the gate of nobility and baseness, the domain of smallness and greatness?"

The River Lord said: "Then what should I do? What should I not do? How should I ultimately manage my acceptance and rejection, my inclinations and aversions?"

The Lord of the Northern Sea said: "From the perspective of the Dao, what is noble? What is base? This is called reversal and overflow—do not constrain your aspirations, for they will greatly diverge from the Dao. What is few? What is many? This is called alternation and exchange—do not act with singularity, for you will be at odds with the Dao.

Solemn as a state with its ruler, yet without private virtue; reverent as a sacrifice with its altar, yet without private blessing; boundless as the four directions without limit, without any restricted domain. Embracing the myriad things, who bears them up? This is called having no fixed direction.

The myriad things are equal—who is short? Who is long? The Dao has no end or beginning; things have death and life, yet do not rely on their completion. Now empty, now full, they do not abide in their form. Years cannot be lifted, time cannot be halted; ebb and flow, fullness and emptiness—when they end, they begin again. This is how one speaks of the great meaning’s direction, discusses the principles of the myriad things.

The arising of things is like a gallop, like a swift ride—there is no movement that does not change, no moment that does not shift. What is there to do? What is there not to do? Things will transform of themselves."

The River Lord said: "Then what is the value of the Dao?"

The Lord of the Northern Sea said: "One who knows the Dao necessarily comprehends principle; one who comprehends principle necessarily understands adaptability; one who understands adaptability does not allow things to harm them. The one of utmost virtue: fire cannot burn them, water cannot drown them, cold and heat cannot harm them, birds and beasts cannot injure them. This does not mean they are careless—it means they discern safety and danger, are serene amid fortune and misfortune, are cautious in approach and retreat, so that nothing can harm them.

Thus it is said: Heaven is within, humans are without; virtue resides in heaven. Knowing the conduct of heaven and humans, rooted in heaven, positioned in attainment—contracting and expanding, returning to the essential and speaking of the ultimate.

What is called heaven? What is called human?"

Boreas replied: "Oxen and horses have four legs—this is called heaven; bridling a horse’s head or piercing an ox’s nose—this is called human. Thus it is said: Do not let the human obliterate heaven, do not let the artificial obliterate fate, do not let attainment be sacrificed for fame. Guard this carefully and do not lose it—this is called returning to one’s true nature.

Kui pitied Xuan, Xuan pitied the snake, the snake pitied the wind, the wind pitied the eye, and the eye pitied the heart.

Kui said to the jiao: 'I hop and stride on one foot—nothing could be simpler. Yet you employ ten thousand feet—how is this possible?'
Xuan replied: 'Not so. Have you not seen one who spits? When they expel it, the large droplets are like pearls, the small like mist, and the countless falling fragments cannot be numbered. Now you move my heavenly mechanism, yet do not understand how it works.'

Xuan said to the snake: 'I walk with many feet yet cannot match you who have none—why is this?'
The snake replied: 'How can one alter what the heavenly mechanism moves? What need have I for feet?'

The snake said to the wind: 'I move my spine and ribs to advance—there is something to it. Yet you rise in a great gust from the Northern Sea and enter the Southern Sea as if nothing were there—why is this?'
The wind replied: 'Indeed, I rise in a great gust from the Northern Sea and enter the Southern Sea, yet a finger can overcome me, a foot can trample me. Nevertheless, only I can snap great trees and topple grand houses. Thus, to turn many small defeats into a great victory—only the sage can achieve this.'

Confucius was traveling in Kuang when men of Song surrounded him several layers deep, yet he continued strumming and singing without pause. Zilu entered and said: 'How can you be so joyful, Master?'
Confucius replied: 'Come, let me tell you. I have long sought to avoid adversity, yet cannot escape it—this is fate. I have long sought success, yet cannot attain it—this is timing. In the age of Yao and Shun, there were no suffering people, not because of wisdom; in the age of Jie and Zhou, there were no successful people, not because of folly. Such is the way of circumstances.

The fisherman who does not avoid dragons and jiao when traveling by water—this is the courage of his craft. The hunter who does not avoid rhinoceroses and tigers when traveling by land—this is the courage of his craft. The warrior who faces crossed blades and regards death as life—this is the courage of the resolute. To know that adversity has its fate, that success has its time, and to face great peril without fear—this is the courage of the sage. Where do you stand? Be at ease.'"

"My fate is constrained by forces beyond me.

Soon after, armored soldiers advanced and declared: 'We mistook you for Yang Hu, hence the siege. Now we see our error—we beg leave to withdraw.'

Gongsun Long asked Wei Mou: 'In my youth, I studied the Dao of the former kings; in maturity, I mastered the practice of benevolence and righteousness. I harmonized sameness and difference, separated hardness and whiteness; affirmed the untrue, negated the possible; confounded the wisdom of the hundred schools, exhausted the eloquence of all debaters. I took myself for one of perfect understanding. Yet now I hear Zhuangzi’s words and am utterly confounded. Do I fall short in debate? Or do I simply lack his depth of insight? Now I stand speechless—dare I ask the way forward?'

Prince Mou leaned against his desk, sighed deeply, looked to the heavens, and laughed: 'Have you never heard of the frog in the shallow well? It boasted to the sea turtle: "How I delight in my life! I leap and bound along the well’s rim, rest in the chinks of its broken walls. When I plunge into the water, it supports my armpits and cradles my chin; when I trample the mud, it swallows my feet and ankles. Look around—tadpoles, crabs, and shrimp—none can match me! Moreover, to command the waters of this entire ravine and revel in the joys of my well—what more could one desire? Why not visit sometime and see for yourself?"

The sea turtle had not yet set its left foot inside when its right knee was already stuck. It backed away cautiously and told the frog of the sea: "A distance of a thousand *li* cannot convey its vastness; a height of a thousand *ren* cannot measure its depth. In the time of Yu, nine years out of ten were floods, yet the waters did not increase; in the time of Tang, seven years out of eight were droughts, yet the shores did not diminish. Unchanged by the passage of moments or ages, unmoved by the ebb and flow of quantity—this is the great joy of the Eastern Sea."

At this, the frog in the shallow well was stunned, bewildered, and lost in self-doubt.

Now, if one’s understanding cannot even grasp the boundaries of right and wrong, yet still seeks to examine Zhuangzi’s words—this is like ordering a flea to carry a mountain or a mosquito larva to race across a river. It is a task one cannot bear. And if one’s knowledge cannot fathom the subtleties of his discourse, yet clings to momentary advantage—is this not the very case of the frog in the shallow well?

Moreover, he roams the Yellow Springs and ascends the Great Void, unbound by south or north, released in all four directions, submerged in the unfathomable. Unrestrained by east or west, he begins in profound obscurity and returns to the Great Thoroughfare. Yet you seek to scrutinize him with narrow inquiry, to probe him with argument—this is truly using a tube to view the sky or a awl to measure the earth. Is it not petty?

Go now. Have you not also heard of the young men of Shouling who went to Handan to learn its way of walking?"

Without mastering the state’s skill, they lost even their former gait—all they could do was crawl home on hands and knees. Should you not leave now, you too will forget your old ways and lose your own craft.”

Gongsun Long’s mouth gaped open, unable to close; his tongue stiffened, unable to descend. He fled in haste.

Zhuangzi was fishing at Pu River when the King of Chu sent two ministers to approach him first, saying, “Our king wishes to burden you with the affairs of the realm.” Zhuangzi held his rod and did not turn, saying, “I have heard that in Chu there is a divine tortoise, dead for three thousand years, which the king keeps wrapped in silk and stored in a shrine. Would this tortoise rather have died to leave its bones venerated, or lived to drag its tail through the mud?” The two ministers replied, “It would rather live and drag its tail through the mud.” Zhuangzi said, “Go then. I too will drag my tail through the mud.”

When Huizi was prime minister of Liang, Zhuangzi went to visit him. Someone told Huizi, “Zhuangzi is coming to replace you as minister.” Thereupon Huizi grew alarmed and searched the state for three days and three nights. Zhuangzi went to see him and said, “In the south there is a bird called the *yuanyang*. Do you know it? The *yuanyang* sets out from the South Sea and flies to the North Sea; it perches only on the *wutong* tree, eats only bamboo fruit, and drinks only from sweet springs. Then a kite, having caught a rotten rat, looked up as the *yuanyang* passed and screeched, ‘Hah!’ Now do you mean to screech at me with your state of Liang?”

Zhuangzi and Huizi strolled above the bridge at Hao River. Zhuangzi said, “The minnows swim about so freely—is this not joy for the fish?” Huizi said, “You are not a fish. How do you know what brings the fish joy?” Zhuangzi said, “You are not me. How do you know that I do not know what brings the fish joy?” Huizi said, “I am not you, so of course I do not know you. But you are certainly not a fish, so it is entirely clear that you do not know what brings the fish joy.” Zhuangzi said, “Let us return to the beginning. When you asked, ‘How do you know what brings the fish joy?’ you already knew that I knew—you were only asking me. I know it here above the Hao.”

*Southern Florescence Perfected Scripture*, Scroll Three, Outer Chapters, “Perfect Joy” Eighteen

Is there in the world perfect joy, or is there not? Is there a way to preserve life, or is there not? What then should one do, and on what should one rely?

Where should one flee, and where should one abide?
What should one approach, and what should one forsake?
What should one delight in, and what should one detest?

Now, what the world reveres are wealth, honor, longevity, and goodness;
what it delights in are bodily ease, rich flavors, fine garments, beautiful sights, and harmonious sounds.
What it despises are poverty, lowliness, early death, and misfortune;
what it suffers are a body denied rest, a mouth denied rich flavors, a form denied fine garments, eyes denied beautiful sights, and ears denied harmonious sounds.
If these are denied, then great sorrow and fear arise.
In their concern for form, how foolish they are!

The wealthy exhaust themselves with toil, amassing wealth they cannot fully use—
in their concern for form, how estranged they are!
The honored pass their nights and days pondering right and wrong—
in their concern for form, how distant they are!

When a person is born, sorrow is born with them.
The long-lived grow weary and resentful, their long sorrow refusing to end—
what bitter suffering!
In their concern for form, how far they have strayed!

The steadfast knight is praised by the world as good,
yet this is not enough to preserve his life.
I do not know whether goodness is truly good, or truly not good.
If it is good, it is not enough to preserve life;
if it is not good, it is enough to preserve others.

Thus it is said:
When loyal admonitions go unheeded, one retreats and does not contend.
Thus Wu Zixu contended and was destroyed in body,
yet had he not contended, his name would not have endured.
Is there truly goodness, or is there not?

Now, what the common world pursues and delights in—
do I know whether this delight is truly delight, or truly not delight?
I observe what the common world delights in:
the masses rush together, restless as if they cannot stop,
and all call it delight.
Yet I find it neither delightful nor not delightful.
Is there truly delight, or is there not?

I take non-action as true delight,
yet this is what the common world finds most bitter.
Thus it is said:
Perfect joy is without joy; perfect praise is without praise.
The right and wrong of the world cannot truly be settled.
Nevertheless, non-action can settle right and wrong.
Perfect joy preserves life—only non-action comes close to this.
Let me try to speak of it.

Heaven is non-active and so becomes clear;
Earth is non-active and so becomes tranquil.
Thus, when non-action unites, the myriad things transform.
Vague and indistinct, with no place from which they emerge;
indistinct and vague, with no form to be seen.
The teeming things, each in its place, all grow from non-action.

Thus it is said:
Heaven and Earth are non-active, yet nothing is left undone.
Who among men can attain non-action?

When Zhuangzi’s wife died, Huizi came to offer condolences.
Zhuangzi was squatting with his legs spread, drumming on a basin and singing.
Huizi said, “To live with someone, raise a child to adulthood, grow old together, and then not weep at their death would be enough—
yet you drum on a basin and sing. Is this not excessive?”
Zhuangzi said, “Not so.
When she first died, how could I alone not feel sorrow?
But I examined her beginning and found she had no life.
Not only no life, but originally no form;
not only no form, but originally no qi.
Amidst the vague and indistinct, there was a transformation, and qi arose;
qi transformed and form arose;
form transformed and life arose.
Now, there is another transformation, and she has gone to death—
this is like the passing of spring, autumn, winter, and summer in the four seasons.

When others lie at rest in the great chamber, I would wail loudly and follow in weeping,
believing myself to be unenlightened about fate—so I stopped.”

Zhilishu and Huajieshu were observing the mound of Mingbo and the wilds of Kunlun,
where the Yellow Emperor once rested.
Suddenly, a wen grew on Huajieshu’s left elbow, and he frowned in displeasure.
Zhilishu said, “Do you detest it?”
Huajieshu said, “No, why would I detest it?
Life is a temporary borrowing;
what borrows and gives rise to life is but dust and grime.
Death and life are like day and night.
Moreover, you and I observe transformation, and transformation has reached me.
Why should I detest it?”

When Zhuangzi went to Chu, he saw an empty skull, still intact in form.
He tapped it with his horsewhip and asked,
“Did you, sir, covet life and lose the Dao, and so come to this?
Or did you suffer the fall of a state, execution by axe, and so come to this?
Or did you commit some unworthy deed, bringing shame to your parents, wife, and children, and so come to this?
Or did you suffer the miseries of cold and hunger, and so come to this?
Or did your years run out, and so come to this?”

Having finished speaking, he took the skull, placed it under his head, and lay down to sleep.
In the middle of the night, the skull appeared to him in a dream and said,
“Your words resemble those of a sophist.
All that you mentioned are the burdens of the living.
In death, there are none of these.
Would you like to hear the truth of death?”
Zhuangzi said, “Yes.”
The skull said, “In death, there is no ruler above, no subject below,
nor the affairs of the four seasons.
At ease, one takes heaven and earth as spring and autumn.
Even the joy of a king facing south cannot surpass it.”
Zhuangzi did not believe him and said,
“I will have the Controller of Fate restore your form,
give you bones, flesh, and skin,
and return you to your parents, wife, children, neighbors, and acquaintances.
Would you desire this?”
The skull furrowed its brow and sighed deeply, saying,
“How could I abandon the joy of a king facing south and return to the toils of the human world?”

When Yan Hui was about to leave for Qi, Confucius wore a worried expression.
Zigong stepped down from his mat and asked,
“May I dare to ask why, when Yan Hui is going to Qi, you wear a worried expression?”
Confucius said, “Excellent is your question.
Long ago, Guanzi spoke words I greatly admired:
‘A small sack cannot hold a large thing; a short rope cannot draw from a deep well.’
Such is the way of things—
fate has its completions, and form has its fitting uses.
These cannot be diminished or increased.
I fear that Yan Hui will speak to the Marquis of Qi of the Ways of Yao, Shun, and the Yellow Emperor,
and further add the words of Suiren and Shennong.
The Marquis will seek within himself and find nothing,
and finding nothing, he will be confused.
When a person is confused, they court death.”

And have you alone not heard this tale? Long ago, a seabird alighted on the outskirts of Lu. The Marquis of Lu received it with ceremony and offered it wine in the ancestral temple, performed the *Nine Shao* as music, and prepared the grandest sacrificial feast. Yet the bird only gazed in distress, grieving—it dared not eat a single morsel, dared not drink a single cup, and died after three days.

This was nourishing the bird with what nourishes oneself, not with what nourishes the bird. To nourish a bird as a bird should be nourished, one ought to let it perch in deep forests, roam over open plains, float upon rivers and lakes, feed on minnows and loaches, follow its flock in flight, and rest where it wills. It cares nothing for human speech—why then all this pomp and fuss?

When the music of *Xianchi* and *Nine Shao* is played across the wilds of Dongting, birds hear it and take flight, beasts hear it and flee, fish hear it and dive deep, while men, should they hear it, turn back to gather and listen. Fish live in water, yet men die in it—surely they differ, for their likes and dislikes are not the same.

Thus the sages of old did not impose a single standard of ability nor demand uniformity in affairs. Names were confined to reality, and principles were set to fit the circumstance—this is what is called "orderly attainment and enduring fortune."

Liezi was traveling and took a meal by the roadside when he saw a century-old skull. Plucking a clump of mugwort, he pointed to it and said: "Only you and I know that you have never died, never been born. Are you truly grieved? Am I truly joyful?"

Seeds possess their latent potential. When they obtain water, they grow robust; when they find the border between water and earth, they become the raiment of dragons. Born upon a mound, they become the mound-crow; if the mound-crow gains dense shade, it becomes crow’s-foot. The root of crow’s-foot becomes the navel-cicada, its leaves become butterflies. Butterflies swiftly transform into insects, born beneath the tortoise’s shell, their form like a cast-off skin, their name *jiurang*. After a thousand days, *jiurang* becomes a bird, called *ganyugu*. The foam of *ganyugu* becomes *simí*, and *simí* becomes *shiyùn*. From *shiyùn* is born *yixia*! From *huangyu* is born *jiuyou*, from *piyin* is born the rotten silkworm cocoon. The sheep’s-tare joins with the uninked bamboo, and from old bamboo is born *qingning*; *qingning* gives birth to *cheng*, *cheng* to the horse, the horse to man—and man, in turn, returns into the mechanism. All things emerge from the mechanism, all things enter into the mechanism.

*The Southern Florescence Sutra*, "Spring, Part Three" concludes.

---

Those who grasp the essence of life do not strive for what life cannot achieve; those who grasp the essence of fate do not strive for what knowledge cannot alter. To nourish the body, one must first provide it with things—yet there are cases where things are abundant yet the body remains unnourished. To sustain life, one must first avoid separation from the body—yet there are cases where the body remains intact yet life perishes. Life’s arrival cannot be refused, its departure cannot be halted. Alas! The people of the world believe that nourishing the body is enough to preserve life, yet if nourishing the body is truly insufficient to preserve life, then what in the world is worth doing? Though it may not suffice, it cannot be left undone—such efforts are inescapable. To escape the demands of the body, nothing surpasses fleeing the world. Abandon the world, and one is free of entanglements; free of entanglements, one attains perfect equilibrium; in perfect equilibrium, one exchanges life with that which is other—and in this exchange, one approaches the subtle. Why cling to affairs? Why discard life? Forsake affairs, and the body is not wearied; relinquish life, and the essence is not depleted. When the body is whole and the essence restored, one becomes one with Heaven. Heaven and Earth are the parents of the myriad things: united, they form a body; dispersed, they become a beginning. When form and essence are not depleted, this is called "the power to shift"; when essence is refined into yet greater essence, it returns to assist Heaven.

Master Liezi asked Guan Yin: "The perfected person walks underwater without obstruction, treads fire without heat, moves above the myriad things without fear. How does one attain this?" Guan Yin replied: "This is the preservation of pure qi, not the rank of cunning, skill, or daring. Sit, and I will tell you. All things possessing form, image, sound, or color are merely objects—how can objects be far from one another? Why should one strive to be first? It is but a matter of appearance. Thus, the creation of things halts at the formless and ceases where there is no transformation. One who attains this and exhausts it—how could things constrain them? They dwell within the measure of no excess, conceal themselves in the thread of no origin, roam through the beginning and end of the myriad things, unify their nature, nurture their qi, harmonize their virtue, and thereby commune with the source of things. Such a one’s Heavenly endowment remains whole, their spirit undivided—how could things intrude? The drunkard who falls from a carriage, though moving swiftly, does not die. His bones and joints are the same as others’, yet the harm he suffers differs—his spirit is whole. He does not know he is riding, does not know he has fallen; death and life, terror and dread do not enter his breast. Thus, he meets things without breaking. If one who is whole through wine can be thus, how much more so one who is whole through Heaven? The sage conceals himself in Heaven, and so nothing can harm him. A flexible vine does not snap in the wind; even one with a vengeful heart does not resent a tile blown by the gale—thus the world is in perfect balance. Therefore, there are no wars of aggression, no punishments of slaughter—this is the Way of such things. Do not open the Heavenly in man, but open the Heavenly of Heaven. Opening the Heavenly gives birth to virtue; opening the human gives birth to harm. Do not reject Heaven, do not neglect man—then the people will approach their true nature."

Confucius, traveling to Chu, emerged into a forest and saw a hunchbacked old man catching cicadas with a pole, as if picking them up by hand. Confucius said: "You are skillful! Is there a method to this?" The man replied: "I have a method."

In the fifth or sixth month, if I stack two pellets and they do not fall, I lose only a fraction; if I stack three and they do not fall, I lose but one in ten; if I stack five and they do not fall, it is as if the cicadas were already fastened to the pole. My body stands as still as a withered stump; my arms remain poised like the branches of a dead tree. Though heaven and earth are vast, and the myriad things are many, I am aware of nothing but the cicada’s wings. I do not turn aside, nor do I waver—how could I exchange the wings of the cicada for the ten thousand things? Thus I never fail to catch them.

Confucius turned to his disciples and said: "When the mind is undivided, it becomes one with the spirit—this is what the hunchbacked old man demonstrates."

Yan Yuan asked Confucius: "Once I crossed the Shangshen Abyss, where the ferryman handled the boat as if by magic. I asked him, 'Can one learn to steer a boat?' He said, 'Yes. Those skilled in swimming can master it quickly. As for divers, they can handle a boat the first time they see one.' I pressed him, but he would not explain. May I ask what he meant?"

Confucius replied: "Those skilled in swimming can master it quickly because they have forgotten the water. As for divers, who can handle a boat the first time they see one—it is because they see the abyss as if it were dry land, and a capsized boat as if it were merely a cart rolling backward. Though a thousand dangers lie before them, none can enter their minds. Wherever they go, they are at ease. One who stakes tiles may play skillfully; one who stakes hooks may grow cautious; one who stakes gold may become flustered. The skill is the same, but when one values the prize, the mind becomes burdened by externals. And when the mind is burdened by externals, the inner self grows clumsy."

Tian Kaizhi visited Duke Wei of Zhou. The duke said: "I have heard that Zhu Shen studied the art of life. You have associated with him—what have you learned?"

Tian Kaizhi replied: "I merely stand guard at the gate with a broom in hand—what could I have learned from the master?"

The duke said: "Do not decline, Master Tian. I wish to hear it."

Kaizhi said: "I heard the master say that one who nurtures life well is like a shepherd: he watches those who lag behind and urges them forward with his whip."

The duke asked: "What does that mean?"

Tian Kaizhi replied: "In Lu there was a man named Shan Bao, who lived in the mountains, drank from streams, and did not compete with others for profit. Though he lived to seventy, his complexion remained like that of a child. Yet he was unlucky enough to meet a hungry tiger, which killed and ate him. There was also Zhang Yi, who frequented the gates of the wealthy and spared no effort in his pursuits. Though he lived only to forty, he died of internal heat. Bao nurtured his inner self, but the tiger devoured his outer; Yi nurtured his outer self, but illness attacked his inner. These two men both failed to urge on those who lagged behind."

Confucius said: "Do not hide so deeply that you are unseen, nor expose yourself so openly that you are exposed—stand like a withered tree in the center. If you attain these three, your name will surely reach its peak. Those who fear the road may kill one in ten travelers, so fathers and sons, brothers and kin warn one another, and none dare venture out without a large escort—is this not wisdom? Yet what people truly fear are the mats they sit on and the food they eat. Not knowing to guard against these is the real mistake."

A sacrificial official, dressed in dark robes, approached the pen of pigs and said to one: "Why do you hate death? I will fatten you for three months, purify you for ten days, fast for three, spread white rushes beneath you, and place your shoulder and haunch upon the carved sacrificial platter—will you agree to this?" Speaking for the pig, he said: "Better to live on husks and糟 in the pen than to face such a fate." But speaking for himself, he said: "Yet if I could live with the honor of a high minister and die with my body laid upon fine mats and coffins, I would choose that." What differs between the pig’s choice and his own?

Duke Huan was hunting in the marshes with Guan Zhong driving his chariot when he saw a ghost. The duke grasped Guan Zhong’s hand and said: "What did you see, Uncle Zhong?"

Guan Zhong replied: "I saw nothing."

On their return, the duke fell ill and did not emerge for days. A Qi scholar named Li Ziao told him: "It is you who have harmed yourself—the ghost could not harm you. When the qi of anger and grief scatters and does not return, it causes deficiency. When it rises but does not descend, it makes one prone to rage; when it descends but does not rise, it makes one prone to forgetfulness. If it neither rises nor descends but lodges in the center of the body, at the heart, it causes illness."

Duke Huan asked: "Then are there ghosts?"

He replied: "There are. In the marshes dwell the *lü*; in the waters, the *ao* with its topknot."

Within the confines of a household’s inner walls, even the clamor of thunder dwells. In the northeast’s lower reaches, the *bei’e* and *guilong* leap; in the northwest’s lower reaches, the *zhiyang* resides. Waters harbor the *wangxiang*, mounds the *xing*, mountains the *kui*, wilds the *fanghuang*, and marshes the *weiyi*.

The duke asked: "What is the form of the *weiyi* like?"

Prince Huangzi replied: "The *weiyi* is as large as a wheel hub, as long as a chariot shaft, clad in purple robes with a crimson crown. As a creature, it abhors the sound of thunderous chariots—when it hears them, it clutches its head and stands upright. To see it is nearly a sign of hegemony."

Duke Huan burst into joyful laughter: "This is what *I* saw!" Thereupon, he straightened his robes and cap, sat with the prince, and before the day was out, he was unaware that his illness had departed.

Ji Yuzi raised fighting cocks for the king. After ten days, the king asked: "Are the cocks ready?"

"Not yet," he replied. "They are still hollow and reliant on their qi."

After another ten days, the king asked again. "Not yet," he said. "They still react to shadows."

After another ten days, the king asked once more. "Not yet," he replied. "They still glare fiercely and bristle with qi."

After yet another ten days, the king asked again. "Almost there," he said. "Though other cocks may crow, they show no change. Gazing at them, they seem like wooden cocks—their virtue is complete. No other cock dares respond; they turn and flee."

Confucius observed the Lüliang Falls, where the water plunged thirty *ren* and its froth stretched forty *li*, a place where even the *aoyu* and *aoman* could not swim. He saw a man swimming there and, assuming he was in distress and wished to die, sent his disciples along the current to rescue him. After several hundred paces, the man emerged, his hair unbound, singing as he strolled beneath the embankment.

Confucius followed and questioned him: "I took you for a ghost, but seeing you, you are a man. May I ask—is there a Dao for treading water?"

"No," he replied. "I have no Dao. I began with habit, grew with nature, and completed it with fate. I enter with the current and emerge with the flow, following the water’s Way without imposing my will. This is how I tread it."

Confucius asked: "What do you mean by beginning with habit, growing with nature, and completing with fate?"

He replied: "I was born on the hills and at ease on the hills—this is habit. I grew up in the water and am at ease in the water—this is nature. I do not know why I am thus, yet I am thus—this is fate."

Ziqing of Lu carved wood into a *ju* stand. When it was finished, those who saw it marveled as if it were the work of ghosts or spirits. The Lord of Lu saw it and asked: "What technique did you use to make this?"

He replied: "I am but a craftsman—what technique could I possess? Nevertheless, there is one thing. When I set out to make the *ju*, I never dared to waste my qi but always fasted to still my mind. After three days of fasting, I no longer dared to think of rewards, ranks, or emoluments. After five days, I no longer dared to think of praise or blame, skill or clumsiness. After seven days, I suddenly forgot I had limbs or a body. At that time, was there any court? My skill became concentrated, and external distractions dissolved. Only then did I enter the mountain forest to observe the natural qualities of the wood. When the perfect form appeared before me, I set to work—otherwise, I would stop. Thus, I matched heaven with heaven. Is this not why the vessel seems divine?"

Ye Ji demonstrated his charioteering before Duke Zhuang. His advances and retreats aligned with the plumb line, his left and right turns followed the compass. The duke thought even Wen could not surpass him and sent him to drive a hundred circuits before returning.

Yan Yu encountered him and, upon entering, said: "Ji’s horses are about to fail."

The duke remained silent and did not respond. Soon after, the chariot indeed failed and returned.

The duke asked: "How did you know?"

He replied: "His horses’ strength was exhausted, yet he still demanded more—hence I said they would fail."

A craftsman’s hammer and chisel follow the compass and square, his fingers transform with the material without mental calculation. Thus, his spirit-terrace is unified and unobstructed. Forget the feet, and the shoes fit; forget the waist, and the belt fits; forget right and wrong, and the mind is at ease. Without inner change or outer compliance, one meets events with perfect accord. To begin with ease and never be without it is to forget the ease of ease.

There was a man named Sun Xiu who, arriving at Bian Qingzi’s door, lamented: "In my village, I am not called unrefined; in times of peril, I am not called cowardly. Yet when I till the fields, I do not meet with good years; when I serve a lord, I do not meet with a favorable era. I am shunned by my village, driven from my district—what sin have I committed against heaven? Why must I suffer this fate?"

Bianzi replied: "Have you never heard of the Perfect Man’s conduct? He forgets his liver and gall, disregards his ears and eyes, wandering in a daze beyond the dust and grime, carefree in the realm of no affairs. This is called acting without reliance, growing without domination. Now you adorn your knowledge to startle the foolish, cultivate your person to expose others’ flaws, shining brightly as if carrying the sun and moon in your stride. You have preserved your body intact, complete with nine orifices, and have not halfway become deaf, blind, lame, or crippled to be counted among the masses. You are fortunate indeed—why do you have leisure to resent heaven? Be on your way."

Sunzi left, and Bianzi entered. As he sat, someone asked a question, and he looked up to the sky and sighed. A disciple inquired, "Master, why do you sigh?"

Bianzi replied, "Just now, when Rest came, I spoke to him of the Perfect Man’s virtue. I fear he was startled and may fall into confusion."

The disciple said, "Not so. If Sunzi’s words were right, then yours were wrong—yet wrong cannot confuse right. If Sunzi’s words were wrong, then yours were right—yet he came already confused. What fault is there in that?"

Bianzi said, "Not so. Long ago, a bird alighted on the outskirts of Lu. The Lord of Lu delighted in it and prepared a grand feast to entertain it, playing the *Nine Shao* to amuse it. The bird grew anxious, sorrowful, and dizzy, daring neither to eat nor drink. This is called nourishing a bird with what nourishes oneself. As for nourishing a bird with what nourishes a bird, one should let it roost in deep forests, drift upon rivers and lakes, and feed on creeping vines—then it would be at ease on level ground. Now, Rest is a man of simple sincerity and little learning. When I spoke to him of the Perfect Man’s virtue, it was like carrying a palace hall in a carriage and horses, or playing metal chimes with bells and drums. How could he not be startled?"

---

**The Southern Florescence Perfect Scripture, Scroll Four: "Outer Chapters – Mountain Trees, Chapter Twenty"**

Zhuangzi was walking in the mountains when he saw a great tree with luxuriant branches and leaves. A woodcutter stood beside it but did not fell it. Asking the reason, Zhuangzi was told, "It is of no use."

Zhuangzi said, "This tree, by being useless, has attained the full span of its natural years."

Leaving the mountains, Zhuangzi lodged at the home of an old friend. The friend, pleased, ordered a servant to kill a goose and cook it. The servant asked, "One can cackle, the other cannot—which should we kill?"

The host said, "Kill the one that cannot cackle."

The next day, a disciple asked Zhuangzi, "Yesterday, the tree in the mountains, by being useless, attained the full span of its natural years. Now, the master’s goose, by being useless, has died. Where will you, Master, take your stand?"

Zhuangzi laughed and said, "I will take my stand between usefulness and uselessness. Between usefulness and uselessness—it seems so, yet it is not, and thus one is not yet free from entanglement.
