But if one rides the Dao and floats upon virtue, it is otherwise: neither praised nor blamed, now a dragon, now a snake, transforming with the times without clinging to any one form; rising and falling, taking harmony as the measure, drifting upon the ancestor of the ten thousand things, treating things as things yet not being treated as a thing by them—how then could one be entangled? This is the method of Shennong and the Yellow Emperor.

"As for the nature of the ten thousand things and the transmission of human relations, it is not so. Union leads to separation, completion to destruction; sharpness is blunted, honor invites criticism, action incurs loss, worth invites schemes, worthlessness invites deceit. How can one be certain of anything? How lamentable!

"Take heed, disciples—only in the realm of Dao and virtue is there refuge."

---

Mishan Yiliao called upon the Lord of Lu, who wore a troubled expression. Mishan said, "My lord, why do you look troubled?"

The Lord of Lu replied, "I have studied the Way of the former kings and cultivated the undertakings of my predecessors. I honor the spirits and revere the worthy, practicing these with devotion, never resting for a moment—yet I cannot escape misfortune. This is why I am troubled."

Mishan said, "My lord’s means of avoiding misfortune are too shallow. The sleek fox and the patterned leopard dwell in mountain forests, crouch in rocky caves, and remain still; they move by night and rest by day, ever vigilant. Though hungry and thirsty, in straitened circumstances, they still roam the rivers and lakes in search of food, ever composed. Yet they cannot escape the calamity of nets and traps. What crime have they committed? Their pelts bring disaster upon them. Is not the state of Lu your pelt?

"I wish for you, my lord, to strip away your form and discard your pelt, cleanse your heart and rid yourself of desires, and wander in the wilds where no man dwells. In the southern lands, there is a city called the Land of Establishing Virtue. Its people are simple and unadorned, with few selfish desires. They know how to work but not to hoard, give without seeking repayment..."

The people know not where righteousness leads, nor whither propriety tends; they act recklessly and wander at will, yet tread the great square. Their life may be joyous, their death may be buried. I wish you, my lord, to abandon your state and cast off custom, to walk in mutual support with the Dao.

The lord said: "That Way is distant and perilous, with rivers and mountains in between. I have no boat or carriage—what then?"

Master Shinan replied: "Be not rigid in form, nor linger in place, and this shall be your carriage."

The lord said: "That Way is secluded and remote, with no one there. With whom shall I be neighbor? I have no provisions, no food—how can I reach it?"

Master Shinan said: "Lessen your lord’s expenditures, diminish your lord’s desires, and though you have no grain, you shall yet be sufficient. Cross the river and float upon the sea; gaze upon it and see no shore, advance ever further and know not where it ends. Those who see you off shall turn back from the shore, and you shall be far from them henceforth. Thus, he who possesses others is encumbered; he who is seen to possess by others is troubled. Therefore, Yao neither possessed others nor was seen to possess by others. I wish to rid you of encumbrance, to remove your troubles, and to wander alone with the Dao in the boundless land.

When a double-hulled boat crosses the river, an empty skiff drifts into it. Even a man of narrow mind would not grow angry. But if there were a man aboard, one would shout to him to open or close—once called and unheard, twice called and unheard, then a third call would surely be followed by harsh words. Before, he was not angry, but now he is; before, it was empty, but now it is full. If one can empty oneself and wander through the world, who could harm him?

Beigong She levied taxes for Duke Ling of Wei to cast bells. He built an altar outside the outer gate, and in three months, the bells were hung below the earthen platform. Prince Qingji saw and asked, "What method did you employ?"

She said: "In the moment of unity, I dared not impose any method. I have heard that once carved and polished, one returns to simplicity. Pity those without understanding, doubt those who hesitate. Gathered in boundless vastness, one sends off the departing and welcomes the arriving. Let those who come not be forbidden, let those who go not be stopped. Follow their strength and bend, adapt to their crookedness, and let them exhaust themselves. Thus, morning and evening, the levies were collected without so much as a hair being harmed—how much more so for those on the great thoroughfare?"

Confucius was besieged between Chen and Cai, going seven days without cooked food. Taigong Ren went to condole with him, saying: "You nearly died, did you not?"

Confucius said: "Yes."

"Do you hate death?"

"Yes."

Ren said: "Have you not spoken of the way of not dying? In the Eastern Sea there is a bird called *Yidai*. As a bird, it is scattered and disorderly, seemingly incapable. It flies only when led, perches only when pressed, advances not daring to be first, retreats not daring to be last. It does not dare to eat first but must take the leavings. Thus, it is not expelled from the flock, and others cannot harm it, so it escapes misfortune. Straight trees are felled first, sweet wells are drained first. You, I fear, adorn your knowledge to startle the foolish, cultivate yourself to expose the impure, and shine forth as if carrying the sun and moon in your march—how could you escape?"

Long ago, I heard from a man of great completion: 'He who boasts of himself achieves no merit; he who achieves merit is envied; he who achieves fame is diminished. Who can relinquish merit and fame and return to the common people? The Dao flows but does not dwell in glory; it acts but does not claim a place. Pure and constant, it is nearly mad. Shave off edges, discard influence, and do not seek merit or fame. Thus, one bears no blame from others, and others bear none from him. The perfected man is unknown—why do you delight in this?'

Confucius said: "Well said!" He took leave of his acquaintances, sent away his disciples, and fled to the great marsh. He wore a robe of coarse cloth and ate acorns and chestnuts. Entering among beasts, he did not disturb their herds; entering among birds, he did not disrupt their flocks. Birds and beasts did not hate him—how much less men?

Confucius asked Zisang Hu: "I was twice banished from Lu, had a tree felled upon me in Song, was made a fugitive in Wei, was destitute between Shang and Zhou, and besieged between Chen and Cai. I have suffered these many misfortunes, my close associates have grown more distant, and my disciples and friends have scattered—why is this?"

Zisang Hu said: "Have you not heard of the flight of the man of Jia? Lin Hui abandoned a jade disc worth a thousand gold pieces and carried a baby on his back as he fled. Someone asked: 'Was it for the money? The baby’s value in coin is little. Was it for the burden? The baby’s burden is great. Why abandon a jade disc worth a thousand gold pieces and carry a baby on your back?' Lin Hui said: 'That was bound by profit; this is bound by Heaven. Those bound by profit abandon one another in straitened circumstances, disaster, and harm. Those bound by Heaven gather together in straitened circumstances, disaster, and harm. The difference between gathering and abandoning is vast. Moreover, the friendship of the noble is as bland as water; the friendship of the petty is as sweet as wine. The noble are bland yet intimate; the petty are sweet yet estranged. Those who come together without cause will part without cause.'

Confucius said: "I respectfully receive your instruction." He walked slowly and leisurely on his return, abandoned his studies, and discarded his books. His disciples no longer bowed before him, yet their affection grew deeper.

Another day, Zisang Hu said: "When Shun was about to die, he sincerely warned Yu: 'Beware! In form, nothing is better than following; in feeling, nothing is better than sincerity. Following, one does not stray; sincere, one does not toil. Not straying, not toiling, one does not seek ornament to adorn form. Not seeking ornament to adorn form, one certainly does not depend on things.'"

Zhuangzi wore a patched robe of coarse cloth, fastened his shoes with hemp, and passed by the King of Wei. The king said: "Why is the master so weary?"

Zhuangzi said: "It is poverty, not weariness. A scholar with the Dao and virtue who cannot practice them is weary. Wearing tattered clothes and threadbare shoes is poverty, not weariness. This is what is called not encountering the times. Have you alone not seen the leaping monkeys?"

When the gibbon obtains towering catalpa or camphor trees, it grasps the vines and swings through their branches, reigning supreme in its domain—even Yi or Feng Meng could not challenge its mastery. Yet when it finds itself among brambles, thorns, and tangled roots, it moves with caution, glancing sideways, trembling and quaking. Not that its sinews and bones have suddenly stiffened or lost their suppleness, but the terrain is unfavorable, insufficient for it to display its full ability.

Now, to dwell amid benighted rulers and chaotic ministers and yet hope to avoid weariness—how could this be possible? This is like the fate of Bi Gan, whose heart was carved out as proof.

Confucius was stranded between Chen and Cai, going seven days without cooked food. Leaning left against a withered tree, striking its dry branches with his right hand, he sang the airs of the House of Shen. The instruments were present, but the measures were not; the sounds were there, but the modes of gong and jue were absent. Yet the resonance of wood and voice plowed deep, striking true in the hearts of men. Yan Hui stood with hands folded, turning his gaze to observe.

Fearing that Hui might expand his own concerns into grand ambitions or turn self-love into sorrow, Confucius said: "Hui, to bear the decrements of Heaven is easy; to bear the benefits of men is hard. There is no beginning that is not an end—man and Heaven are one. Who, then, is the singer of this song today?"

Yan Hui replied: "May I ask why bearing the decrements of Heaven is easy?"

Confucius said: "Hunger and thirst, cold and heat, the blockage of one’s path—these are the workings of Heaven and Earth, the outpourings of the movement of things. To speak of going along with them is to say one moves in tandem with them. As a minister, one does not dare to abandon this way. If even the way of serving as a minister is thus, how much more so the way of awaiting Heaven’s decree?"

"What, then, is meant by bearing the benefits of men being hard?"

Confucius said: "At the outset, one’s path opens in four directions, ranks and emoluments arrive without end, and all that benefits things is not one’s own—my fate lies beyond me. The noble man does not steal; the worthy man does not pilfer. If I were to take such things, what would I be? Thus it is said: No bird is wiser than the magpie. Where its eyes ought not to linger, it does not even glance. Though the fruit falls before it, it abandons it and flees. It fears men and yet nests among them—merely to preserve its nest and young. What is meant by ‘no beginning that is not an end’?"

Confucius said: "When the myriad things transform and one does not know who succeeds them, how can one know their end? How can one know their beginning? Simply align oneself and await it, that is all."

What is meant by "man and Heaven as one"?

Confucius said: "There is man—this is Heaven; there is Heaven—this too is Heaven. That man cannot be Heaven is his nature. The sage rests in this and drifts to his end."

Zhuang Zhou was wandering within the enclosure of Carved Mound when he saw an unusual magpie coming from the south. Its wings spanned seven feet, its eyes measured an inch across. It brushed against Zhou’s forehead and alighted in the chestnut grove. Zhuang Zhou said: "What bird is this? Its wings are vast yet it does not fly, its eyes are large yet it does not see." He hitched up his robe, strode forward, took up his sling, and waited. He saw a cicada, having just found a fine shade, forget its own body; a mantis, seizing the opportunity, raised its forelegs to strike it, seeing only its gain and forgetting its form; the strange magpie, drawn by this, saw only the advantage and forgot its true nature. Zhuang Zhou shuddered and said: "Ah! Things indeed entangle one another—two kinds summon each other." He cast aside his sling and turned to flee, but the warden of the grove pursued and berated him. Zhuang Zhou returned home and for three months did not appear in the courtyard.

Lan Ju followed and asked him: "Master, what has been the matter? For some time now, you have not come to the courtyard."

Zhuang Zhou said: "I guarded my form and forgot my body. I gazed upon muddy water and was lost in the clear abyss. Moreover, I have heard from my master: 'Enter their customs, follow their customs.' Today, I wandered in Carved Mound and forgot my body; the strange magpie struck my forehead. I roamed in the chestnut grove and forgot my true nature; the warden of the grove took me for a thief. That is why I have not come to the courtyard."

Yangzi traveled to Song and lodged at an inn. The innkeeper had two concubines—one beautiful, the other ugly. The ugly one was honored, the beautiful one despised. Yangzi asked the reason, and the innkeeper’s servant replied: "The beautiful one is beautiful in her own eyes, but I do not see her beauty. The ugly one is ugly in her own eyes, but I do not see her ugliness."

Yangzi said: "Disciples, take note: act worthily and abandon the conduct of self-regard. Wherever you go, will you not be loved?"

---

*Southern Florescence Perfected Scripture*, Scroll Four, Outer Chapters: "Tian Zifang," Twenty-First

Tian Zifang was in attendance, sitting beside the Lord of Wei. He repeatedly praised Xi Gong. The Lord said: "Is Xi Gong your teacher?"

Zifang said: "No. He is a man of my village. He speaks of the Dao often and aptly, so I praise him."

The Lord said: "Then do you have no teacher?"

Zifang said: "I do."

The Lord said: "Who is your teacher?"

Zifang said: "Shu Guo Shunzi."

The Lord said: "Then why have you never spoken of him?"

Zifang said: "His person is true: his human form holds Heaven’s emptiness, he is pure and preserves the real, clear yet accommodating of things. When things lack the Dao, he rectifies his bearing to awaken them, dissolving their intentions. How could I be worthy to praise him?"

Zifang departed. The Lord sat in a daze for the rest of the day without speaking. He summoned his ministers who stood before him and said: "How distant is the gentleman of perfect virtue! At first, I took the words of sagely wisdom and the conduct of benevolence and righteousness to be the ultimate. But now that I have heard of Zifang’s teacher, my form is unstrung and I do not wish to move, my mouth is sealed and I do not wish to speak. What I have learned is but a clay figurine—Wei has truly been a burden to me."

---

Wen Bo Xuezi traveled to Qi and stayed in Lu. A man of Lu requested to see him. Wen Bo Xuezi said: "No. I have heard that the gentlemen of the Central States are enlightened in ritual and righteousness but coarse in understanding the human heart. I do not wish to see them."

When he returned to Qi and stayed again in Lu, the same man requested to see him. Wen Bo Xuezi said: "Before, he sought to see me; now, he seeks to see me again. He must have something to stir me." He went out to meet the guest, then returned and sighed. The next day, he met the guest again and returned, sighing once more.

His servant said: "Each time you meet this guest, you return and sigh. Why?"

He said: "I have already told you: the people of the Central States are enlightened in ritual and righteousness but coarse in understanding the human heart. The one who saw me before advanced and retreated as if bound by compass and square, conducted himself as if a dragon or a tiger. He admonished me like a son, guided me like a father. That is why I sighed."

Confucius saw him and did not speak. Zilu said: "You have long wished to see Wen Bo Xuezi. Now that you have seen him, why do you not speak?"

Confucius said: "With such a man, the Dao is present the moment one’s eyes meet. There is no room for words."

---

Yan Yuan asked Confucius: "When you walk, I walk; when you hasten, I hasten; when you run, I run. But when you race ahead, leaving all dust behind, I can only stare blankly from behind."

Confucius said: "Hui, what do you mean?"

Yan Yuan said: "When you walk, I walk; when you speak, I speak; when you hasten, I hasten; when you debate, I debate; when you run, I run; when you speak of the Dao, I speak of the Dao. Yet when you race ahead, leaving all dust behind, and I can only stare blankly from behind—you do not speak yet are trusted, do not compare yet are all-encompassing, have no vessel yet the people tread before you, and I do not know why."

Confucius said: "Alas! How can this not be examined? There is no greater sorrow than the death of the heart, and the death of the person is secondary to it. The sun rises in the east and sets in the west. All things align with it: those with eyes and feet depend on it to succeed. When it rises, they exist; when it sets, they perish. All things are thus: some depend on it to die, some depend on it to live. Once I received my completed form, I did not transform but awaited its end, moving in accord with things, day and night without pause, yet not knowing where it would lead—so naturally my form was completed. Knowing fate, I cannot plan for what lies ahead. I have followed you all my life, grasped your arm for an instant, and lost it—how can this not be sorrowful?"

You are likely clinging to what I cling to. That has already vanished, yet you seek it as if it still exists—this is like seeking a horse in an empty market. I have so thoroughly forgotten you that you have become nothing to me, and you have so thoroughly forgotten me that I have become nothing to you. Yet why should you grieve? Though you may forget the old me, there remains something in me that cannot be forgotten.

Confucius visited Lao Dan, who had just washed his hair and was letting it dry, unbound. He stood there so still he seemed not human. Confucius withdrew and waited. After a while, he approached and said: "Did my eyes deceive me, or was it truly so? Just now, Master, your form was withered like a dead tree, as if you had abandoned all things and stood apart from humanity."

Lao Dan said: "I was letting my mind wander in the origin of things."

Confucius asked: "What do you mean?"

Lao Dan replied: "The heart is confounded and cannot know; the mouth is shut and cannot speak. Let me try to describe it for you. The utmost yin is solemn and still; the utmost yang is blazing and bright. Solemn stillness arises from heaven; blazing brightness issues from earth. The two intermingle and harmonize, and things are born. Some pattern governs this, yet no form is seen. They wax and wane, fill and empty, now dark, now bright, changing day by day, month by month, acting without cease, yet no trace of their work is seen. Life has its source from which it sprouts; death has its place to which it returns. Beginning and end revolve without end, yet none know where it will cease. If not this, then what else could be their origin?"

Confucius said: "May I ask about wandering in this?"

Lao Dan said: "To attain this is the utmost beauty and joy. To attain the utmost beauty and wander in the utmost joy—this is called the Perfected Person."

Confucius said: "I would like to hear the method."

Lao Dan said: "Herbivorous beasts do not fret over changing marshes; aquatic creatures do not fret over changing waters. They undergo few changes yet do not lose their great constancy. Joy and anger, sorrow and happiness do not enter the breast. The world is where the myriad things are one. If you attain this oneness and become the same as it, then your four limbs and hundred parts will be but dust and dirt, and death and life, end and beginning will be like day and night—nothing can disturb them, much less the distinctions of gain and loss, fortune and misfortune. Those who cast off such concerns are like those who discard mud and mire, knowing the body is more precious than such things. What is precious resides in me and is not lost in change. Moreover, the myriad transformations have never had an end—what could trouble the heart? Those who have become one with the Dao are freed from this."

Confucius said: "Master, your virtue matches heaven and earth, yet you still borrow the most profound words to cultivate your heart. Among the noble men of old, who could escape this?"

Lao Dan said: "Not so. Water, when clear, requires no effort to be naturally so. The Perfected Person, in regard to virtue, does not cultivate it, yet things cannot depart from it—just as heaven is naturally high, earth is naturally thick, and the sun and moon are naturally bright. What cultivation is needed?"

Confucius left and told Yan Hui: "In regard to the Dao, I am like a gnat in vinegar. Were it not for the Master lifting the cover from my eyes, I would not have known the great completeness of heaven and earth."

Zhuangzi visited Duke Ai of Lu. The Duke said: "There are many Confucians in Lu, but few who follow your methods."

Zhuangzi said: "There are few Confucians in Lu."

The Duke said: "The whole state of Lu wears Confucian robes—how can you say there are few?"

Zhuangzi said: "I have heard that Confucians who wear round caps know the seasons of heaven; those who wear square shoes know the forms of earth; and those who wear sashes with pendants decide matters as they arise. A noble man who possesses the Dao may not necessarily wear such attire, and those who wear such attire may not necessarily know the Dao."

Duke Ai stubbornly disagreed. "Why not issue a decree throughout the state: 'Those who possess not the Dao yet don this attire shall be put to death'?" For five days, the Duke proclaimed this decree, and no one in Lu dared wear Confucian robes—save for one man who stood at the palace gate in full Confucian garb.

The Duke summoned him and questioned him on state affairs. Though the questions twisted and turned a thousand ways, his answers never faltered.

Zhuangzi said: "With all of Lu, there is but one true Confucian. Can this be called many?"

Baili Xi cared nothing for rank or salary, so when he fed cattle, the cattle grew fat. This made Duke Mu of Qin forget his lowly status and entrust him with governance. The Lords of Yu cared nothing for life or death, and thus could move others.

The Lord of Song Yuan was about to commission paintings. All the court scribes arrived, bowed in reception, and stood in attendance. Half had already begun licking their brushes and mixing ink when one scribe arrived late. Unhurried, he did not rush forward; having bowed, he did not stand stiffly but went straight to his quarters. The Lord sent someone to observe him and found him sitting with his robes undone, legs sprawled in ease.

The Lord said: "This is the true painter."

King Wen was observing at Zang when he saw an old man fishing. Yet his fishing was no fishing—he did not hold the rod as one who fishes, but fished as one always does. King Wen wished to raise him to office and entrust him with governance, but feared his great ministers and elders would not accept it. Yet to abandon the idea and leave him be would be to bear the weight of the people’s suffering without a lord’s protection.

At dawn, he gathered his ministers and said: "Last night, I dreamed of a worthy man—dark-complexioned and bearded, riding a dappled horse with one red hoof. He commanded: 'Entrust your governance to the old man of Zang, and perhaps the people will find relief.'"

The ministers, startled, said: "This was your late father, the former king."

King Wen said: "Then let us divine it."

The ministers said: "This is the command of our former lord; let the king have no other thought. Why divine further?" Thereupon they welcomed the old man of Zang and entrusted him with governance. No laws were altered, no decrees issued beyond the usual. After three years, King Wen surveyed the state and found that the ranks of scholars had dispersed, their factions dissolved; officials no longer cultivated virtue; and measures of grain dared not enter from the four borders. The dispersal of scholars and factions meant unity of purpose; officials’ failure to cultivate virtue meant shared endeavor; the refusal of grain tribute from the borders meant the lords harbored no divided loyalties.

King Wen then made him Grand Preceptor, facing north to inquire: "Can this governance extend to all under Heaven?" The old man of Zang gave no response, his words drifting away, and by nightfall he had fled, never to be heard of again.

Yan Yuan asked Confucius: "Was King Wen still deluded? Why then did he dream?" Confucius said: "Hush, say no more. King Wen fulfilled it to the utmost—what need is there for criticism? He merely followed the moment’s course."

Lie Yukou was shooting for Bohun Wuren, drawing the bow to its fullest, balancing a cup of water on his elbow. When he loosed the arrow, one shaft followed another in perfect succession. At that moment, he was like a wooden figure. Bohun Wuren said: "This is the shooting of one who shoots, not the shooting of one who does not shoot. If I were to climb a high mountain with you, tread on perilous rocks, and stand at the edge of a hundred-fathom abyss, could you shoot then?" Thereupon Wuren ascended the mountain, trod the perilous rocks, stood at the brink of the abyss, his back to the edge, his feet half hanging over, then beckoned Lie Yukou forward. Lie Yukou prostrated himself, sweat streaming to his heels. Bohun Wuren said: "The perfected man ascends to the azure sky above, plunges into the Yellow Springs below, roams to the eight limits, yet his spirit and qi remain unchanged. Now you tremble with fear in your eyes—you are in peril indeed!"

Jian Wu asked Sunshu Ao: "You served three times as Prime Minister without pride, and resigned three times without sorrow. At first I doubted you, but now I see your nostrils flare with ease—what is the secret of your heart?" Sunshu Ao said: "How do

Thus it is said: the world is but a single qi. Therefore the sage values unity.

Zhi said to the Yellow Emperor: "I asked Wuwei Wei, but Wuwei Wei did not respond to me." Not that he would not respond to me—he did not know how to respond to me. "I asked Kuang Qu, and Kuang Qu wished to tell me but did not tell me." Not that he would not tell me—he wished to tell me but forgot. "Now I ask you, and you know—why then are you not close to the truth?"

The Yellow Emperor said: "He is truly so because he does not know; this one seems so because he has forgotten. You and I will never be close to the truth because we know."

Kuang Qu heard this and took the Yellow Emperor to be one who knew the words.

Heaven and earth possess great beauty yet do not speak. The four seasons follow clear patterns yet do not debate. The myriad things have their inherent principles yet do not explain. The sage traces the beauty of heaven and earth and comprehends the principles of the myriad things. Thus the perfected man does nothing, the great sage takes no action—this is called observing heaven and earth.

Now that numinous brightness and utmost refinement, along with the hundred transformations—whether things die or live, take shape or become round—none know their root. Yet all things have existed since ancient times, secure in themselves. The Six Directions are vast, yet they do not depart from its bounds; the tip of an autumn hair is minute, yet it depends on this to exist. In the world, nothing does not sink or float, yet nothing remains unchanged throughout life. Yin and yang and the four seasons move in their courses, each keeping to its proper order. Vast and indistinct, it seems to vanish yet remains; flowing and formless, it is spirit. The myriad things are nurtured by it yet do not know. This is called the root and foundation—by it one may observe heaven.

Nie Que asked Dao of Bei Yi. Bei Yi said: "Straighten your form, unify your gaze, and the harmony of heaven will arrive. Gather in your knowledge, unify your measures, and spirit will come to dwell. Virtue will adorn you, the Dao will be your abode. Your eyes will be bright as a newborn calf’s, seeking nothing of the past."

Before the words were finished, Nie Que fell asleep. Bei Yi rejoiced greatly and walked away singing, saying: "His form like withered bones, his heart like dead ashes, truly he knows the real. Not clinging to the past, vague and obscure, without mind—none can scheme with him. What manner of man is this?"

Shun asked Cheng: "Can the Dao be possessed?" Cheng said: "Your body is not your own—how can you possess the Dao?" Shun said: "If my body is not my own, then whose is it?" Cheng said: "It is heaven and earth’s entrusted form. Life is not your own—it is heaven and earth’s entrusted harmony. Nature and destiny are not your own—they are heaven and earth’s entrusted compliance. Children and grandchildren are not your own—they are heaven and earth’s entrusted shedding. Thus when you walk, you do not know where you are going; when you dwell, you do not know what you are holding; when you eat, you do not know the taste. It is the surging yang qi of heaven and earth—how could you possess it?"

Confucius asked Lao Cong: "Today I have leisure—may I dare ask about the supreme Dao?" Lao Cong said: "Purify yourself with fasting, relax your mind and wash away its impurities, cleanse your spirit and strike down your knowledge. The Dao—how recondite and hard to speak of! I shall tell you its broad outlines."

The radiant emerges from the obscure, the ordered from the formless; spirit arises from the Dao, and form originates from essence. All things beget one another through form—thus creatures with nine orifices are born from the womb, and those with eight from the egg. Their coming knows no nearness, their going no boundary; no gate, no chamber, yet they extend boundlessly in all four directions. Those who roam here have limbs of strength, thoughts that penetrate, ears and eyes keen—yet their minds exert no labor, and their responses to things know no fixed pattern. Heaven cannot but be high, earth cannot but be vast, sun and moon cannot but move, and the myriad things cannot but flourish—such is the Dao.

Moreover, vast learning does not necessarily bring knowledge, nor eloquence wisdom; the sage has already settled this. As for that which, when augmented, does not increase, and when diminished, does not decrease—this is what the sage preserves. Profound as the sea, towering in its endless return to the beginning, it measures and sustains the ten thousand things without exhaustion. Thus the Dao of the noble person lies beyond such distinctions. All things turn to it for sustenance yet are never depleted—such is the Dao.

There is a being within the central realm, neither yin nor yang, dwelling between heaven and earth, merely human for a time, yet destined to return to its origin. Viewed from its root, life is but the dark fermentation of things. Though lifespans vary, how great is the difference? A mere moment’s discourse. Why then debate the right and wrong of Yao and Jie? The fruit of the fern has its pattern, and human relations, though complex, follow their order. The sage encounters them without resistance, passes them without clinging. To harmonize and respond is virtue; to coincide and respond is the Dao. This is what empowers emperors and gives rise to kings.

Human life between heaven and earth is like a white colt passing a gap—suddenly, it is gone. Bursting forth, all things emerge; gently dispersing, all things return. Having transformed and lived, they transform again and die. Living creatures mourn them, humankind grieves. Loosening heaven’s bonds, unraveling its threads, in swirling confusion they drift—soul and spirit depart, and the body follows, returning to the great homecoming. The formless taking form, form dissolving into the formless—this all people know, yet it is not the concern of those who attain the Dao. This is what the common crowd debates. Those who attain do not debate; those who debate do not attain. Clear insight finds no value; eloquence is not as good as silence. The Dao cannot be heard; hearing it is not as good as blocking it—this is called the great attainment.

Dongguo Zi asked Zhuangzi: "Where does the so-called Dao reside?"
Zhuangzi said: "It is nowhere absent."
Dongguo Zi said: "You must specify."
Zhuangzi said: "It is in the ants."
Dongguo Zi said: "Why so low?"
Zhuangzi said: "It is in the millet stalks."
Dongguo Zi said: "Why even lower?"
Zhuangzi said: "It is in the tiles and shards."
Dongguo Zi said: "Why still lower?"
Zhuangzi said: "It is in excrement and urine."
Dongguo Zi did not respond.
Zhuangzi said: "Your question fundamentally misses the essence. When Zheng Huo asked the market inspector about the fat content of a pig, the lower the part, the clearer the answer. You must not insist on a fixed place—nothing escapes the Dao. The supreme Dao is like this, and so too is great speech. *Zhou*, *bian*, and *xian*—these three are different names for the same reality, pointing to the one.

Once, wandering together in the Palace of Nothing-Whatsoever, we merged in discussion without end. Once, dwelling together in non-action—serene and still, vast and clear, harmonious and at ease. My will was empty, and though I went forth, I knew not where I arrived; though I departed and returned, I knew not where I stopped. I had already come and gone yet knew not where it would end. Wandering in the boundless, great knowledge enters yet knows no limit. That which gives form to things is not bounded by things, yet things have boundaries—what we call the boundaries of things are the boundaries of non-boundary, the boundary of the unbounded.

We speak of fullness and emptiness, decline and decay, yet that which is full and empty is not full and empty; that which declines and decays is not decline and decay. That which is root and branch is not root and branch; that which accumulates and disperses is not accumulation and dispersal."

Kuang He Gan and Shen Nong studied together under Old Dragon Ji. One day, Shen Nong was dozing with the door closed when Kuang He Gan burst in at noon, shouting: "Old Dragon is dead!"
Shen Nong, leaning on his staff, rose with a start, then tossed it aside and laughed, saying: "Heaven knew my stubbornness and arrogance, so it cast me aside and let him die. Alas, master! You have perished without leaving me your wild words."
Hui Gang Diao heard of this and said: "Those who embody the Dao are what the noble persons of the world strive for. Yet even in the Dao, he had not attained so much as a hair’s tip out of ten thousand parts, yet still knew to conceal his wild words before dying—how much more so for those who truly embody the Dao!"

To see it is to see no form; to hear it is to hear no sound. In human discourse, it is called the obscure—this is how the Dao is discussed, yet it is not the Dao.

Then Tai Qing asked Wu Qiong: "Do you know the Dao?"
Wu Qiong said: "I do not know."
He asked Wu Wei, who said: "I know the Dao."
Tai Qing said: "Does your knowledge of the Dao have a pattern?"
Wu Wei said: "It does."
Tai Qing said: "What is this pattern?"
Wu Wei said: "I know the Dao can be noble or base, can be gathered or dispersed—this is the pattern by which I know the Dao."
Tai Qing then asked Wu Shi: "If so, then is Wu Qiong’s not-knowing or Wu Wei’s knowing correct, and which is mistaken?"
Wu Shi said: "Not-knowing is profound; knowing is shallow. Not-knowing is internal; knowing is external."
Thereupon Tai Qing sighed and said: "Is not-knowing then knowing? Is knowing then not-knowing? Who knows the knowing of not-knowing?"
Wu Shi said: "The Dao cannot be heard; to hear it is not it."

The Dao cannot be seen; to see it is not it.
The Dao cannot be spoken; to speak it is not it.
Do you know that the shapeless cannot be shaped?

The Dao should not be named.

Wu Shi said: "One who answers when asked about the Dao does not know the Dao. Even the one who asks about the Dao has not heard of the Dao. The Dao admits no questions, and questions admit no answers. To question what admits no question is to ask into exhaustion; to answer what admits no answer is to lack inwardness. One who responds to exhausting questions with no inwardness—such a one outwardly does not observe the cosmos, inwardly does not know the Great Beginning. Thus they do not pass beyond Kunlun, nor wander in the Great Void."

Guangyao asked Wu You: "Does the Master exist? Or does he not exist?"
Guangyao could not frame his question, so he gazed intently at Wu You’s appearance—empty and still, vacant and silent. He looked at him all day and saw nothing; he listened and heard nothing; he reached out and grasped nothing.

Guangyao said: "This is perfection! Who could attain such a state? I can attain having and non-being, yet I have not attained non-being of non-being. How could one reach this state of nothingness?"

The master smith of Great Horse who forged hooks was eighty years old, yet he never lost the finest hair’s edge.

Great Horse asked: "Are you skillful, or do you possess the Dao?"
He replied: "I have my discipline. At twenty, I took delight in forging hooks. I saw nothing in other things—nothing but hooks did I examine. What I employed was the use of what was not employed, thus prolonging its use. How much more so for that which has no part unused? What thing does not draw upon this?"

Ran Qiu asked Confucius: "Was there anything knowable before Heaven and Earth existed?"
Confucius said: "There was. The past was like the present."
Ran Qiu, perplexed by the answer, withdrew. The next day, he returned and said: "Yesterday, I asked whether anything was knowable before Heaven and Earth existed. You said, 'There was. The past was like the present.' Yesterday, I was enlightened; today, I am benighted. May I ask what this means?"
Confucius said: "Yesterday’s enlightenment came because the spirit received it first. Today’s benightedness comes because you now seek it with what is not spirit. There is no past, no present, no beginning, no end. Could there be descendants before there were descendants? It is possible."
Ran Qiu did not respond.
Confucius said: "Enough. There is no answer. Do not take life to give death, nor take death to give life. Do life and death depend on something? All share a single body. Is there something that existed before Heaven and Earth? That which gives rise to things is not itself a thing. Things emerge, yet nothing precedes them—just as there are things. Just as there are things, there is no end. Does the sage’s love for others ever end? It is simply drawn from this."

Yan Yuan asked Confucius: "I once heard you say, 'Do not send off, do not welcome.' May I ask how one wanders?"
Confucius said: "The ancients outwardly transformed while inwardly remaining unchanged. People today inwardly transform while outwardly remaining unchanged. One who transforms with things is one with what does not transform. Where transformation and non-transformation rest, where they blend without conflict—there, one must be without excess. The parks of Xi Wei, the gardens of the Yellow Emperor, the palaces of Yu, the halls of Tang and Wu—these were where the noble-minded, the masters of Ru and Mo, clashed with their 'rights' and 'wrongs.' How much more so with people today? The sage dwells among things without harming them. One who does not harm things—things cannot harm them. Only one who harms nothing can send off and welcome others. Mountains and forests, hills and plains—do they not make me joyful and glad? Yet before the joy is complete, sorrow follows. Joy and sorrow come—I cannot resist them; they depart—I cannot stop them. Alas! People of the world are but lodgers in the realm of things. They know what they encounter but not what they do not; they know what they can do but not what they cannot. To be without knowledge, without ability—this is something no one can avoid. Yet to strive to avoid what no one can avoid—is this not also lamentable? The ultimate word leaves words behind; the ultimate act leaves acts behind. To equal the knowledge of all that is known—this is shallow indeed."

*The Southern Florescence Perfected Scripture*, Scroll Four: Miscellaneous Chapters, "Gengsang Chu" Twenty-Third

Among Lao Dan’s disciples was one named Gengsang Chu, who had partially grasped the Master’s Dao. He went north to dwell on Mount Weilü. Those among his retainers who were clever and knowing departed; those among his concubines who were soft and benevolent kept their distance. Only the hunchbacked and the idle remained to serve him. After three years, Weilü flourished.

The people of Weilü said to one another: "When Master Gengsang first arrived, we were struck by his difference. Now, when we reckon our days, we find them lacking; when we reckon our years, we find them abundant. He must be a sage. Why do we not together offer him sacrifices as to an ancestral spirit, and worship him as we do the gods of soil and grain?"

Gengsang Chu heard this and, facing south, remained unsettled.
His disciples found this strange.
Gengsang Chu said: "Disciples, why do you find me strange? When spring’s qi arises, the hundred grasses grow; when autumn’s season arrives, the myriad treasures ripen. Do spring and autumn not act without purpose? The Dao of Heaven has already unfolded. I have heard that the perfected person dwells like a corpse in a walled hut, while the common people run wild, not knowing where to turn. Now, with the petty folk of Weilü whispering among themselves, wishing to place me among the worthy with sacrificial vessels—am I a man to be measured thus? This is why I remain unsettled by the words of Lao Dan."
His disciples said: "Not so."

For in a ditch of ordinary width, the great fish has no room to turn its body, and the minnows and gudgeons become its rulers; on a hillock of but a few paces, the mighty beast has no space to stretch its limbs, and the foxes and weasels become its omens of good fortune. Moreover, to honor the worthy and grant office to the capable, to put goodness and benefit first—this has been so since the time of Yao and Shun, how much more so for the folk of Weilü! Master, you should heed this."

Gengsang Chu said: "Come here, young ones. The beast that fills a carriage, once parted from the mountain and encased in armor, cannot escape the snares and nets; the fish that swallows a boat, once beached and deprived of water, may be vexed by ants. Thus birds and beasts do not weary of the heights, and fish and turtles do not weary of the depths. The man who preserves his form and life hides himself, and does not weary of the profound and distant—this alone suffices. As for those two, how could they be worthy of praise? In their disputations, they would recklessly hack at walls to plant mugwort and wormwood. Combing their hair to a fine sheen, counting grains of rice to cook—how could such petty acts save the age? When the worthy are raised up, the people contend; when cunning is employed, the people steal. These few things are not enough to enrich the people. The people are ever eager for gain: sons kill fathers, ministers kill rulers, in broad daylight they turn to robbery, at midday they bore through walls. I tell you, the root of great disorder must arise in the age of Yao and Shun, and its branches will endure for a thousand generations. A thousand generations hence, there will surely be men who devour one another."

Nanrong Chu straightened his robes, sat upright, and said: "At my age, I am already advanced in years—how can I entrust my work and attain this cultivation?"

Gengsang Chu said: "Preserve your form, embrace your life, and do not let your thoughts and cares run wild. If you can do this for two years, you may reach this state."

Nanrong Chu said: "The eye and the form—I do not see them as different, yet the blind cannot see themselves; the ear and the form—I do not see them as different, yet the deaf cannot hear themselves; the heart and the form—I do not see them as different, yet the mad cannot grasp themselves. The form and the form are set apart—could it be that things intervene? Do they seek one another yet fail to find? Now you tell me: preserve your form, embrace your life, do not let your thoughts and cares run wild. I have striven to hear the Dao and let it reach my ears."

Gengsang Chu said: "My words are exhausted. The wasp of the day cannot transform the chrysalis of the night; the chicken of Yue cannot hatch the egg of the swan—only the chicken of Lu can do so. Chickens and chickens—their virtue is not different, yet some can and some cannot, for their capacities are inherently great or small. Now my capacity is small and insufficient to transform you. Why do you not go south to see Laozi?"

Nanrong Chu prepared provisions, and after seven days and seven nights arrived at Laozi’s abode.

Laozi said: "Have you come from Chu?"

Nanrong Chu said: "Yes."

Laozi said: "Why have you come with so many people?"

Nanrong Chu, startled, looked behind him.

Laozi said: "Do you not understand what I mean?"

Nanrong Chu bowed his head in shame, then looked up and sighed: "Now I have forgotten my answer and so lost my question."

Laozi said: "What do you mean?"

Nanrong Chu said: "If I am ignorant, people call me a fool. If I am wise, it only brings sorrow to my body. Without benevolence, I harm others; with benevolence, I bring sorrow upon myself. Without righteousness, I injure others; with righteousness, I bring sorrow upon myself. How can I escape this? These three sayings are my afflictions. I wish to rely on you to resolve them."

Laozi said: "A moment ago, I saw between your brows, and thereby understood you. Now you speak again and confirm it. You are as one who has lost his parents, holding up a pole to seek them in the sea. You are lost indeed, bewildered! You wish to return to your nature and disposition yet have no way in—how pitiable!"

Nanrong Chu begged to enter and receive instruction. He summoned what he loved and cast aside what he despised. After ten days of self-torment, he again saw Laozi.

Laozi said: "You have cleansed yourself—how dense and troubled you still are! Yet within, you seethe as if still harboring resentment. He who is bound without cannot be restrained by force, for he will grow stronger within; he who is constrained within cannot be seized by deception, for he will lash out without. He who is bound both without and within cannot be upheld by the Dao and virtue—how much less one who abandons the Dao and acts on his own?"

Nanrong Chu said: "When a villager is ill, and the villagers ask after him, if the sick man can describe his illness, then though he suffers, he is not yet truly ill. As for my hearing of the great Dao, it is like taking medicine that only worsens the disease. I wish only to hear the classic of preserving life."

Laozi said: "The classic of preserving life—can you embrace the One? Can you not lose it? Can you know good and ill without divination? Can you stop? Can you cease? Can you set aside others and seek within yourself? Can you be supple? Can you be yielding? Can you be like a child? The child wails all day yet its throat does not grow hoarse—so perfect is its harmony; it clenches its fist all day yet its hand does not cramp—so complete is its virtue; it gazes all day yet its eyes do not blink—its focus is not on externals. It walks without knowing where it goes, dwells without knowing what it does, drifts with things and moves with the current—this is the classic of preserving life."

Nanrong Chu said: "Then is this the virtue of the perfected person?"

Laozi said: "No."

This is what is called "melting like ice, dissolving like frost."

The perfected person shares sustenance with the earth and rejoices with heaven, not entangled by the gains and losses of others, not engaging in strangeness, not scheming together, not acting in concert. Supple they depart, yielding they arrive—this is the classic of preserving life.

Nanrong Chu said: "Then is this perfection?"

Laozi said: "Not yet. I have already told you: can you be like a child? The child moves without knowing what it does, walks without knowing where it goes, its body like the branch of a withered tree, its heart like dead ashes. In such a state, misfortune does not arrive, nor does fortune come. With neither misfortune nor fortune, how could there be human calamity?

When the cosmos is at peace and settled, it emits the light of heaven. Those who emit the light of heaven are seen as human. Those who cultivate themselves now possess constancy; those who possess constancy are abandoned by others yet aided by heaven. Those abandoned by others are called the people of heaven; those aided by heaven are called the sons of heaven.

The learner learns what cannot be learned; the doer does what cannot be done; the debater debates what cannot be debated. To know when to stop at what cannot be known—this is the ultimate. If there are those who do not conform to this, the balance of heaven will destroy them.

Prepare things to sustain the form, conceal the unforeseen to nurture the heart, revere the center to reach the other. If despite this, myriad evils still arrive, they are from heaven, not from man—they cannot disturb completion or enter the numinous terrace. The numinous terrace holds something yet does not know what it holds, and there is that which cannot be held.

If one does not see the sincerity within oneself and acts, each act is inappropriate; if karma enters and does not depart, each change brings loss. Those who do evil in the light are punished by others; those who do evil in the dark are punished by spirits. Only those who are enlightened about others and enlightened about spirits can walk alone.

Those who hold inner bonds act without fame; those who hold outer bonds aspire to stipulated rewards. Acting without fame, only the mediocre gain luster; aspiring to stipulated rewards, they are but merchants—others see their divergence as towering. Those who exhaust things are entered by things; those who contend with things cannot even contain themselves, how can they contain others? Those who cannot contain others have no intimacy; those with no intimacy are forsaken by all.

No weapon is more invasive than the will—even the *mo* and *zou* blades are inferior. No enemy is greater than yin and yang, from which there is no escape within heaven and earth. It is not that yin and yang harm them, but that the heart commands it.

The Dao flows through. Its division is its completion, its completion is its destruction. What is detestable about division is that it divides in order to prepare; what is detestable about preparation is that it prepares for something. Thus, to depart and not return is to see one’s ghost; to depart and obtain is called obtaining death. To perish yet have substance is one kind of ghost.

By using the formed to model the formless, one may attain stability. Departing without root, entering without aperture. Having substance yet no place, having length yet no beginning or end, having an exit yet no aperture—this is having substance. Having substance yet no place is space. Having length yet no beginning or end is time. There is life, there is death, there is departure, there is entry. Departing and entering without seeing its form—this is called the Gate of Heaven. The Gate of Heaven is Nonbeing. The myriad things emerge from Nonbeing. Being cannot use being to create being; it must emerge from Nonbeing, and Nonbeing is no Nonbeing.

The sage conceals himself therein.

The ancients—some of their knowledge reached the utmost. To what extent? Some believed there had never yet been things—this is the utmost, the ultimate, beyond which nothing can be added. Next were those who believed things existed, taking life as loss and death as return—thus they were already divided. Next were those who said: first there was Nonbeing, then there was life, and life swiftly became death. They took Nonbeing as the head, life as the body, and death as the corpse. Who knows the one who guards the unity of being, nonbeing, life, and death? I would befriend such a one.

Though these three differ, they belong to the same clan—like the *Zhao* and *Jing* lineages, the *Zhu* and *Dai* families, the *Jia* clan, the *Zhu* and *Feng* houses—not one and the same. There is life, like a sealed letter, unfolding day by day. I have spoken of this unfolding, but it is not what I meant. Nevertheless, it cannot be known.

The victory of the *la* sacrifice can be dispersed yet cannot be dispersed. The observer of a chamber is thorough in the ancestral temple, but what of its collapse? This is to cite the unfolding. Let me speak constantly of this unfolding.

Thus, taking life as the root and knowledge as the teacher, one rides upon right and wrong, resulting in names and realities. Taking oneself as the standard, one makes others conform to one’s own measure, using death to repay one’s principles. Those who act thus use action as wisdom, non-action as foolishness, thoroughness as fame, and poverty as disgrace—this unfolding is the way of people today, like the cicada and the fledgling dove in their sameness.

If you tread on a market-goer’s foot, you apologize with alarm; if it is an elder brother, you soothe him; if it is a parent, you stop at that. Thus it is said: the utmost ritual does not involve others, the utmost righteousness does not involve things, the utmost knowledge does not scheme, the utmost benevolence has no intimacy, the utmost trust dispenses with metal.

Break the fervor of the will, untie the knots of the heart, cast off the burdens of virtue, clear the blockages of the Dao. Wealth, honor, prominence, dignity, fame, and profit—these six stir the will. Appearance, movement, complexion, pattern, qi, and intent—these six entangle the heart. Aversion, desire, joy, anger, sorrow, and happiness—these six burden virtue. Advancement, retreat, taking, giving, knowledge, and ability—these six obstruct the Dao. When these four sets of six do not agitate the breast, one is upright; upright, one is tranquil; tranquil, one is luminous; luminous, one is empty; empty, one does nothing yet nothing is left undone.

The Dao is the reverence of virtue.

Life is the radiance of virtue; nature is the essence of life. The movement of nature is called action; the artificiality of action is called loss.

"Likeness" is connection; "knowledge" is planning. What knowledge does not know is like a sidelong glance. Movement from inevitability is called virtue; movement without self is called governance. Names may oppose each other, yet realities align.

Yi excelled at hitting the subtle mark yet was clumsy at preventing others from praising him. The sage excels at Heaven yet is clumsy with men. Only the whole person can excel at Heaven while contending with men. Only the creature can be a creature; only the creature can be Heaven. The whole person detests Heaven, detests men’s Heaven—how much more my Heaven among men!

A sparrow that comes to Yi is certain to be caught—this is success. If one takes the world as one’s cage, then no sparrow can escape. Thus Tang used a kitchen servant to enclose Yi Yin, and Duke Mu of Qin used five sheepskins to enclose Bai Li Xi. Therefore, if one does not use what they love to enclose them, nothing can be obtained.

The crippled man flaunts his adornments, rejecting external praise and blame. The convict climbs high without fear, having abandoned life and death. To return to speech without offering and forget others—by forgetting others, one thereby becomes a Heavenly person. Thus, one who is revered yet feels no joy, or insulted yet feels no anger, is one who harmonizes with Heaven’s harmony.

When anger arises from non-anger, then anger emerges from non-anger. When action arises from non-action, then action emerges from non-action. To desire stillness, level the qi; to desire spirit, follow the heart. In action, to desire appropriateness, adhere to inevitability. The category of inevitability is the Way of the sage.

---

*Master Xu Wugui*, through Lady Shang, was received by Marquis Wu of Wei. The marquis greeted him, saying, "Master, you are weary—wearied by the toil of mountains and forests, and thus willing to see me."

Xu Wugui replied, "It is I who weary you—what weariness have you for me? If you indulge your desires and prolong your likes and dislikes, the essence of your nature and life will sicken. If you suppress your desires and learn your likes and dislikes, your ears and eyes will sicken. I would weary you—what weariness have you for me?"

The marquis, startled, made no reply. After a moment, Xu Wugui said, "Let me tell you: I read dogs. The inferior dog stops at being full—this is the virtue of a wildcat. The middling dog looks as if watching the sun; the superior dog seems to have lost its unity. But my reading of dogs is not as good as my reading of horses.

In my reading of horses, the straight align with the plumb line, the curved with the hook, the square with the carpenter’s square, the round with the compass—these are the state’s horses, yet they do not compare to the world’s horses. The world’s horses have perfect material: seeming anxious, seeming lost, as if they have forgotten their unity. Such horses surpass all, leaving no trace—one cannot know where they go."

The marquis was greatly pleased and laughed. When Xu Wugui left, Lady Shang asked, "Master, how did you alone delight my lord? Those who please my lord do so by expounding the *Odes*, *Documents*, *Rites*, and *Music* in breadth, or by citing the *Golden Tablets and Six Strategies* in depth. Those who serve him with great merit are countless, yet my lord has never once smiled. How did you delight him so?"

Xu Wugui said, "I simply told him how I read dogs and horses."

Lady Shang said, "Is that all?"

He replied, "Have you not heard of the exiles of Yue? After a few days away from their state, they rejoice at seeing those they know. After a month, they rejoice at seeing those they once saw in their state. After a year, they rejoice even at seeing those who resemble people. Is it not that the longer one is away from others, the deeper the longing for them?

Those who flee into the void tread the paths of foxes and weasels, treading lightly in the emptiness. When they hear the sound of human footsteps, they rejoice—how much more when brothers and kin drink and feast beside them! For too long, no one has offered my lord the medicine of a true person’s words by his side."

Xu Wugui later saw the marquis, who said, "Master, you dwell in the mountains and forests, eating taro and chestnuts, disdaining onions and leeks, yet you deign to visit me—how long it has been! Are you now old? Do you crave the taste of wine and meat? Or do you bring blessings to my altars of soil and grain?"

Xu Wugui replied, "I, Wugui, was born poor and lowly and have never dared to taste your wine and meat. I came to weary you."

The marquis said, "How so? Why weary me?"

He replied, "To weary your spirit and form."

The marquis said, "What do you mean?"

Xu Wugui replied, "The nurturing of Heaven and Earth is one. Ascending high does not make one tall, nor does dwelling low make one short. Yet you alone, as lord of ten thousand chariots, bring suffering to the people of your state to nourish your ears, eyes, nose, and mouth—your spirit does not consent to this. The spirit delights in harmony and detests perversity. Perversity is a sickness, and so I weary you. It is only your sickness that wearies you—why is this?"

The marquis said, "I have long wished to meet you. I wish to love the people and practice righteousness by laying down arms—would this be possible?"

Xu Wugui said, "It would not. Loving the people is the beginning of harming them; practicing righteousness and laying down arms is the root of creating arms. If you proceed thus, you will likely not succeed. All completed beauty is a dangerous tool. Though you act with benevolence and righteousness, you will nearly become false. Form inevitably produces form, completion inevitably invites attack, and change inevitably brings external strife. You must not array grand cranes in the towers of your palace, nor parade fine steeds in the courtyards of your halls. Do not store resentment in your gains, do not overcome others with skill, do not overcome others with schemes, do not overcome others with war. Those who kill the people’s soldiers and seize others’ lands to nourish their own desires and spirits—how can one know which side is good in such a war? Where does victory lie? If you cannot stop, cultivate sincerity within your heart to respond to the feelings of Heaven and Earth, and do not obstruct them. The people are already dead and free—how will you use the laying down of arms?

Once, the Yellow Emperor wished to visit Great Vision on Mount Juci. Fangming drove his chariot, Changyu rode as attendant, Zhangruo and Weipeng led the horses, while Kunhe and Guji followed in the rear carriage. When they reached the wilds of Xiangcheng, the Seven Sages all lost their way and had nowhere to ask directions. They happened upon a boy herding horses and asked him the way. The boy said, 'Do you know Mount Juci?' 'Yes,' they replied. 'Do you know where Great Vision dwells?' 'Yes,' he said. The Yellow Emperor exclaimed, 'How extraordinary, this little boy! Not only does he know Mount Juci, but he also knows where Great Vision dwells. Allow me to ask how to govern the world.' The boy said, 'Governing the world is just like this—what more is there to do? When I was young, I roamed freely within the Six Directions. Then I fell ill with a sudden affliction, and an elder taught me, saying, "Ride the sun’s chariot and roam the wilds of Xiangcheng." Now that my illness has slightly improved, I am about to roam beyond the Six Directions again. Governing the world is just like this—what more is there to do?'"

The Yellow Emperor said, "Governing the world is truly not your concern. Nevertheless, allow me to ask about governing the world." The boy declined. The Yellow Emperor asked again. The boy said, "What makes governing the world different from herding horses? It is merely a matter of removing what harms the horses." The Yellow Emperor bowed twice, touching his forehead to the ground, and called him "Heavenly Master" before withdrawing.

The contemplative scholar finds no joy without the vicissitudes of thought, the eloquent scholar finds no joy without the order of discourse, and the scrutinizing scholar finds no joy without the sting of reproach—all are confined by things.

Those who summon the age to court, the common folk who covet office, the strong who boast of hardship, the brave who rush into peril, warriors who delight in battle, recluses who cling to fame, legalists who expand governance, ritualists who refine demeanor, and the benevolent who value connections—each has their place.

Farmers find no fellowship without the toil of clearing fields, merchants find no fellowship without the bustle of markets. Common folk are spurred by daily labor, artisans take pride in their tools’ craftsmanship. Without accumulating wealth, the greedy grow anxious; without surpassing power, the proud grow sorrowful. Those who delight in change seize the moment when it serves them, yet cannot remain inactive. All these follow the turning of the seasons, unyielding to transformation.

They exhaust their nature, submerge themselves in the ten thousand things, and never return for the rest of their lives—how lamentable!

Zhuangzi said, "If an archer hits the mark without aiming, we call him a skilled shot. If everyone in the world were like Yi, would that be acceptable?"
Huizi said, "It would."
Zhuangzi said, "If the world had no shared standard of rightness, and each affirmed only what they deemed right, would it be acceptable for everyone in the world to be like Yao?"
Huizi said, "It would."
Zhuangzi said, "Then among the Confucians, Mohists, Yangists, and Bingists—four schools—and you, making five, which one is truly right? Or is it like the case of Lu Ju? His disciple said, 'I have grasped the Master’s Way! I can boil a cauldron in winter and make ice in summer.' Lu Ju said, 'This merely summons yang with yang and yin with yin—it is not what I call the Way. Let me show you my Way.' Thereupon, he tuned a zither, placing one in the hall and one in the chamber. When he plucked the *gong* note, the *gong* note resonated; when he plucked the *jiao* note, the *jiao* note resonated—their harmonies were identical. But if he adjusted one string so that it no longer fit the five notes and plucked it, all twenty-five strings vibrated, yet the sound was not distinct from the rest, though it became the ruler of the tones. Is this not akin to your case?"
Huizi said, "Now the Confucians, Mohists, Yangists, and Bingists all contend with me in debate, clashing in words and overpowering in voice, yet none have ever proven me wrong. How does this compare?"
Zhuangzi said, "A man from Qi who lost his son in Song had a fate that was not whole; when he sought a lost bell, he bound it tightly; when he sought Tang’s son, he never left his domain—he left something behind. A man from Chu who lodged in a place where arrows were stored met with boatmen at midnight in a deserted land. Though he never left the cliff, he was enough to incur resentment.

Zhuangzi, while attending a funeral, passed by Huizi’s grave. Turning to his followers, he said, "A man from Ying had plaster splattered on the tip of his nose, as thin as a fly’s wing. He had Carpenter Shi shave it off. Carpenter Shi swung his axe with the force of the wind, listening as he struck, removing all the plaster without harming the nose. The man from Ying stood unmoved. When the Lord of Song Yuan heard of this, he summoned Carpenter Shi and said, 'Try to do it for me.' Carpenter Shi replied, 'Your servant once could do this, but the material for my skill has long been dead. Since the Master’s passing, I have had no one to work with—I have no one to speak to.'"

When Guan Zhong fell ill, Duke Huan asked him, "Your illness has grown severe. If it worsens, to whom should I entrust the state?" Guan Zhong said, "Whom does the Duke wish to appoint?" The Duke said, "Bao Shuya." Guan Zhong said, "No. He is a meticulous and upright gentleman. Those who do not measure up to him, he does not associate with. Once he hears of someone’s fault, he never forgets it. If he governed the state, he would soon offend the ruler above and alienate the people below. It would not be long before he incurred the Duke’s displeasure." The Duke said, "Then who is suitable?" Guan Zhong replied, "If you must choose, then Yang Peng will do. His character is such that he forgets those above and rebels against those below. He feels ashamed of not being like the Yellow Emperor and pities those who do not measure up to himself. To distribute virtue is called sageliness; to distribute wealth is called worthiness. To rule others with worthiness never wins their hearts; to humble oneself with worthiness never fails to win them. He is deaf to affairs of state and blind to matters of the household. If you must choose, then Yang Peng will do."

King Wu was floating on the river and ascended Mount Ju. The troop of monkeys, seeing him, fled in alarm and hid in the dense thickets. One monkey, however, lingered, swinging its arms and scratching, displaying its skill before the king. The king shot at it, and the monkey deftly caught the swift arrow. The king ordered his attendants to rush forward and shoot, and the monkey was seized and killed. The king turned to his friend Yan Buyi and said, "This monkey relied on its skill and agility to defy me, bringing about its own demise. Take heed! Alas, do not let your demeanor make you arrogant toward others." Yan Buyi returned home and studied under Dong Wu to uproot his arrogance. He renounced pleasure and declined fame. After three years, the people of the state praised him.

Nanbo Zijing sat leaning on his armrest, gazing at the sky and sighing. Yan Chengzi entered and said, "Master, you are the most extraordinary of beings. Can the body truly be made like withered bones, the heart like dead ashes?" He replied, "Once I dwelled in a mountain cave. At that time, when the fields of Qi caught a glimpse of me, the people of the state all celebrated three times."

I must precede them, and thus they know it; I must offer it, and thus they purify themselves for it. If I did not possess it, how could they know it? If I did not offer it, how could they purify themselves for it? Alas! I grieve for those who lose themselves, and I grieve again for those who grieve for them, and I grieve yet again for those who grieve for the grief of others—ever receding with each passing day.

When Confucius traveled to Chu, the king of Chu offered him wine. Sunshu Ao stood holding the goblet, while Shinan Yiliao received the wine and made an offering, saying, "Men of old have spoken of this." Confucius replied, "I have heard of the speech that is without words, yet I have never spoken of it—until now." Shinan Yiliao juggled his balls to resolve the feud between two families, while Sunshu Ao slept soundly, holding a feather fan, and the people of Ying laid down their weapons. Confucius wished for a beak three feet long.

What they call the Dao that is not the Dao, this is what I call the eloquence of wordlessness. Thus virtue gathers where the Dao is one. And speech rests where knowledge knows no more—this is perfection. Where the Dao is one, virtue cannot be the same; where knowledge knows no more, eloquence cannot encompass it. Names like those of the Confucians and Mohists bring only misfortune.

Thus the sea does not refuse the streams that flow into it—such is its greatness. The sage embraces heaven and earth, bestowing blessings upon the world, yet no one knows his name. Therefore, in life he holds no rank, in death he leaves no wealth; he does not accumulate goods, nor does he establish a name—this is what is called the Great Man.

A dog is not good because it barks well, nor is a man worthy because he speaks well—how much less so for one who is great! To be great is not enough to be called great—how much less so for virtue! Greatness is complete, yet nothing compares to heaven and earth. What do they seek, and yet are complete? One who knows completeness seeks nothing, loses nothing, discards nothing, and does not let things alter his true self. Turning inward without exhaustion, following the ancient without friction—this is the sincerity of the Great Man.

Master Jing had eight sons. He lined them up before him and summoned Jiufang Yan, saying, "Examine my sons for me and tell me which one is most auspicious." Jiufang Yan replied, "Kun is the most auspicious." Master Jing started in surprise and joy, asking, "In what way?" Jiufang Yan said, "Kun will share the ruler’s table and feast for the rest of his life." Master Jing’s face fell, and tears welled up as he said, "What has my son done to reach such an extreme?" Jiufang Yan replied, "One who shares the ruler’s table brings blessings to three clans—how much more so to his parents! Now that you have heard this and weep, you reject good fortune. Your son is auspicious, but you are not."

Master Jing said, "Yan, how could you possibly know that Kun is auspicious? His fate is bound to meat and wine, entering only his nose and mouth—how could you know where it comes from? I have never herded cattle, yet a calf was born in the inner chamber; I have never hunted, yet a quail hatched in the granary. If you find this strange, why? Those with whom I associate wander between heaven and earth. With them, I roam in joy beneath the sky and feast upon the earth. I do not involve myself in their affairs, nor do I scheme with them, nor do I share in their strange omens. I ride the sincerity of heaven and earth without letting things entangle me; I drift with them, unburdened by worldly concerns. Now, suddenly, there is this worldly reward. Whenever there are strange signs, there must be strange conduct. Alas, this is not the fault of my son or me—it is heaven’s doing! That is why I weep."

Before long, Kun was sent to Yan. Bandits seized him on the road. To keep him whole and offer him as a sacrifice would be difficult; to cut off his foot would be easier. So they cut off his foot and presented him as a treasure to the state of Qi, where he ended up at Duke Qu’s gate, eating meat for the rest of his life.

Nie Que met Xu You and asked, "Where are you going?" Xu You replied, "I am fleeing from Yao." Nie Que asked, "Why?" Xu You said, "Yao is so earnestly benevolent that I fear he will be ridiculed by the world. Future generations may even turn to cannibalism. The people are not hard to gather: love them, and they draw near; benefit them, and they come; praise them, and they are encouraged; bring them what they hate, and they scatter. Love and benefit arise from benevolence and righteousness, but those who abandon benevolence and righteousness are few, while those who profit from them are many. The practice of benevolence and righteousness lacks sincerity—it is merely a tool for the greedy and rapacious. Thus, one man’s judgment brings profit to the world, yet it is like a single glance. Yao knows that worthy men benefit the world, but he does not know that they also harm it. Only those outside the realm of worthiness understand this."

There are those who are *nuanmei*, those who are *runxu*, and those who are *juanlou*. The *nuanmei* learn the words of a single master and delight in them privately, believing themselves sufficient, unaware that nothing has truly begun—this is what is called *nuanmei*. The *runxu* are like lice on a pig: they choose the sparse bristles and think it a vast palace, nestling between the hooves and thighs, believing it a safe and comfortable dwelling. They do not know that one day the butcher will roll up his sleeves, spread straw, and light a fire, and they will burn along with the pig. They advance and retreat within their narrow domain—this is what is called *runxu*. The *juanlou* is like Shun. Mutton does not long for ants, but ants long for mutton because mutton has a strong scent. Shun had a rank odor in his conduct, and the people delighted in him. Thus, he moved three times, and each time his settlement grew, until at Deng’s ruins he had a hundred thousand households. Yao, hearing of Shun’s worthiness, raised him from the barren land, saying, "I hope to benefit from his presence." Shun was raised from the barren land, and as his years and teeth grew long, his hearing and sight declined, yet he could not retire—this is what is called *juanlou*.

Thus, the spirit-like man dislikes the multitude’s arrival, for when the multitude arrives, they do not harmonize, and when they do not harmonize, no benefit comes of it.

Thus, he has no extreme attachments, no extreme aversions, embracing virtue and kindling harmony to follow the world—this is called the *True Man*. To ants, he discards cunning; to fish, he finds the right measure; to sheep, he abandons intent.

With the eye, he sees the eye; with the ear, he hears the ear; with the heart, he returns to the heart. Such a one is as level as a carpenter’s line, and in change, he follows the natural course.

The *True Man* of old awaited all with Heaven, not imposing man upon Heaven. The *True Man* of old gained life through it, lost it through death; gained death through it, lost it through life.

Medicines—some are *dong*, some are *jiegeng*, some are *jiyong*, some are *shiling*—each in its time serves as the sovereign remedy. How can they all be enumerated?

Goujian, with three thousand armored men, perched on Kuaiji. Only Zhong knew how ruin could be preserved, yet only Zhong did not know why his own person brought sorrow.

Thus, the crow’s eye has its destination, the crane’s step has its measure—releasing them brings sorrow.

Thus, the wind passing over a river diminishes it, the sun passing over a river diminishes it. If only the wind and sun were to guard the river together, the river would still deem itself untouched, for it relies on its source to flow onward.

Thus, water guards earth with precision, shadows guard people with precision, things guard things with precision.

Thus, the eye’s clarity is perilous, the ear’s acuity is but a beginning, the heart’s pursuit is but a beginning. All that relies on the storehouse is perilous, and once peril is formed, it cannot be amended.

Misfortune’s growth is dense and clustered; its reversal is swift, yet its fruition waits long. Yet people treasure it as their own—is this not lamentable?

Thus, states perish and people are slaughtered without end, for they do not question what is right.

Thus, the foot treads upon the earth, and though it treads, it relies on what it does not tread to walk broadly. Man’s knowledge is scant, and though scant, he relies on what he does not know to comprehend Heaven’s intent.

Know the Great Oneness, know the Great Yin, know the Great Eye, know the Great Balance, know the Great Square, know the Great Faith, know the Great Constancy—this is perfection.

The Great Oneness unites them, the Great Yin dissolves them, the Great Eye sees them, the Great Balance harmonizes them, the Great Square embodies them, the Great Faith verifies them, the Great Constancy sustains them.

Exhaust Heaven’s fullness, follow illumination, grasp the pivot, begin with that which is other.

Then, in dissolving them, one seems not to dissolve; in knowing them, one seems not to know. It is by not knowing that one comes to know.

In questioning, there can be no bounds, yet there can be no lack of bounds. Though tangled and slippery, it has substance—past and present do not replace it, nor can it be diminished. Can this not be called the great exaltation and collapse?

Why not simply question this? Why be so perplexed? Resolving perplexity with non-perplexity, returning to non-perplexity—this is the ultimate non-perplexity.

---

*The Southern Florescence: True Classic, Scroll Four – "Miscellaneous Chapters: Zeyang, Twenty-Five"*

Zeyang traveled in Chu. Yijie spoke of him to the king, but the king did not summon him. Yijie returned.

Pengyang met Wang Guo and said, "Master, why not recommend me to the king?"

Wang Guo replied, "I am not as good as Gongyue Xiu."

Pengyang asked, "What does Gongyue Xiu do?"

He said, "In winter, he retreats alone to the river; in summer, he rests in the mountain thickets. If someone passes by and asks, he says, 'This is my dwelling.' Yijie could not do this—how much less could I? I am not even Yijie’s equal."

As for Yijie’s character: he had no virtue but possessed knowledge. He did not approve of himself, yet used this to mystify his associations, stumbling blindly into the realm of wealth and honor. He did not assist others with virtue but instead aided their dissipation.

The frozen borrow clothes from spring; the scorched seek winter’s cool breeze.
