The King of Chu’s character was one of imposing form and severity. In punishing crimes, he was as relentless as a tiger. Without flatterers and upright virtue, who could sway him?

Thus, the sage—when impoverished, makes his family forget their poverty; when prosperous, makes kings and lords forget their ranks and emoluments, transforming them to humility.

Toward things, he delights in them; toward people, he rejoices in their communion while preserving himself. Thus, sometimes without speaking, he harmonizes others; standing beside them, he transforms them.

The proper roles of father and son—where do they return to dwell? One inquires into where they are applied. His distance from the hearts of men is thus.

Thus, they await Gongyue Xiu.

The sage’s understanding is thorough and all-encompassing, embracing all as one, yet unaware of how this is so—it is his nature.

Responding to fate and acting with Heaven as his teacher, others then follow and name it. Yet in his concern for knowledge, his actions seldom last long—how can they have an end?

One born beautiful—people offer him a mirror. If not told, he does not know his beauty surpasses others. Whether he knows or does not know, hears or does not hear, his joy never ceases. People’s fondness for him never ceases—it is his nature.

The sage’s love for others—people give him a name. If not told, he does not know his love for others. Whether he knows or does not know, hears or does not hear, his love never ceases. People’s comfort in him never ceases—it is his nature.

An old state, an old capital—gazing upon them brings delight. Even if hills, mounds, grasses, and trees encroach, covering nine-tenths, the delight remains.

Death is seeing what is seen, hearing what is heard—like a hundred-foot tower suspended to answer the many questions.

Ran Xiangshi grasped the center of the ring and followed completion, moving with things without end or beginning, almost without time.

One who daily transforms with things is the one who does not transform. Once set aside—

The one who takes Heaven as his teacher yet cannot attain it, who follows things in pursuit—how can his affairs proceed?

The sage has never begun with Heaven, never begun with man, never begun with a beginning, never begun with things. He moves with the world without ceasing, his actions complete without excess—how does he unite with them?

Tang obtained his steward, the Gatekeeper Yin Deng, to transmit this—following the master without confinement. Obtaining its "following completion," he became the steward of its name. This name, though excessive in method, attained its dual vision.

Confucius, exhausting his thoughts, transmitted it as well.

Rong Chengmin said: "Remove the days, and there are no years; no inner, no outer."

Wei Ying made a pact with Marquis Tian Mou, but Marquis Tian Mou betrayed it. Wei Ying grew enraged and was about to send an assassin. Xishou, hearing of this, found it shameful: "You are a lord of ten thousand chariots, yet you would follow the ways of a common man? Yan requests to receive two hundred thousand armored troops to attack him on your behalf, capturing his people, seizing his cattle and horses, so that his lord’s inner heat erupts upon his back. Then we shall uproot his state. Ji will flee, and then we shall strike his back and break his spine."

Jizi, hearing of this, found it shameful: "To build a city wall seventy feet high—once it reaches seventy feet, to then tear it down—this is the toil that convict laborers lament. Now, for seven years, no war has arisen; this is the foundation of your kingship. Yan is a disturber of order; he must not be heeded."

Huazi, hearing of this, found it contemptible: "Those who speak well of attacking Qi are disturbers of order; those who speak well of not attacking are also disturbers of order. To call both attacking and not attacking disturbers of order—this too is a disturber of order."

The lord asked: "Then what should be done?"

He replied: "My lord, seek only the Dao."

Huizi, hearing this, went to see Dai Jinren. Jinren said: "There is a creature called the snail—do you know of it?"

The lord said: "Yes."

"On the left horn of the snail is a state called Chushi, and on the right horn, a state called Manshi. At times, they contend over land and wage war, leaving tens of thousands of corpses in their wake. The pursuit of the routed lasts fifteen days before they return."

The lord said: "Alas!"

"Is this merely empty talk?"

He replied: "Let your servant demonstrate its truth to you. Does my lord imagine the four directions and the realms above and below to have limits?"

The lord said: "They are without limit."

He said: "To know the mind roams in the boundless and yet returns to the states that lie within reach—do they seem as if they exist, as if they do not?"

The lord said: "So it is."

He said: "Within the reachable lies Wei; within Wei lies Liang; within Liang lies the king. Is there any distinction between the king and the Manshi?"

The lord said: "There is no distinction."

The guest departed, and the lord remained in a daze, as if something had been lost.

When the guest had gone, Huizi came to see him. The lord said: "That guest was a great man—even a sage would not suffice to match him."

Huizi said: "When one blows through a pipe, there is still a sound; when one blows across a sword’s ring, there is but a faint whistle. Yao and Shun are those whom men praise; yet to speak of Yao and Shun before Dai Jinren is like that faint whistle.

When Confucius traveled to Chu, he lodged at the thatched hut by Ant Mound. His neighbors, a husband and wife with their servants, climbed to the roof. Zilu said: 'What are those bustling figures doing?'

Zhongni said: 'They are the servants of a sage. He buries himself among the people, conceals himself at the margins. His voice fades, yet his will is boundless. Though his mouth speaks, his heart has never truly spoken. He goes against the world, yet his mind disdains to accompany it. Such a one is a 'land-sunk' man—is this not the Market South’s Yi Liao?'

Zilu asked to go and summon him. Confucius said: 'Enough. He knows that I am known to him, that I am traveling to Chu, and that I would surely have the king of Chu summon him. He would take me for a flatterer. One like that feels shame even to hear the words of a flatterer—how much more to see him in person? And how could he remain here?'

Zilu went to look, but the house was empty.

The Warden of Changwu asked Zilao: 'In governing, do not be reckless; in ordering the people, do not be careless. Once, when I planted grain, I plowed recklessly, and the harvest repaid me in kind; I weeded carelessly, and the harvest repaid me in kind. The next year, I changed my ways, deepened the plowing, and tended the soil with care. The grain stalks flourished, and I had my fill for the whole year.'

Zhuangzi, hearing of this, said: 'Nowadays, when men cultivate their bodies and order their minds, many resemble what the warden described. They flee from Heaven, depart from their nature, extinguish their emotions, and lose their spirit, all to follow the crowd. Thus, those who recklessly harm their nature give rise to the thorns of desire and aversion. These grow like reeds and rushes, first supporting the body, then seizing the nature, bursting forth in sores and ulcers, erupting without regard for place—festering abscesses, scabies, tumors, inner heat, and the draining of marrow. This is the result.'

Wu Ju studied under Lao Cong and said: 'Let me wander throughout the world.'

Lao Cong said: 'Enough.'"

The world remains as it is. Wu Ju again requested to wander, and Lao Cong asked: "Where will you begin?" Wu Ju replied: "I will begin in Qi."

Arriving in Qi, he saw a man who had been punished, pushed him forward, and forced him to kneel. Removing his court robes, he covered the man with them and wailed to Heaven, crying: "Oh, sir! Oh, sir! The world suffers a great blight, and you alone have been the first to encounter it. Day and night, they say: 'Do not steal, do not kill.' Yet only when honor and disgrace are established do people see what ails them; only when wealth is amassed do they see what they contend over. Now, you have established what ails people and amassed what they contend over, exhausting their bodies without respite. Could they not have avoided reaching this point?

The rulers of old took success as belonging to the people and failure as their own; they took uprightness as belonging to the people and crookedness as their own. Thus, if even one person suffered harm, they would withdraw and blame themselves. Today, it is not so. They conceal their deeds to fool the ignorant, create great difficulties and blame the timid, impose heavy burdens and punish the incapable, and set distant paths to condemn those who cannot reach. When the people exhaust their strength, they resort to deception to sustain themselves. Day by day, deceit multiplies—how can scholars and commoners not follow suit?

When strength is insufficient, they feign; when knowledge is lacking, they deceive; when wealth is wanting, they steal. For such thievery, whom can one blame? Lian Boyu lived sixty years and transformed sixty times, never failing to begin with what he deemed right only to end by condemning it as wrong. Who knows whether what he now calls right is not the wrong of fifty-nine years past?

All things have life, yet none see their roots; all things emerge, yet none see their gates. People all honor the knowledge they possess but do not know that they must rely on what their knowledge does not grasp before they can truly know. Can this not be called a great delusion? Enough, enough! There is nowhere to escape. Is this what is meant by 'so it is,' or is it so?

Confucius asked the Grand Historian Da Qiang, Bo Changqian, and Xi Wei: "Duke Ling of Wei indulged in wine and revelry, neglecting the governance of his state. He devoted himself to hunting and archery, ignoring his duties among the feudal lords. How then did he come to be called 'Ling'?" Da Qiang replied: "It was because of this."

Bo Changqian said: "Duke Ling had three wives, with whom he bathed in promiscuous waters. When Historian Qi presented the ritual jades, he struck them with his fists and supported the duke with his arms. Such was his extreme arrogance, yet when he beheld a worthy man, such was his reverence—this is why he was called 'Ling.'"

Xi Wei said: "When Duke Ling died, divination for burial in the ancestral tomb proved inauspicious, but divination at Shaqiu proved auspicious. After digging several *ren* deep, they found a stone outer coffin. Washing it and examining it, they saw an inscription that read: *'Do not rely on his son; Duke Ling shall seize and bury it.'* Duke Ling’s 'Ling' had long been so—how could these two men understand it?"

Little Knowledge asked Grand Duke Diao: "What is meant by 'the speech of the village and the hamlet'?"

Grand Duke Diao replied: "The village and hamlet gather ten surnames and a hundred names to form customs. They unite differences into sameness and disperse sameness into differences. Now, if you point to the hundred parts of a horse and do not obtain the horse, yet the horse stands before you—it is by assembling its hundred parts that you call it a horse. Thus, hills and mountains accumulate the lowly to become high; rivers and streams gather waters to become great; the great man unites all to become impartial. Therefore, what enters from without has a master but is not grasped; what issues from within has a norm but is not resisted. The four seasons have distinct *qi*, yet Heaven does not bestow favor, and so the year is completed. The five offices have distinct duties, yet the ruler does not act for private ends, and so the state is well-governed. The civil and martial great men do not bestow favor, and so their virtue is complete. The myriad things have distinct principles, yet the Dao does not act for private ends, and so it has no name. Having no name, it does nothing; doing nothing, yet there is nothing it does not do. The seasons have endings and beginnings; the world has changes and transformations. Fortune and misfortune flow like a current—where there is resistance, there is also what is fitting. Those who sacrifice themselves do so in different ways—where there is rectitude, there is also error. Compared to a great marsh, all goods pass through; observed in a great mountain, trees and stones share the same ground. This is what is meant by 'the speech of the village and the hamlet.'"

Little Knowledge said: "Then can this be called the Dao?"

Grand Duke Diao said: "No. Now, the number of things is not limited to ten thousand, yet we call them 'the ten thousand things' because we use the term for the greatest number to name them. Thus, Heaven and Earth are the greatest of forms; *yin* and *yang* are the greatest of *qi*; the Dao is their impartiality. To name and call them by their greatness is permissible, but once they exist, how can they be compared? If you debate in this way, it is like comparing dogs and horses—how far they fall short!

Little Knowledge said: "Within the four directions, within the six conjunctions, from whence do the ten thousand things arise?"

Grand Duke Diao said: "*Yin* and *yang* illuminate and cover each other, govern and regulate each other. The four seasons succeed one another, giving birth and bringing death. Desires and aversions, departures and approaches arise in this way; male and female unite in this manner. Safety and danger alternate; fortune and misfortune beget each other. Urgency and slowness rub against each other; gathering and scattering complete things. These are the facts that can be recorded, the essences that can be noted. Following the sequence of their principles, the movements of their bridges and mechanisms, when exhausted they return, when ended they begin—this is what things possess. What words can exhaust, what knowledge can reach, is merely the limit of things. Those who perceive the Dao do not follow what it abandons, do not trace what it initiates—this is where discussion stops."

Little Knowledge said: "Wei Zhen’s 'nothing doing' and Jiezi’s 'something making'—which of these two doctrines accords with reality, which is biased in its principle?"

Grand Duke Diao said: "The crowing of roosters and the barking of dogs are what people know. Even one of great knowledge cannot use words to describe how they transform themselves, nor can they anticipate what they will do. Analyzing this, the essence reaches the unparalleled, the greatness reaches the unbounded. Whether something makes or nothing does, they cannot escape things and thus ultimately consider it a fault. Something making is substantial; nothing doing is empty. Having name and reality is where things reside; having no name and no reality is the emptiness of things. What can be spoken and conceived—speaking of it only makes it more distant. What has not yet been born cannot be hindered; what has already died cannot be obstructed. Life and death are not far apart—principle cannot be seen. Whether something makes or nothing does, doubt borrows from this. Observing its root, its going is endless; seeking its end, its coming is unceasing. Endless and unceasing, words have nothing to say—it shares the principle of things. Whether something makes or nothing does, words are its root, sharing the beginning and end of things. The Dao cannot be possessed; what is possessed cannot be without it. The name 'Dao' is what is borrowed to act. Whether something makes or nothing does, it is but one corner of things—how does it compare to the great square? If words suffice, then speaking all day long exhausts the Dao; if words do not suffice, then speaking all day long exhausts things. The ultimate of the Dao and things—speech and silence cannot bear it. Neither speaking nor silent, discussion reaches its limit."

*The Fourth Scroll of the True Scripture of Southern Florescence concludes.*

**Miscellaneous Chapters of the True Scripture of Southern Florescence**
**Chapter Twenty-Six: External Things**

---

**Textual Variants:**
1. *Shi* (世) in the *Shide* edition originally read *Huang* (皇).
2. *Ci* (此) was originally *Bi* (比), emended according to the *Shide* edition.
3. *Lei* (累) was originally *Lei* (类), emended according to the *Shide* edition.
4. *Wei* (为) was originally *Wei* (谓), emended according to the *Shide* edition.
5. *Xing* (行) was originally *Peng* (抨), emended according to the *Shide* edition.
6. *Yu* (於) was absent in the original, added according to the *Shide* edition.
7. *Fu* (夫) was originally *Tian* (天), emended according to the *Shide* edition.
8. The phrase *you qi you wu ye wu yi* (犹其有物也无已) was absent in the original, added according to the *Shide* edition.
9. *Nian* (年) was originally *Zhe* (者), emended according to the *Shide* edition.
10. *Zhong* (中) was originally *Kou* (口), emended according to the *Shide* edition.
11. The phrase *wu you bei fu bei ren zhe* (吾又悲夫悲人者) was absent in the original, added according to the *Shide* edition.
12. *Ran* (然) was originally *Zhong* (终), emended according to the *Shide* edition.
13. *Ren* (人) was originally *Wai ren* (外人), emended according to the *Shide* edition.
14. *De* (得) was originally *De* (德), emended according to the *Shide* edition.
15. *Wan* (万) was originally *Wan wu* (万物), emended according to the *Shide* edition.

---

External things cannot be relied upon—thus Longfeng was executed, Bigan was slain, Jizi feigned madness, Elai died, and Jie and Zhou perished. All men desire their ministers to be loyal, yet loyalty does not necessarily earn trust; thus Wu Yun was cast into the river, and Chang Hong died in Shu, his blood stored for three years before turning to jade. All parents desire their children to be filial, yet filial piety does not necessarily bring love; thus Xiaoji grieved, and Zeng Shen mourned.

When wood rubs against wood, it ignites; when metal is held to fire, it melts. When yin and yang clash, heaven and earth convulse—thus there is thunder and lightning, fire within water, and the great locust tree burns. When one is beset by double sorrows with no escape, the falling mantis fails to mature, and the heart hangs suspended between heaven and earth, comfort and gloom entangled. As benefit and harm grind against each other, fire blazes fiercely. The multitude burns harmony away; the moon, though constant, cannot withstand the flames. Thus, there is a sudden collapse, and the Dao is exhausted.

Zhuangzi was poor, so he went to borrow grain from the Marquis of the River. The Marquis said, "Very well. When I receive the taxes from my fief, I will lend you three hundred gold pieces—will that suffice?" Zhuang Zhou flushed with anger and replied, "On my way here yesterday, I heard a cry from the road. I turned to look and saw a bream in the rut of a chariot wheel. I asked, 'Bream, what are you doing here?' It answered, 'I am a wave-minister of the Eastern Sea. Might you have a dipper of water to keep me alive?' I said, 'Very well. I am about to travel south to persuade the kings of Wu and Yue, and I will divert the waters of the West River to bring you there—will that suffice?' The bream flushed with anger and replied, 'I have lost my accustomed element and have nowhere to go. A dipper of water would keep me alive for now, yet you speak thus—you might as well seek me in a dried-fish shop!'"

Lord Ren made a great hook with a massive black rope, baited it with fifty steers, crouched on Mount Kuaiji, cast his line into the Eastern Sea, and fished day after day for a year without catching anything. Then a great fish took the bait, dragged the hook down, plunged violently, and thrashed its fins, raising white waves like mountains. The sea churned, the sound terrifying spirits for a thousand *li*. Lord Ren caught the fish, butchered it, and salted it. From Zhihe eastward to north of Cangwu, none did not feast on it. Thereafter, those of later generations—petty talents, wandering persuaders—all marveled and spread the tale. Yet those who raise flimsy poles, rush to irrigation ditches, and cling to shallow theories—how could they ever catch a great fish? Those who adorn trivial words to seek high office are as far from great understanding as the sky from the earth. Thus, they have never heard of the customs of the Ren clan, and their inability to navigate the world is equally distant.

Confucians use the *Odes* and *Rites* to rob graves. A great Confucian recited the liturgy: "Dawn breaks in the east—how goes the affair?" A lesser Confucian replied, "The skirt and trousers are not yet untied, but there is a pearl in the mouth." The *Odes* indeed say: "The green, green wheat grows on the hillside. If one does not give in life, why hold onto a pearl in death?" They seized his temples, rubbed his skull, and with a metal mallet pried open his jaw, carefully parting his cheeks to avoid damaging the pearl in his mouth.

A disciple of Lao Laizi went to gather firewood and encountered Confucius. He returned and reported, "There is a man over there—his upper body is long, his lower body short, his back hunched, his ears set far back. His gaze seems to survey the four seas—who is he?" Lao Laizi said, "That is Qiu." He summoned Confucius, who arrived. Lao Laizi said, "Qiu, cast aside your bodily pride and your cultivated knowledge—then you may become a gentleman." Confucius bowed and withdrew, his expression troubled, and asked, "May I advance in my work?" Lao Laizi replied, "One who cannot bear the wounds of a single generation but startles at the calamities of ten thousand ages—is this not stubborn and narrow? Or is it that your plans fall short? To take joy in alarm is a lifelong disgrace. The conduct of the common people advances thus: they draw one another by fame, bind one another by secrecy. Rather than praise Yao and condemn Jie, it is better to forget both and close off what you praise. To turn back is to invite harm; to act is to invite error. The sage hesitates in affairs, achieving success by chance—why should you burden yourself with such pride to the end?"

Duke Yuan of Song dreamed in the dead of night of a man with disheveled hair peering through the side gate, who said, "I come from the Zailu Abyss. I was sent by the Clear River as an emissary to the River Lord, but the fisherman Yu Ju caught me." The duke awoke and had the dream interpreted. The diviner said, "This is a sacred turtle."

The duke asked, "Is there a fisherman named Yu Ju among you?" His attendants replied, "There is." The duke said, "Summon Yu Ju to court." The next day, Yu Ju appeared at court. The duke asked, "What did you catch in your fishing?" Yu Ju answered, "I caught a white turtle in my net, five feet in circumference." The duke said, "Present this turtle to me." When the turtle arrived, the duke wavered twice—first wanting to kill it, then wanting to spare it. His mind unsettled, he divined with it, saying, "Killing the turtle for divination will bring good fortune." So they split the turtle open, drilling seventy-two times without a single miscast.

Confucius said: "The sacred turtle could appear in a dream to Duke Yuan, yet could not escape Yu Ju’s net; it could know enough to yield seventy-two flawless divinations, yet could not avoid the calamity of having its entrails cut out. Thus, even knowledge has its limitations, and even the numinous has its shortcomings. Though one possesses the highest wisdom, ten thousand men may still outscheme it. Fish do not fear the net but fear the roc. Discard petty knowledge, and great knowledge becomes clear; abandon contrived goodness, and true goodness arises of itself. A newborn babe, without a teacher of stone, can speak—because it dwells among those who can speak."

Huizi said to Zhuangzi, "Your words are useless." Zhuangzi replied, "Only when one knows the useless can one begin to speak of the useful. Heaven and earth are neither narrow nor small, yet what man uses is no more than the space his feet occupy. If, then, one were to dig away all the earth around his feet down to the Yellow Springs, would there still be any use for it?" Huizi said, "There would be none." Zhuangzi said, "Then the usefulness of the useless is also clear."

Zhuangzi said: "If a man has the ability to wander, can he choose not to wander? If a man cannot wander, can he force himself to do so? Those with drifting, evasive ambitions and reckless, self-destructive conduct—alas, are they fit for the tasks of perfect wisdom and profound virtue? They topple and do not recover, rush headlong and do not look back. Though they may be bound as ruler and minister, it is but a matter of time; when the age changes, they will no longer hold each other in contempt. Thus, the Perfect Man does not linger in his conduct. Those who exalt the past and belittle the present belong to the current of scholars. Yet if one views the present age through the lens of the Xiawei clan, who could avoid being swept along? Only the Perfect Man can wander through the world without bias, follow others without losing himself. He does not learn from teachings, but grasps their intent without clinging to them. Eyes that penetrate are called bright; ears that penetrate are called keen; a nose that penetrates is called discerning; a mouth that penetrates is called savoring; a mind that penetrates is called wise; wisdom that penetrates is called virtue. The Dao does not thrive on obstruction; obstruction leads to choking, and if choking persists, it turns to ruin, from which a multitude of harms arise. All creatures with knowledge depend on breath; if it does not flow smoothly, it is not Heaven’s fault. Heaven bores through them day and night without cease, yet man turns and blocks his own treasure. The womb has its layered gates, the mind has its celestial wanderings. If a room has no empty space, mother and daughter-in-law will quarrel; if the mind has no celestial wandering, the six senses will jostle one another. The great forests and mountains are good for man, yet even the spiritually endowed cannot fully master them. Does virtue overflow into fame, and fame into violence? Do schemes depend on knowledge, and knowledge arise from contention? Does obstruction grow from rigidity, and official affairs culminate in the harmony of the many? When spring rains fall in season, grasses and trees flourish, and the plow begins its work. Half the plants that sprout do so without man knowing why. Stillness can heal illness, slander can rest the aged, and tranquility can halt haste. Yet even so, these are the concerns of the toiling, not the leisurely who have never troubled themselves to inquire. What startles the common people is what the sage takes as his time and realm; what startles the sage is what the spirit man has never troubled to inquire about. What startles the worthy is what the sage has never troubled to inquire about; what startles the gentleman is what the worthy has never troubled to inquire about; what unites the petty man is what the gentleman has never troubled to inquire about.

When Yanmen’s father died, he mourned so perfectly that he was appointed an official teacher. His fellow villagers, emulating his grief, died from excessive mourning. Yao offered the world to Xu You, but Xu You fled from it; Tang offered it to Wu Guang, and Wu Guang grew angry. When Ji Ta heard of this, he led his disciples to kneel by the waters of the Ji River. The feudal lords came to console him, and after three years, Shentu Di drowned himself in the river.

The fish trap exists for the sake of the fish; once the fish is caught, the trap is forgotten. The rabbit snare exists for the sake of the rabbit; once the rabbit is caught, the snare is forgotten. Words exist for the sake of meaning; once the meaning is grasped, the words are forgotten. Where can I find a man who has forgotten words, that I might speak with him?"

*The Southern Florescence Perfected Scripture, Scroll Five: Miscellaneous Chapters, "Parables" Twenty-Seven*

Nineteen parts are parables, relying on external voices to expound. Seventeen parts are weighty words. Goblet words emerge daily, harmonizing with the Heavenly Equality. A father does not act as his son’s matchmaker.

A father’s praise is not as good as that of one who is not his father. It is not my fault—it is the fault of others. When something aligns with oneself, one responds; when it does not, one resists. What aligns with oneself is deemed right; what differs is deemed wrong.

The seventeen weighty words serve to conclude speech, for they are the words of elders. One may be senior in years, yet without the warp and weft of fundamental principles to be deemed truly venerable—this is not true seniority. A person who cannot surpass others lacks the Way of humanity; one who lacks the Way of humanity is called a mere remnant of the past.

Goblet words emerge daily, harmonizing with the Heavenly Equality, flowing and extending to exhaust the years. Without speech, all is equal; equality with speech is not equality, and speech with equality is not equality—thus it is said, "No speech." Speaking without speech, one speaks all one’s life yet has never spoken; not speaking all one’s life, one has never not spoken.

There is a source from which something may be deemed acceptable, and a source from which it may not; a source from which something is so, and a source from which it is not. How is it so? It is so because it is so. How is it not so? It is not so because it is not so. How is it acceptable? It is acceptable because it is acceptable. How is it unacceptable? It is unacceptable because it is unacceptable. Things inherently have what they affirm, things inherently have what they accept; nothing is not affirmed, nothing is not accepted.

If not for goblet words emerging daily, harmonizing with the Heavenly Equality, who could endure for long? All things are seeds, succeeding one another in different forms, beginning and end like a ring, with no discernible order—this is called the Heavenly Equilibrium. The Heavenly Equilibrium is the Heavenly Equality.

Zhuangzi said to Huizi: "Confucius lived sixty years and underwent sixty transformations. What he initially affirmed, he later rejected—who knows whether what he now calls right is not what he deemed wrong at fifty-nine?" Huizi said: "Confucius diligently applied his will and served his knowledge." Zhuangzi said: "Confucius has renounced such things, yet he has never spoken of it. Confucius said: 'One receives one’s endowment from the great root and revives one’s spirit to live. Singing in accord with the pitch, speaking in accord with the law. Profit and righteousness are set before one, yet likes and dislikes, right and wrong merely submit to people’s mouths. To make people submit with their hearts and not dare to stand in opposition, to settle the world’s settlements—enough, enough! I shall never reach his level.'"

Zengzi took office twice, and his heart transformed twice. He said: "When I served while my parents were alive, three *fu* of grain brought me joy; later, when I served, three thousand *zhong* did not suffice, and my heart grieved." A disciple asked Confucius: "Can Zeng Shen be said to have no attachment to blame?" Confucius said: "He was already attached. How could one without attachment feel sorrow? To him, three *fu* and three thousand *zhong* were like watching sparrows and mosquitoes pass before him."

Yan Chengzi You said to Shu Guozijing: "Since I heard your words, after one year I became rustic, after two years I followed, after three years I penetrated, after four years I became one with things, after five years things came to me, after six years spirits entered, after seven years Heaven was accomplished, after eight years I knew neither death nor life, and after nine years I attained the great mystery. Life is action; death is rest—exhort yourself. Because death has a source, while life, being yang, has no source. Is it truly so? Where does it go? Where does it not go? Heaven has its celestial reckoning, earth has its human foundations—where should I seek it? No one knows its end—how can there be no fate? No one knows its beginning—how can there be fate? When there is a response, how can there be no spirits? When there is no response, how can there be spirits?"

The multitude of *wangliang* asked the shadow: "Earlier you were bowing, now you are raising your head; earlier your hair was tied up, now it is loose; earlier you were sitting, now you are standing; earlier you were walking, now you are still—why?" The shadow said: "Such trifling questions! Why ask? I act without knowing why. I am like the cicada’s shell, the snake’s slough—seemingly so, yet not so. Fire and sun, I gather with them; yin and night, I alternate with them. Do I depend on them? How much more so for those who depend on others! When they come, I come with them; when they go, I go with them; when they are strong and yang, I am strong and yang with them. What question is there in strong yang?"

Yang Ziju was traveling south to Pei when Lao Dan was journeying west to Qin. They met at the outskirts of Liang, where Laozi sighed to the sky mid-journey and said: "At first I thought you could be taught, but now you cannot." Yang Ziju did not respond. Upon reaching their lodging, he brought water for washing, a towel, and comb, removed his shoes outside the door, advanced on his knees, and said: "Earlier, your disciple wished to ask you, Master, but you were traveling without speaking, so I did not dare. Now you have spoken—please tell me my fault." Laozi said: "Your haughty and overbearing demeanor—who would wish to dwell with you? The purest white appears sullied; the greatest virtue seems insufficient." Yang Ziju’s expression changed abruptly, and he said: "I respectfully receive your instruction." When he first arrived, the innkeeper welcomed him, the master of the house laid out the mat, his wife brought the towel and comb, the guests moved from their seats, and those warming themselves stepped away from the hearth. When he returned, the innkeeper vied with him for his seat.

*The Southern Florescence Perfected Scripture, Scroll Five: Miscellaneous Chapters, "Yielding the Throne" Twenty-Eight*

Yao offered the empire to Xu You, who refused it. He then offered it to Zizhou Zhipo, who said: "To make me Son of Heaven would be acceptable. However, I happen to suffer from a deep melancholy, and I am just now treating it—I have no leisure to govern the empire. The empire is the weightiest of things, yet I would not let it harm my life—how much less other things! Only one who does not treat the empire as his own can be entrusted with it." Shun offered the empire to Zizhou Zhibo.

Zizhou Zhibo said: "I happen to suffer from a deep melancholy, and I am just now treating it—I have no leisure to govern the empire. Thus the empire is the greatest of vessels, yet one does not exchange life for it. This is how those who embody the Dao differ from the common world."

Shun offered the empire to Shan Juan. Shan Juan said: "I stand amidst the cosmos—winter I clothe myself in furs, summer in hempen cloth. In spring I plow and sow, my body sufficient for labor; in autumn I reap and gather, my person sufficient for rest and sustenance. I rise with the sun and work, set with the sun and rest, wandering carefree between heaven and earth, my heart and will content. Why should I desire the empire?" He sighed, "Alas, you do not know me." And so he refused it. Thereupon he departed and entered the depths of Mount Tan, and none knew his whereabouts.

Shun offered the empire to his friend, the farmer of Shihu. The farmer of Shihu said: "How earnest is the lord’s conduct—a man who preserves his strength! Yet I deem Shun’s virtue still insufficient." Thereupon he and his wife, carrying their child on their backs, fled to the sea and never returned.

King Tai of Zhi dwelled in Deng. The Di tribes attacked him. He offered them furs and silks, but they refused; he offered them dogs and horses, but they refused; he offered them pearls and jade, but they refused. What the Di sought was land. King Tai of Zhi said: "To dwell with a man’s elder brother and kill his younger brother, to dwell with a man’s father and kill his son—I cannot bear it. Go, all of you, and dwell as you will. Whether one serves as my subject or as a subject of the Di—what difference is there? Moreover, I have heard that one does not harm what nourishes life for the sake of what is used to nourish it." Thereupon he took up his staff and departed. The people followed him in droves, and he established a state beneath Mount Qi.

King Tai of Zhi may be said to have known how to honor life. Those who honor life, though noble and wealthy, do not let what nourishes harm their person; though poor and lowly, they do not let profit burden their form. Yet today’s men who occupy high office and exalted rank all fear losing them. Seeing profit, they lightly risk their lives—how deluded they are!

The people of Yue assassinated their ruler for three generations. Prince Sou, troubled by this, fled into the cinnabar caves. When Yue was left without a ruler, the people sought Prince Sou but could not find him. They followed him to the cinnabar caves. Prince Sou refused to emerge, so the people smoked him out with mugwort. They bore him away in a jade carriage. Prince Sou grasped the carriage strap and mounted, gazing heavenward and crying: "Rulership! Rulership! Could you not spare me alone?" Prince Sou did not hate being a ruler—he hated the calamity of being a ruler. Such a man as Prince Sou may be said not to have harmed his life for the sake of the state. This was precisely what the people of Yue desired in a ruler.

Han and Wei contended over encroached land. Master Hua Zi visited Marquis Zhao of Xi, who wore a troubled expression. Master Hua Zi said: "Suppose now that the world inscribed a pledge before you, its words saying: 'Grasp it with your left hand, and your right hand will be severed; grasp it with your right hand, and your left hand will be severed. Yet whoever grasps it shall possess the world.' Would you grasp it?" Marquis Zhao of Xi said: "I would not grasp it." Master Hua Zi said: "Excellent. From this we see that one’s two arms are weightier than the world, and one’s body is weightier than one’s two arms. The state of Han is far lighter than the world, and what you now contend over is far lighter than Han. Yet you grieve and harm your life, fretting over what you cannot obtain." The marquis said: "Well said! Many have instructed me, but never have I heard such words." Master Hua Zi may be said to have known the measure of light and heavy.

The Lord of Lu heard that Yan He was a man who had attained the Dao. He sent an envoy bearing gifts to precede him. Yan He dwelled in a humble lane, wearing coarse cloth and feeding his own oxen. When the Lord of Lu’s envoy arrived, Yan He personally received him. The envoy asked: "Is this the home of Yan He?" Yan He replied: "This is He’s home." The envoy presented the gifts. Yan He said: "I fear the messenger may have misheard and would incur blame. It would be better to verify." The envoy returned, confirmed the matter, and came again to seek him, but Yan He could not be found. Thus one such as Yan He truly despised wealth and honor.

Therefore it is said: The true essence of the Dao is for governing the self; its remnants may be used for ordering the state; its dregs may be used for governing the world. From this we see that the achievements of emperors and kings are but the leftover tasks of sages—never what perfects the person or nourishes life. Yet today’s worldly gentlemen often endanger their bodies and abandon their lives to pursue things—is this not lamentable?

Whenever sages act, they must examine their purpose and their means. Suppose now there were a man here who used a pearl fit for the Duke of Sui to shoot a sparrow a thousand fathoms high—surely the world would laugh at him. Why? Because what he used was weighty, and what he sought was light. Yet life—how is it not weightier than the pearl of the Duke of Sui?

Master Liezi was poor, his countenance bearing the hue of hunger. A guest mentioned this to Zi Yang of Zheng, saying: "Lie Yukou is a man of the Dao. That he dwells in your state yet suffers poverty—does this not suggest you do not esteem scholars?" Zi Yang of Zheng immediately ordered his officials to send him grain. Master Liezi met the envoy, bowed twice, and declined. The envoy departed. Master Liezi entered his home, where his wife gazed at him and beat her breast, saying: "I have heard that the wife and children of a man of the Dao all enjoy ease and happiness. Now we bear the hue of hunger. The lord deigned to send you food, yet you refused it—is this not fate?" Master Liezi laughed and said to her: "The lord does not know me himself. He sent me grain on another’s word, and when he punishes me, it will likewise be on another’s word. This is why I did not accept it." In the end, the people indeed rose in rebellion and killed Zi Yang.

King Zhao of Chu lost his state. The butcher Yue followed in flight after King Zhao.

When King Zhao regained his state, he wished to reward those who had followed him, including the butcher Yue. The butcher Yue said: "When Your Majesty lost the state, I lost my butchery; now that Your Majesty has regained the state, I have regained my butchery. My rank and emolument are already restored—what reward is there to give?"

The king said: "Insist that he accept."

The butcher Yue said: "When Your Majesty lost the state, it was not my fault, so I dared not suffer punishment; now that Your Majesty has regained the state, it was not my merit, so I dare not claim reward."

The king said: "Let him be summoned to an audience."

The butcher Yue said: "By the laws of Chu, only those who have rendered great merit through heavy reward may be granted an audience. My wisdom was insufficient to preserve the state, and my courage insufficient to die in battle against the enemy. When the Wu army entered Ying, I fled from peril and avoided the foe—not that I deliberately followed Your Majesty. Now, if Your Majesty wishes to set aside the law and break the covenant to grant me an audience, this is not what I have heard of under heaven."

The king said to Marshal Zi Jing: "The butcher Yue dwells in lowly and humble circumstances, yet his exposition of principle is most lofty. You must extend to him the rank of the Three Standards."

The butcher Yue said: "The rank of the Three Standards—I know it is nobler than my butcher’s stall; the emolument of ten thousand *zhong*—I know it is richer than my butcher’s profit. Yet how could I covet rank and emolument and thereby cause my lord to bear the name of reckless bestowal? I dare not accept. I wish only to return to my butcher’s stall." Thus he refused.

Yuan Xian dwelt in Lu, in a wattle hut with walls no more than a fathom square, thatched with living grass. The door of woven reeds was incomplete, its hinge made of mulberry wood. Two rooms were windowed with earthen jars, their gaps stopped with coarse cloth. Rain leaked through above, and damp seeped below, yet he sat upright and plucked his zither.

Zi Gong arrived in a grand carriage, clad in a purple robe with a white sash, his high-wheeled chariot too wide for the lane. He went to see Yuan Xian.

Yuan Xian, wearing a hat of rushes and slippers of untanned leather, leaned on a staff of thornwood to answer the door.

Zi Gong said: "Alas! Master, what ails you?"

Yuan Xian replied: "I have heard that to lack wealth is called poverty; to study yet be unable to practice is called affliction. Now I am poor, not afflicted."

Zi Gong retreated, his face flushed with shame.

Yuan Xian laughed and said: "To act for the sake of worldly approval, to befriend for the sake of cliques, to study for others, to teach for oneself, to conceal one’s virtue beneath benevolence and righteousness, to adorn oneself with carriages and horses—these are things I cannot bear to do."

Zengzi dwelt in Wei, wrapped in a quilted robe without lining, his complexion swollen and sallow, his hands and feet calloused and cracked. For three days he had not kindled a fire, for ten years he had not made new clothes. When he straightened his cap, its strings snapped; when he grasped his lapels, his elbows showed; when he pulled on his shoes, his heels split. Yet he dragged his hem and sang the *Odes of Shang*, his voice filling heaven and earth, resonant as metal and stone.

No Son of Heaven could make him a subject, no feudal lord could make him a friend. Thus, he who nurtures his will forgets his form; he who nurtures his form forgets profit; he who attains the Dao forgets his mind.

Confucius said to Yan Hui: "Come, Hui. Your family is poor and your station low—why do you not enter official service?"

Yan Hui replied: "I do not wish to serve. I have fifty *mu* of fields outside the outer wall, enough to provide millet gruel; ten *mu* within the wall, enough to supply silk and hemp. Playing the zither is enough for my amusement; studying the Master’s Dao is enough for my joy. I do not wish to serve."

Confucius’s expression changed, and he said: "Excellent, Hui’s intent! I have heard that he who knows contentment is not burdened by profit; he who is truly self-possessed does not fear loss; he whose conduct is cultivated within does not chafe at lack of position. I have recited this for a long time, but only now, in you, Hui, do I see it. This is my gain."

Prince Mou of Zhongshan said to Zhanzi: "My body dwells by rivers and seas, yet my heart resides beneath the palace gates of Wei—what is to be done?"

Zhanzi said: "Value life. If you value life, profit will seem light."

Prince Mou of Zhongshan said: "Though I know this, I cannot overcome myself."

Zhanzi said: "If you cannot overcome yourself, then follow your inclination—what harm is there to the spirit? But if you cannot overcome yourself and yet force yourself not to follow, this is called double injury. A doubly injured man belongs not to the long-lived."

Prince Mou of Wei, a prince of ten thousand chariots, found it harder to retire to caves and wilds than a commoner. Though he had not yet attained the Dao, he could be said to have grasped its intent.

Confucius was beset between Chen and Cai, going seven days without cooked food, eating only a thin vegetable broth without grain. His complexion was utterly exhausted, yet he plucked his zither and sang in his chamber. Yan Hui was picking greens.

Zilu and Zigong said to each other: "The Master was twice driven from Lu, had his borders pared in Wei, had his tree felled in Song, was reduced to extremity in Shang and Zhou, was besieged between Chen and Cai. Those who would kill the Master bear no guilt; those who would trample him face no prohibition. Yet he plucks his zither and sings, never ceasing his music—is this how a gentleman is without shame?"

Yan Hui had no reply. He entered and told Confucius.

Confucius pushed aside his zither and sighed deeply: "You and Ci are petty men. Summon them, and I will speak to them."

Zilu and Zigong entered.

Zilu said: "This may be called utter destitution."
Confucius replied: "What words are these? The gentleman who is at one with the Dao is called 'at one'; he who is cut off from the Dao is called 'destitute.' Now I embrace the Dao of benevolence and righteousness and encounter the calamities of a disordered age—how can this be called destitution? Thus, though I examine myself thrice daily, I am never cut off from the Dao; facing peril, I do not lose my virtue. When bitter cold arrives and frost and snow descend, it is then I know the flourishing of the pine and cypress. The straits of Chen and Cai—are they not a blessing for me?"

Confucius then calmly returned to his zither and played and sang, while Zilu, suddenly inspired, grasped his shield and danced. Zigong said: "I did not know the height of heaven or the depth of earth. Those of old who attained the Dao found joy in adversity and joy in prosperity. What brought them joy was not adversity or prosperity, but the presence of virtue and power. Thus, adversity and prosperity became as the sequence of cold and heat, wind and rain. Therefore, Xu You found delight by the Ying River, and Gong Bo found fulfillment on Mount Qiu. Shun offered the world to his friend, the Northerner Wuze. The Northerner Wuze said: 'How strange the ruler’s conduct! Dwelling amid the fields and furrows, yet wandering at Yao’s gate. Not content with this, he now seeks to sully me with his shameful conduct. I am ashamed to see him.'"

He then cast himself into the limpid depths of the pool.

When Tang sought to overthrow Jie, he consulted Bian Sui, who said: "This is not my affair." Tang asked: "Who, then, is suitable?" He replied: "I do not know." Tang then consulted Wu Guang, who said: "This is not my affair." Tang asked: "Who, then, is suitable?" He replied: "I do not know." Tang said: "What of Yi Yin?" Wu Guang answered: "He is strong and enduring, patient of disgrace—I know nothing beyond this." Thereupon Tang conspired with Yi Yin to overthrow Jie, and having conquered him, offered the throne to Bian Sui.

Bian Sui declined, saying: "When you plotted against Jie, you consulted me—I must be deemed a traitor. Now that you have vanquished Jie and offer me the throne, I must be deemed greedy. Born in an age of chaos, a man without the Way has twice sought to sully me with his shameful conduct. I cannot bear to hear it again." He then cast himself into the Zhou River and drowned.

Tang next offered the throne to Wu Guang, saying: "The wise devise the plan, the valiant execute it, and the humane occupy the throne—this is the Way of old. Why do you not ascend?" Wu Guang declined, saying: "To depose one’s superior is not righteous. To kill the people is not humane. Others endure hardship while I enjoy the gain—this is not integrity. I have heard it said: 'One who does not act righteously does not accept its emoluments; in an age without the Way, one does not tread its soil.' How much less would I accept such honor? I cannot bear to remain here long."

He then carried stones on his back and drowned himself in the Lu River.

In the rise of the Zhou, there were two scholars from Guzhu named Boyi and Shuqi. The two conferred, saying: "We have heard that in the west there is a man who seems to follow the Way. Let us go and observe." Arriving at Qiyang, King Wu heard of them and sent Shu Dan to meet them, making a covenant: "I shall grant you wealth two ranks higher and appoint you to the first rank of office." They slaughtered an animal and buried it as an oath. The two looked at each other and laughed, saying: "Alas, how strange! This is not what we call the Way. In the days when Shennong ruled the world, sacrifices were made with utmost reverence yet without prayers for blessings. In his dealings with others, he was loyal and trustworthy, governing thoroughly yet without seeking anything. He delighted in governance and took joy in order, never using others’ ruin to build himself up, never exalting himself through others’ lowliness, never profiting from others’ misfortune. Now, the Zhou, seeing the disorder of Yin, hastily seizes power. They scheme above and bribe below, rely on military force to maintain authority, slaughter animals to swear oaths as a sign of trust, flaunt their conduct to please the masses, and resort to killing and warfare to seize profit. This is merely replacing one form of chaos with another. We have heard that the scholars of old, when encountering an age of order, did not shirk their duty, and when facing an age of chaos, did not seek mere survival. Now, the world is in turmoil, and the virtue of Zhou has declined. To align ourselves with Zhou would sully our persons—better to avoid it and preserve our conduct." The two then traveled north to Mount Shouyang, where they starved to death.

As for Boyi and Shuqi, when it came to wealth and honor, if they could be avoided, they would never rely on them. With lofty integrity and unyielding conduct, they alone delighted in their own aspirations, refusing to serve the world. Such was the resolve of these two scholars.

---

*The Southern Florescence Perfected Scripture*, Scroll Five: "Miscellaneous Chapters," Chapter Twenty-Nine: "Robber Display"

Confucius was a friend of Liuxia Ji. Liuxia Ji’s younger brother was named Robber Zhi. Robber Zhi led a band of nine thousand followers, rampaging across the land and bullying the feudal lords. They broke into homes, drove off people’s cattle and horses, and abducted their wives and daughters...

Robber Zhi was insatiably greedy, forgetting all kinship—he cared not for parents or brothers, nor did he offer sacrifices to his ancestors. Wherever his band passed, the great states fortified their cities, the small states took refuge in their strongholds, and the common people suffered bitterly.

Confucius said to Liuxia Ji, "As a father, one must be able to instruct his son; as an elder brother, one must be able to teach his younger brother. If a father cannot guide his son, or an elder brother cannot teach his younger brother, then the bond of father and son, elder and younger brother, is worthless. Now, sir, you are a man of talent in this age, yet your younger brother is Robber Zhi, a scourge upon the world, and you have not been able to teach him. I am secretly ashamed for you. Allow me to go and reason with him on your behalf."

Liuxia Ji replied, "You say that as a father one must instruct his son, and as an elder brother one must teach his younger brother. But if a son does not heed his father’s instruction, or a younger brother does not accept his elder brother’s teaching, what can even your eloquence accomplish? Moreover, Zhi’s nature is such that his mind surges like a spring, his thoughts rush like the whirlwind. His strength is sufficient to resist enemies, his eloquence enough to gloss over wrongs. He delights when his will is followed and rages when it is opposed. He is quick to insult others with words. You must not go."

Confucius would not listen. Yan Hui drove the carriage, and Zigong stood at his right as they went to meet Robber Zhi. Robber Zhi was then resting his troops on the southern slope of Mount Tai, mincing human livers to serve as his meal. Confucius alighted from the carriage and stepped forward. Addressing the attendant, he said, "I am Kong Qiu of Lu. Having heard of the general’s lofty virtue, I respectfully bow twice and request an audience."

The attendant went in to announce him. When Robber Zhi heard this, he flew into a great rage, his eyes blazing like stars, his hair bristling beneath his cap. He said, "Is this not that cunning and deceitful man Kong Qiu of Lu? Tell him for me: You fabricate words and invent phrases, falsely invoking the names of Kings Wen and Wu. You wear a cap of dead branches and gird yourself with the ribs of a dead ox. You prattle on with your deceptive speeches, eating without plowing, clothing yourself without weaving. You flap your lips and wag your tongue, stirring up right and wrong to delude the rulers of the world, leading the scholars of the age astray from their roots. You falsely perform filial piety and brotherly respect, hoping to gain enfeoffment and riches by luck. Your crimes are vast and your guilt extreme. Flee back at once—or else I shall add your liver to today’s feast."

Kong Qiu again sent word, saying, "I have found favor with the Ji family. I wish only to gaze upon your tent-flap." The attendant relayed this, and Robber Zhi said, "Let him come forward." Kong Qiu hastened in, stepped back from his mat, and bowed twice to Robber Zhi.

Robber Zhi flew into a rage, stretched out his legs, pressed his sword, glared with blazing eyes, and roared like a nursing tiger: "Qiu, come forward! If your words please me, you shall live; if they run counter to my heart, you shall die."

Kong Qiu said, "I have heard that in all the world there are three virtues: to be born and grow tall, surpassingly fair and peerless, so that young and old, noble and base, all who see you rejoice—this is the highest virtue. To comprehend the bounds of Heaven and Earth, to distinguish all things—this is the middle virtue. To be fierce, resolute, and bold, to gather multitudes and lead armies—this is the lowest virtue. Whoever possesses but one of these virtues is fit to face south and style himself a lord. Now, General, you combine all three: your body stands eight feet and two inches, your face and eyes radiant, your lips like stirred cinnabar, your teeth like aligned cowries, your voice attuned to the Yellow Bell. Yet you are called Robber Zhi—I privately feel shame for you that you do not claim what is yours. If you would heed me, I shall go south to Wu and Yue, north to Qi and Lu, east to Song and Wei, west to Jin and Chu, and have built for you a great city of several hundred *li*, with a town of several hundred thousand households. I shall exalt you as a feudal lord, make a fresh start with the world, disband your troops, rest your soldiers, gather and nurture your brothers, and together offer sacrifice to your ancestors. This is the conduct of a sage and a man of talent, and the world’s deepest wish."

Robber Zhi flew into a rage: "Qiu, come forward! Those who can be swayed by profit or admonished by words are but the dull and vulgar common folk. Now, to be tall and fair, so that all who see you rejoice—this is the legacy of my father and mother. Though you do not praise me, do I not know it myself? Moreover, I have heard that those who flatter others to their faces also slander them behind their backs. Now you tell me of great cities and teeming multitudes—this is to lure me with profit and treat me like a beast to be penned. How could such things last? Of all great cities, none is greater than the world itself. Yao and Shun possessed the world, yet their descendants had not a pin’s place to stand. Tang and Wu became Son of Heaven, yet their posterity perished utterly. Was this not because their profit was too great?

Furthermore, I have heard that in ancient times beasts were many and people few. Thus, people all dwelt in nests to avoid them, gathering acorns by day and roosting in trees by night—hence they were called the people of the Nest Builder. In ancient times, people knew not clothing; in summer they piled firewood, in winter they warmed themselves by it—hence they were called the people who knew how to live. In the age of Shen Nong, people lived at ease, rising and moving about freely. They knew their mothers but not their fathers, dwelt with elk and deer, plowed to eat, wove to clothe themselves, and harbored no thought of harming one another. This was the height of perfect virtue. Yet the Yellow Emperor could not sustain this virtue: he fought Chi You on the plains of Zhuolu, and blood flowed for a hundred *li*. Yao and Shun arose and established a host of ministers; Tang exiled his lord, King Wu slew Zhou. From that time onward, the strong oppressed the weak, the many bullied the few. From Tang and Wu to the present, all have been men of disorder.

Now you cultivate the Way of Wen and Wu, grasp the world’s eloquence, and teach posterity. You wear fine robes with narrow sashes, speak in affected tones, and act with false conduct to delude the world’s rulers, seeking thereby wealth and honor. There is no greater thief than you. Why does the world not call you Thief Qiu, instead of calling me Robber Zhi? You beguiled Zilu with sweet words to make him follow you, had him remove his martial cap and lay aside his long sword to receive your teachings. The world all says that Kong Qiu can quell violence and forbid wrongdoing. Yet in the end, Zilu sought to kill the lord of Wei and failed, and his body was impaled above the eastern gate of Wei. This was because your teaching was insufficient. Do you call yourself a man of talent and a sage? Then why were you twice driven from Lu, your traces erased in Wei, impoverished in Qi, besieged in Chen and Cai, and left with no place in the world? You taught Zilu to meet this disaster, leaving him neither honor above nor humanity below. Is your Way truly worthy of esteem?

Of all those the world exalts, none is greater than the Yellow Emperor, yet even he could not preserve his virtue intact. He fought on the plains of Zhuolu, and blood flowed for a hundred *li*. Yao was unkind, Shun was unfilial, Yu was crippled on one side, Tang exiled his lord, King Wu attacked Zhou, King Wen was imprisoned at Youli. These six men are those the world exalts, yet when one examines them, all were deluded by profit and forced their true natures to rebel against their inborn dispositions. Their conduct was most shameful indeed.

Of those the world calls worthy men, none surpass Boyi and Shuqi. Boyi and Shuqi renounced the lordship of Guzhu and starved to death on Mount Shouyang, their flesh and bones left unburied. Bao Jiao adorned his conduct to condemn the age and embraced a tree to die. Shentu Di remonstrated but was not heeded, carried a stone and cast himself into the river, where he was eaten by fish and turtles. Jie Zitui was most loyal: he cut flesh from his own thigh to feed Duke Wen. Later, Duke Wen betrayed him, and in anger he departed, embraced a tree, and burned to death. Weisheng made a tryst with a woman beneath a bridge. When she did not come and the waters rose, he would not leave, embraced the bridge pillar, and drowned. These six men were no different from stray dogs, drifting swine, or beggars clutching gourds. All pursued fame and scorned death, heedless of their roots, nurturing life, and preserving their inborn nature.

Of those the world calls loyal ministers, none surpass Prince Bigan and Wu Zixu. Zixu was drowned in the river, Bigan was disemboweled—these two are called loyal ministers, yet in the end they were laughed at by the world. From the highest perspective, down to Zixu and Bigan, none are worthy of esteem."

Confucius said to me: "If you speak to me of ghostly matters, I cannot know them; but if you speak to me of human affairs, they do not exceed these—all are things I have heard and understood. Now I shall tell you of human nature: the eyes desire to see beauty, the ears to hear sound, the mouth to savor flavors, and the will and qi seek fulfillment. A man may live a long life of a hundred years, a middling life of eighty, or a short life of sixty; yet if we subtract days lost to illness, emaciation, mourning, death, and sorrow, the days in which he opens his mouth to laugh number no more than four or five in a month. Heaven and earth are without end, but man’s death comes at a fixed time. To grasp a vessel of fixed duration and entrust it to the boundless void is to pass like a swift steed galloping past a crack in the wall—suddenly, in an instant. Those who cannot delight their will and nurture their lifespan are not masters of the Dao.

All that I have said, you must cast aside. Hurry, leave, and return home—do not speak of it again. Your Way is wild and erratic, full of deceit, cunning, and falsity—it cannot preserve the true. What is there to discuss?"

Confucius bowed twice, then hastened away. He exited the gate, mounted his carriage, and three times lost hold of the reins. His eyes were dazed and sightless, his complexion ashen like the dead. He leaned against the crossbar, head bowed, unable to catch his breath. Upon returning to the eastern gate of Lu, he chanced upon Liuxia Ji.

Liuxia Ji said: "I have not seen you for several days, and your carriage and horses bear signs of travel. Might you have gone to see Jue?"

Confucius looked up to the heavens and sighed: "Indeed."

Liuxia Ji said: "Did he perhaps go against your intentions as before?"

Confucius said: "Indeed. I was like a man without illness who moxibusts himself—running swiftly to measure a tiger’s head or braid its whiskers, nearly escaping not from the tiger’s jaws!"

Zizhang asked Mang Goude: "Why do you not act?"

"Without action, there is no trust; without trust, no responsibility; without responsibility, no benefit. Thus, when one observes reputation and calculates profit, righteousness is truly at stake. If one abandons fame and gain and turns inward to the heart, then the scholar’s conduct cannot be neglected for even a day."

Mang Goude said: "The shameless grow rich, and the deceitful gain prominence. The greatest of fame and profit lie in shamelessness and deceit. Thus, when one observes reputation and calculates profit, trust is truly at stake. If one abandons fame and gain and turns inward to the heart, then the scholar’s conduct embraces the celestial."

Zizhang said: "In ancient times, Jie and Zhou were honored as Sons of Heaven and possessed all under heaven. Yet if today you tell a man of Zang or Ju, 'Your conduct is like Jie or Zhou,' he will flush with anger and feel indignant—such is the contempt of petty men. Confucius and Mo Di were impoverished commoners, yet if today you tell a minister, 'Your conduct is like Confucius or Mo Di,' he will change his expression and protest his inadequacy—such is the true esteem of the scholar. Thus, to be a Son of Heaven does not necessarily confer nobility, nor does poverty as a commoner necessarily confer baseness. The distinction between noble and base lies in the beauty or ugliness of one’s conduct."

Mang Goude said: "Petty thieves are imprisoned, but great thieves become lords; and at the gates of lords, righteous scholars take refuge. In the past, Duke Huan of Qi, Xiaobai, killed his brother and took his sister-in-law, yet Guan Zhong served as his minister. Tian Chengzi murdered his lord and usurped the state, yet Confucius accepted his gifts. In speech, they are condemned; in action, they are deferred to—is this not a contradiction in the heart, a perversion? Thus, the Book says: 'Who is base? Who is noble? The successful are heads, the unsuccessful are tails.'

Zizhang said: "If you do not act, then kinship and affection will lack order, noble and base will lack righteousness, and elder and younger will lack sequence. How then can the Five Constants and Six Positions be distinguished?"

Mang Goude said: "Yao killed his eldest son, Shun exiled his younger brother—where is the order in kinship? Tang exiled Jie, King Wu killed Zhou—where is the righteousness in noble and base? King Ji was the rightful heir, yet the Duke of Zhou killed his brother—where is the sequence in elder and younger? The Confucians speak falsely, the Mohists preach universal love—how can the Five Constants and Six Positions be distinguished?

Moreover, you rectify for fame, I rectify for profit. The reality of fame and profit does not accord with principle, nor does it reflect the Dao. Daily I debate with you before Wu Yue, saying: 'The petty man dies for wealth, the gentleman dies for fame.' Though their emotions and natures differ in what they change and alter, when it comes to abandoning what they do for what they do not do, they are the same. Thus it is said: Do not be a petty man, turn back and die for the celestial. Do not be a gentleman, follow the principle of Heaven. Whether crooked or straight, align with the celestial pole; face the four directions and ebb and flow with the times. Whether right or wrong, grasp the round mechanism, stand alone in intent, and wander with the Dao. Do not act for gain, do not establish righteousness, lest you lose what you seek. Do not rush toward wealth, do not die for success, lest you abandon the celestial.

Bigan was disemboweled, Wu Zixu had his eyes gouged out—such are the calamities of loyalty. Zhigong testified against his father, Weisheng drowned—such are the perils of trust. Master Bao stood firm, Shenzi did not defend himself—such are the harms of integrity. Confucius did not see his mother, Kuangzi did not see his father—such are the failures of righteousness. These are the traditions of the upper ages and the talk of later generations. For scholars who rectify their words and verify their conduct, they bring upon themselves disaster and calamity."

Wuzu asked Zhihe: "There has never been a man who did not pursue fame and profit. The wealthy attract followers; followers defer to them; deference brings esteem. To be deferred to and esteemed is the way to long life, bodily ease, and joyful contentment. Why do you alone have no such intention? Is your knowledge insufficient? Or do you know but lack the strength to act? Or do you cling to some other principle?"

Zhihe said: "Now, those who consider themselves contemporaries and neighbors regard those who transcend custom and surpass the world as..."

This is to be without a guiding principle, by which one surveys the ages of past and present and distinguishes right from wrong, yet still drifts with the world’s transformations. They cast aside what is most weighty and abandon what is most honorable, all for the sake of their own pursuits. How far this is from the Way of long life, bodily ease, and joyful contentment! The pain of sorrowful illness and the peace of serene delight are not reflected in the body; the fear of constant dread and the joy of heartfelt delight are not reflected in the mind. They know how to act but not why they act—thus, though they are honored as Son of Heaven and possess all under Heaven, they cannot escape calamity."

Wuzu said: "Wealth brings every advantage to man. It exhausts beauty and exhausts power, reaching what even the most perfect cannot attain and what the worthiest cannot match. It harnesses the strength and courage of the bold to create might, wields the wisdom and schemes of others to achieve clarity, and adopts the virtue of others to appear worthy and good. Without even holding office, one commands reverence like a sovereign or father. Moreover, the pleasures of sound, color, flavor, and power—these delight the heart without need of learning, and the body finds comfort in them without need of imitation. Desires and aversions, what to seek and what to avoid, are innate and require no teacher: this is the nature of man. Though all under Heaven may condemn them, who can refuse them?"

Zhihe said: "The actions of the wise are thus: they move in accord with the people, never transgressing proper measure. Therefore, they are content and do not contend, and for no purpose do they seek. Those who lack seek, and though they contend in all directions, they do not consider themselves greedy. Those who have surplus decline, casting aside all under Heaven without considering themselves modest. The true nature of modesty and greed is not imposed from without but lies in observing proper measure. Though one holds the position of Son of Heaven, one does not use nobility to intimidate others; though one possesses all under Heaven, one does not use wealth to toy with others. Calculating the harm and considering the consequences, one sees that it harms one’s nature, and so one declines and does not accept—not to seek fame and reputation. Yao and Shun ruled as emperors and brought harmony, not to benevolently govern all under Heaven, but to avoid letting beauty harm their lives. Shanjuan and Xu You were offered the throne but refused, not out of empty humility, but to avoid letting affairs harm themselves. All of them pursued what benefited them and declined what harmed them. Though all under Heaven called them worthy, they could have accepted, but they did not do so for the sake of fame and reputation."

Wuzu said: "If one must cling to reputation, endure bodily hardship, reject sweetness, and restrict nourishment to preserve life, then one is no better than a long-suffering invalid who lingers on without dying."

Zhihe said: "Equilibrium brings fortune, while excess brings harm—this is true of all things, and wealth is the most extreme case. Now, the rich man’s ears are filled with the sounds of bells, drums, pipes, and flutes; his mouth savors the flavors of fine meats and sweet wines, distracting his mind and causing him to neglect his affairs—this may be called disorder. Engulfed in surging qi, he moves as if burdened with a heavy load while climbing—this may be called suffering. Greedy for wealth, he takes comfort in it; greedy for power, he exhausts himself. At rest, he is drowned in it; in motion, he is overwhelmed by it—this may be called illness. Driven by the desire for wealth and the pursuit of profit, he is filled to the ears yet does not know to avoid it, pressing forward without cease—this may be called disgrace. Wealth accumulates without use; he clings to it without release, his heart filled with sorrow and bitterness, endlessly seeking more—this may be called worry. Within, he suspects the treachery of those who flatter and beg; without, he fears the harm of bandits and thieves. He surrounds himself with high towers and deep moats and dares not travel alone—this may be called fear. These six are the greatest harms under Heaven, yet all are forgotten and unexamined. When calamity strikes, one seeks to exhaust one’s nature and deplete one’s wealth, hoping in vain to return to a single day of carefree ease. Thus, when one gazes upon fame, it is not seen; when one seeks profit, it is not obtained. Yet one wearies the mind and wears out the body in contention for these—is this not delusion?"

---

*The Southern Florescence: The True Classic, Scroll Five, Miscellaneous Chapters – "Discourse on Swords"*

Long ago, King Wen of Zhao delighted in swords. Over three thousand swordsmen gathered as retainers at his gates, clashing before him day and night. Each year, more than a hundred died or were wounded, yet his passion never waned. After three years of this, the state declined, and the feudal lords plotted against it. The crown prince, Li, grew anxious and offered a reward to his attendants: "Whoever can persuade the king to abandon his fondness for swordsmen shall receive a thousand gold pieces." His attendants said, "Zhuangzi is the one who can do this."

The crown prince then sent messengers bearing a thousand gold pieces to present to Zhuangzi. Zhuangzi refused the gift but accompanied the messengers to see the crown prince. He asked, "What instruction does the crown prince have for me, that you offer me a thousand gold pieces?" The crown prince replied, "I have heard that you, Master, are enlightened and sagely. I humbly present this thousand gold as a gift for your attendants. If you refuse, how dare I speak further?" Zhuangzi said, "I have heard that the crown prince wishes to employ me to put an end to the king’s fondness for swords. If I persuade the king but fail to align with his wishes, or if I do not meet the crown prince’s expectations, I will face execution. How then could I concern myself with gold? If I persuade the king and meet the crown prince’s expectations, what in the state of Zhao could I not obtain?"

The crown prince said, "Indeed. My father, the king, sees only swordsmen." Zhuangzi replied, "Very well. I am skilled in the use of the sword." The crown prince said, "But the swordsmen my father sees all have unkempt hair, bristling sideburns, and drooping caps with coarse tassels. Their robes are short in the back, their eyes glower, and their speech is harsh. The king delights in them. If you, Master, appear before him in scholar’s robes, the matter will surely go awry." Zhuangzi said, "I request to prepare a swordsman’s attire." After three days of preparing the attire, he met with the crown prince, who then accompanied him to see the king. The king drew his unsheathed sword and waited.

Zhuangzi entered the palace gates without haste and did not bow upon seeing the king. The king said, "What instruction do you bring me, that the crown prince has gone to such lengths?" Zhuangzi replied, "I have heard that Your Majesty delights in swords, and so I have come to present my swordsmanship." The king asked, "What can your sword do?" Zhuangzi said, "My sword can cut down one man every ten paces, and its edge never falters for a thousand li."

The king was greatly pleased and said, "You would be unmatched in all under Heaven!" Zhuangzi replied, "As for the sword, one displays emptiness, offers advantage, follows with the strike, and arrives first. I wish to demonstrate this." The king said, "Master, rest at your lodging and await my command. I shall arrange a contest and summon you." The king then assembled swordsmen for seven days; over sixty were killed or wounded, and five or six remained. These were ordered to present their swords at the palace steps, and Zhuangzi was summoned. The king said, "Today I shall have the swordsmen engage in a trial of skill." Zhuangzi said, "I have long awaited this." The king asked, "What length of weapon does the Master wield?" Zhuangzi replied, "Any I am given will suffice. Yet I possess three swords—only Your Majesty may choose which to employ. Permit me first to describe them, then to demonstrate."

The king said, "I wish to hear of the three swords."
Zhuangzi said, "There is the Sword of the Son of Heaven, the Sword of the Feudal Lords, and the Sword of the Commoner."

The king asked, "What is the Sword of the Son of Heaven like?"
Zhuangzi replied, "The Sword of the Son of Heaven has its edge in Yanxi and Shicheng, its guard in Mount Tai of Qi, its spine in Jin and Wei, its base in Zhou and Song, and its blade in Han and Wei. It is wrapped in the Four Barbarians, enveloped by the Four Seasons, encircled by the Bohai Sea, and girded by Mount Chang. It is governed by the Five Phases, judged by punishment and virtue, opened by yin and yang, sustained by spring and summer, and wielded in autumn and winter."

This sword, when thrust forward, knows no rival; when raised, nothing stands above it; when pressed downward, nothing lies beneath it; when swung, nothing flanks it. Above, it cleaves the floating clouds; below, it severs the earth’s foundations. Once this sword is wielded, it rectifies the feudal lords, and all under heaven submits. Such is the Sword of the Son of Heaven.

King Wen, bewildered, lost his composure and asked, "What of the Sword of the Feudal Lords?"
Zhuangzi replied, "The Sword of the Feudal Lords has its edge in the wise and brave, its guard in the pure and incorrupt, its spine in the worthy and good, its base in the loyal and victorious, and its blade in the heroic and outstanding. This sword, when thrust forward, likewise knows no rival; when raised, nothing stands above it; when pressed downward, nothing lies beneath it; when swung, nothing flanks it. Above, it models the round heavens to follow the Three Luminaries; below, it models the square earth to follow the Four Seasons; within, it discerns the people’s will to pacify the four quarters. Once this sword is wielded, it strikes like thunder, and within the four borders, none fail to submit and heed their lord’s command. Such is the Sword of the Feudal Lords."

The king asked, "What of the Sword of the Commoner?"
Zhuangzi replied, "The Sword of the Commoner is wielded by those with disheveled hair, unkempt sideburns, drooping caps, frayed tassels, short-backed robes, glaring eyes, and contentious speech. They clash before one another, severing necks above and piercing livers and lungs below. This sword of the commoner differs not from fighting cocks—once their lives are spent, they serve no purpose in the affairs of state. Now, Your Majesty, possessing the station of the Son of Heaven, yet favoring the sword of the commoner—I humbly deem this unworthy of you."
