The king then led Zhuangzi up to the hall. The royal steward presented the feast, and the king circled the table thrice. Zhuangzi said, "Your Majesty, be seated and compose yourself. The matter of the swords has been presented in full."

Thereupon, King Wen did not leave his palace for three months, and the swordsmen all perished in their quarters.

---

From *The True Classic of Southern Florescence*, Scroll Five, "Miscellaneous Chapters: The Fisherman, Thirty-One"

Confucius wandered through the forest of black curtains and rested upon the secluded altar. His disciples read aloud while Confucius strummed the zither and sang. Before the melody was half-finished, an old fisherman descended from his boat and approached. His temples and brows were white, his hair unbound, his sleeves tattered. He strode up from the marsh, halted at the shore, left hand resting on his knee, right hand cradling his chin, and listened. When the song ended, he beckoned to Zilu and Zigong, who both stepped forward.

The stranger pointed at Confucius and asked, "What is that man doing?"
Zilu replied, "He is a gentleman of Lu."
The stranger asked of his lineage.
Zilu answered, "The lineage of Kong."
The stranger asked, "And what does this Kong lineage practice?"
Zilu did not respond, but Zigong answered, "The Kong lineage cultivates loyalty and trust in their nature, embodies benevolence and righteousness in their conduct, refines ritual and music, and selects human relationships. Above, they serve the rulers of the age with loyalty; below, they transform the common people, seeking to benefit all under heaven. Such is the practice of the Kong lineage."

The stranger asked again, "Is he a lord with lands?"
Zigong said, "No."
"A counselor to kings and marquises?"
Zigong said, "No."
The stranger laughed and turned away, saying as he walked, "Benevolent, to be sure—but I fear he will not escape harm to himself. He wearies his mind and wears his body, endangering his true nature. Alas, how far he has strayed from the Dao!"

Zigong returned and reported this to Confucius. Confucius pushed aside his zither and rose, saying, "Is he a sage?" He descended to seek the man, reaching the marsh’s edge just as the fisherman was about to pole his boat away. Glancing back, he saw Confucius, turned toward him, and stood still. Confucius retreated, bowed twice, and stepped forward.

The stranger asked, "What do you seek?"
Confucius replied, "Earlier, sir, you left with words unfinished. I, Qiu, am unworthy and did not grasp their meaning. I humbly awaited in your wake, hoping to hear even the sound of your cough or spit to complete my instruction."

The stranger said, "Ah! How earnest is your love of learning!"
Confucius bowed twice and rose, saying, "From my youth I have cultivated learning, and now, at sixty-nine, I have yet to hear the ultimate teaching. I dare not but remain open-hearted."

The stranger said, "Like attracts like, and like sounds resonate—this is the principle of heaven. Allow me to expound what I possess and apply it to your concerns. What you concern yourself with are human affairs. The Son of Heaven, feudal lords, great officers, and commoners—when these four are properly aligned, governance is at its finest. If these four abandon their stations, no greater disorder exists. When offices fulfill their duties and people attend to their tasks, there is no encroachment. Thus:

For the commoner, the worries are: fields lying fallow, homes exposed to the elements, clothing and food insufficient, taxes unpaid, discord between wife and concubine, and disorder between elders and juniors.

For the great officer, the worries are: incompetence in office, official duties neglected, conduct impure, subordinates idle, achievements unrecognized, and ranks and emoluments unstable.

For the feudal lord, the worries are: no loyal ministers in court, the state in chaos, craftsmen lacking skill, tribute unrefined, falling behind in spring and autumn ceremonies, and disobeying the Son of Heaven.

For the Son of Heaven’s ministers, the worries are: yin and yang out of harmony, cold and heat untimely, harming the myriad things; feudal lords violent and disorderly, seizing and attacking one another, ravaging the people; ritual and music unrestrained, resources exhausted, human relationships unadorned, and the hundred clans licentious.

Now, you, sir, possess neither the authority of a lord or minister above nor the office of a great officer below, yet you presume to refine ritual and music, select human relationships, and transform the common people. Is this not overreaching?

Moreover, people have eight flaws, and affairs have four perils—these cannot be left unexamined.

To attend to what is not one’s affair is called *meddling*;
To advance where none heed is called *obsequiousness*;
To guide speech by others’ expectations is called *flattery*;
To speak without discerning right or wrong is called *sycophancy*;
To delight in speaking of others’ faults is called *slander*;
To sever friendships and divide kin is called *treachery*;
To praise falsely and deceive to harm others is called *connivance*;
To accept both good and bad without distinction, flattering both sides to advance one’s desires, is called *perfidy*."

These eight flaws disrupt others outwardly and harm oneself inwardly—noble persons do not befriend such men, and enlightened rulers do not employ them.

As for the four perils:
To delight in managing great affairs, altering and overturning constants in pursuit of merit and fame is called *presumption*;
To monopolize knowledge, arrogate authority, and encroach upon others through self-assertion is called *greed*;
To persist in error despite correction, and grow more obstinate when admonished, is called *obduracy*;
To approve only what aligns with oneself, and dismiss even the good if it differs, is called *arrogance*.
These are the four perils.

Only by shedding the eight flaws and avoiding the four perils can one begin to receive instruction.

Confucius sighed deeply, bowed twice, and rose, saying: "I was twice exiled from Lu, had my lands pared away in Wei, was felled like a tree in Song, and besieged between Chen and Cai. I know not what faults I committed to incur these four slanders—why was this?"

The guest’s expression turned somber as he replied: "How deeply you resist awakening! There was a man who feared his shadow and loathed his footprints, and so ran to escape them. The more he lifted his feet, the more prints appeared; the faster he ran, the closer his shadow clung. Believing himself still too slow, he ran without cease until he collapsed and died. He did not know that standing in shade would still his shadow, or that stillness would erase his prints—how utterly foolish!

You scrutinize the questions of benevolence and righteousness, examine the boundaries of sameness and difference, observe the shifts of activity and stillness, adjust the measures of giving and receiving, order the passions of like and dislike, and harmonize the rhythms of joy and anger—yet you nearly escape none of this. If you diligently cultivate yourself, guard your authenticity, and return things to others, you will be free from entanglements. To neglect self-cultivation while seeking from others is to miss the mark entirely."

Confucius sighed again and asked: "May I ask what is meant by *authenticity*?"

The guest replied: "Authenticity is the utmost of sincerity. Without sincerity, one cannot move others. Thus, forced weeping, though sorrowful, lacks grief; forced anger, though stern, lacks awe; forced affection, though smiling, lacks harmony. True grief is silent yet mournful; true anger is unexpressed yet awe-inspiring; true affection is unsmiling yet harmonious. When authenticity resides within, the spirit manifests without—this is why authenticity is prized.

Applied to human affairs:
In serving parents, it is *filial devotion*;
In serving rulers, it is *loyal integrity*;
In drinking, it is *joy*;
In mourning, it is *grief*.
Loyal integrity is measured by merit; drinking, by joy; mourning, by grief; serving parents, by suitability. When merit is perfected, no single path is prescribed.

Serving parents with suitability requires no discussion of means;
Drinking with joy requires no selection of vessels;
Mourning with grief requires no inquiry into ritual.
Ritual is what世俗 constructs; authenticity is what one receives from Heaven—natural and unalterable. Thus, the sage models Heaven and values authenticity, unbound by convention. The foolish do the opposite: unable to model Heaven, they fret over others, ignorant of authenticity’s worth, drifting with俗 and thus never sufficient. Alas, you were steeped in human artifice early on and only now hear of the Great Dao!"

Confucius bowed twice and rose again: "Today I have met you as if by Heaven’s grace. You deign to instruct me, though I am no better than a servant. May I ask where you dwell, that I might follow and complete my study of the Great Dao?"

The guest said: "I have heard that those who can journey with you may be led to the subtleties of the Dao; those who cannot, do not grasp the Dao—beware of sharing it with them, lest you bring harm upon yourself. Strive on! I leave you now, I leave you now." With that, he poled his boat away, vanishing among the green reeds.

Yan Yuan turned the carriage, and Zilu handed Confucius the reins, but Confucius did not look back. He waited until the water’s ripples stilled and no sound of oars could be heard before daring to board. Zilu stepped beside the carriage and asked: "I have long served you, yet never seen you meet another with such reverence. Kings of ten thousand chariots and lords of a thousand have met you without failing to observe proper courtesy, yet you still bore a haughty air. Now this old fisherman, leaning on his pole, stood upright before you, and you bent at the waist like a chiming stone, bowing repeatedly in response—was this not excessive? Your disciples all find it strange. How did the fisherman deserve such deference?"

Confucius leaned on the crossbar and sighed: "How difficult you are to transform, Zilu! You have been steeped in ritual and righteousness for so long, yet your coarse and vulgar heart remains. Come closer, and I will tell you: To meet an elder without respect is a breach of ritual; to meet a worthy without reverence is a lack of benevolence. If he were not a perfected person, he could not humble himself before others. Without humbling oneself with precision, one cannot attain authenticity—and thus risks lasting harm to the self. Alas, nothing is more disastrous to a person than a lack of benevolence, and you alone cling to it!

Moreover, the Dao is the path by which all things proceed. Creatures that lose it die; those that attain it live. In affairs, opposing it brings failure; following it brings success. Thus, wherever the Dao resides, the sage reveres it. This old fisherman possesses the Dao—how could I not revere him?"

He replied: "I once dined at ten millet-porridge stalls, and at five of them, the porridge was served first."

Berwyn Puren said: "If that is so, then why are you alarmed?"

He answered: "When inner sincerity is unresolved, outward demeanor radiates an imposing light, which overawes others’ hearts, making them disregard age and rank while magnifying their concerns. Those porridge-sellers merely peddle their wares for profit—meager gains, trifling influence—and yet even they behave so. How much more so, then, for a ruler of ten thousand chariots? If I exhaust myself for the state and expend all my knowledge on affairs, he will entrust me with tasks and demand results from me. That is why I am alarmed."

Berwyn Puren said: "Excellent observation! If you conduct yourself thus, others will safeguard you."

Shortly after, he went to visit, only to find the courtyard filled with shoes outside the door. Berwyn Puren stood facing north, leaning on his staff and pressing it against his chin. After standing for a while, he departed without a word. The attendant informed Liezi, who rushed out in haste, shoes in hand, and caught up at the gate, saying: "Master, since you have come, will you not prescribe a remedy?"

He replied: "Enough. I already told you that others would safeguard you—and indeed, they do. It is not that you can make others protect you, but that you cannot prevent them from doing so. Why then do you seek to impress or display your uniqueness? If you must stir emotions and disrupt your fundamental nature, it is meaningless. Those who accompany you in your wanderings will not warn you: their petty words are all poison. Unaware and unenlightened, how can they scrutinize one another? The cunning toil, the wise fret, the incapable seek nothing. Eat your fill and roam freely, drifting like an untethered boat, empty and unbound in your wanderings."

In Zheng, there was a man named Huan who groaned and wailed in the land of Qiu Shi. After three years, Huan became a Confucian scholar. His virtue moistened the land for nine *li*, and his beneficence extended to three clans, even converting his younger brother to Mohism. The Confucians and Mohists debated, and their father sided with Mo Di. Ten years later, Huan took his own life. His father dreamed of him, saying: "It was I who made your son a Mohist. Why did you not examine his true nature? Once he became the fruit of an autumn paulownia, what could be done?"

The Creator’s retribution does not punish the person but the Heaven within the person. Thus, it is Heaven that enables them. Those who deem themselves exceptional, distinct from others, and despise their kin are like the people of Qi who fought over well-water. Hence, it is said: "In this age, all are like Huan." From this, those with virtue remain unaware—how much more so those who embody the Dao?

In ancient times, this was called "the punishment of fleeing from Heaven." The sage finds peace in what is truly peaceful and does not find peace in what is not; the multitude finds peace in what is not peaceful and does not find peace in what is. Zhuangzi said: "To know the Dao is easy; to refrain from speaking of it is difficult. To know and not speak—this is how one aligns with Heaven. To know and speak—this is how one aligns with man." The people of old followed Heaven and not man.

Zhu Hongman studied the art of dragon-slaying from Zhi Liyi, exhausting a thousand gold pieces from his family. After three years, his skill was perfected, yet there was no use for his craft. The sage takes the inevitable as not inevitable, thus having no need for weapons; the multitude takes the not inevitable as inevitable, thus possessing many weapons. Yielding to weapons, their actions become demands. Weapons, once relied upon, bring ruin.

The petty man’s knowledge does not extend beyond bundled firewood and bamboo slips. He exhausts his spirit in trivial pursuits, yet seeks to guide and transform all things, to embody the Great Unity and the formless void. Such a one is deluded by the cosmos, burdened by form, and unaware of the Great Beginning. The perfected person, however, returns their spirit to the beginningless and delights in the realm of Nothing Whatever. Water flows without form, issuing forth into the Great Purity.

Alas! You take wisdom to reside in the finest details yet do not know the Great Peace.

There was a man of Song named Cao Shang who was sent by the King of Song as an envoy to Qin. On his departure, he received several carriages; the King of Qin was pleased with him and added a hundred more. Returning to Song, he saw Zhuangzi and said: "To dwell in a cramped lane, impoverished and weaving sandals, with a withered neck and sallow face—this is what I was unskilled at. To awaken the lord of ten thousand chariots and follow with a hundred carriages—this is what I excel at."

Zhuangzi replied: "The King of Qin had an illness and summoned physicians. Those who lanced boils and drained abscesses received one carriage; those who licked his hemorrhoids received five. The baser the treatment, the more carriages they received. Did you, perchance, treat his hemorrhoids? How else could you have obtained so many carriages? Begone!"

Duke Ai of Lu asked Yan Wen, saying: "I regard Confucius as the pillar of the state—will the realm be secure?"

Yan Wen replied: "It is perilous, verging on collapse! Confucius adorns himself with plumes and ornaments, busies himself with florid speech, takes branches for the root, and suppresses his nature to impress the people, yet he is neither trusted nor believed. He receives from the heart and governs by the spirit—how could such a one be fit to lead the people? Is he truly suited to you? Do you truly approve of him?"

To err may yet be permissible. Now, if the people are led to abandon substance for falsity, this is no way to guide them. Considering posterity, it would be better to cease such practices. Governance is difficult—what is imposed upon others yet never forgotten is not the spontaneous bounty of Heaven. Merchants and traders are held in contempt; even when ranked among others by office, the spirit does not acknowledge them. External punishments are meted out by metal and wood; internal punishments arise from movement and transgression. When petty men incur external punishments, metal and wood interrogate them; when they incur internal punishments, yin and yang consume them. Only the true person can escape both external and internal punishments.

Confucius said: "The human heart is more treacherous than mountains and rivers, harder to fathom than Heaven. Heaven at least has its seasons—spring, autumn, winter, summer—and its fixed times of dawn and dusk. But humans conceal their true nature beneath thick appearances and deep emotions. Thus, some appear earnest yet are deceitful; some seem long-suffering yet are unworthy; some are compliant out of fear yet achieve success; some are resolute yet lax; some are slow yet sharp. Therefore, those who rush toward righteousness as if parched may flee from it as if scorched. Thus, the noble person employs them at a distance to observe their loyalty, employs them closely to observe their reverence, employs them in complexity to observe their ability, questions them abruptly to observe their wisdom, sets urgent deadlines to observe their trustworthiness, entrusts them with wealth to observe their benevolence, warns them of danger to observe their integrity, intoxicates them with wine to observe their restraint, and mingles with them in various settings to observe their demeanor. When these nine tests are complete, the unworthy are revealed.

"Zheng Kaofu, upon receiving his first appointment, bowed low; upon his second, bent at the waist; upon his third, stooped to the ground, creeping along the wall—who would dare not follow the proper way? But those like him, upon their first appointment, strut like Lü Ju; upon their second, dance atop their carriages; upon their third, address their elders by name—who would align with Tang or Xu?

"There is no greater harm than virtue with intent, and intent with eyes. When intent has eyes, it turns inward; turning inward, it fails. There are five baleful virtues, and the foremost is the virtue of the center. What is the virtue of the center? It is when one prides oneself on what one favors and disparages what one does not do.

"Poverty has eight extremes, success three certainties, and the body six repositories. Beauty, whiskers, height, size, strength, loveliness, courage, and boldness—when all eight surpass others, they lead to ruin. Humility, yielding, and timidity that fall short of others—these three lead to success. Wisdom and intelligence that reach outward breed resentment; courage and boldness invite many grudges; benevolence and righteousness incur many demands.

"Those who grasp the essence of life are like puppets; those who grasp knowledge are diminished; those who grasp the great mandate follow it; those who grasp the small mandate encounter it.

"A man who had an audience with the King of Song was granted ten carriages, with which he proudly confronted Zhuangzi. Zhuangzi said: 'By the river lived a poor family who made their living weaving rushes. Their son, diving into the depths, found a pearl worth a thousand gold pieces. His father said to him: "Bring a stone to smash it!" For a pearl worth a thousand gold pieces must lie beneath the chin of the black dragon in the ninefold abyss. You could only have obtained it because the dragon was asleep. Had the black dragon awakened, what scrap of you would remain? Now, the depths of the state of Song are not merely a ninefold abyss; the ferocity of the King of Song is not merely that of a black dragon. You could only have obtained those carriages because he was asleep. Had the King of Song awakened, you would be reduced to dust.'

"Another man boasted to Zhuangzi, who replied to his messenger: 'Have you seen the sacrificial ox? It is clothed in embroidered robes and fed with fine grain and beans. Yet when it is led into the great temple, though it might wish to be a lone calf, could it ever be so?'

"As Zhuangzi neared death, his disciples wished to give him a lavish burial. Zhuangzi said: 'I take heaven and earth as my inner and outer coffins, the sun and moon as my paired jades, the stars and constellations as my pearls and beads, and the myriad things as my funeral gifts. Are my burial preparations not already complete? What could be added to them?' His disciples said:"

"‘I fear the crows and kites will feed upon you, Master.’
Zhuangzi said: ‘Above, I would be food for crows and kites; below, I would be food for mole-crickets and ants. To snatch from one to give to the other—how partial you are! To level with what is not level is to make leveling itself unlevel; to verify with what is not verifiable is to make verification itself unverifiable. The enlightened are but servants of the numinous; the numinous verifies them. That the enlightened cannot surpass the numinous has long been so—yet fools rely on what they see to impose upon others. Their merit lies in externals—is this not lamentable?’

*Southern Florescence Perfected Scripture, Scroll Five: "The World" (Miscellaneous Chapters, Chapter Thirty-Three)*

The world’s cultivators of methods and arts are many, each believing their achievements cannot be surpassed. Where, then, does the ancient *Dao*-art reside? It resides nowhere that it is not. How does the numinous descend? How does enlightenment emerge? The sage is born of it; the king is perfected by it—all originate from the One.

Not departing from the Source is called the Celestial Person.
Not departing from the essence is called the Spirit Person.
Not departing from the real is called the Perfected Person.
Taking heaven as their source, virtue as their root, and the Dao as their gate, foreshadowing transformation—this is the sage.
Taking benevolence as kindness, righteousness as principle, ritual as conduct, and music as harmony, suffused with compassion—this is the noble person.
Taking law as division, names as markers, comparison as verification, and examination as decision—such are the numbers one, two, three, four, by which the hundred officials rank themselves.
Taking affairs as constancy, clothing and food as primary, multiplying and storing, caring for the old, weak, orphaned, and widowed—all have their means of nurture: this is the governance of the people.

The ancients were complete in this. They matched the numinous and bright, harmonized heaven and earth, nourished the myriad things, brought harmony to the world, and extended their blessings to the hundred clans. Illuminating the root and the numbers, connecting to the branches and measures, penetrating the six directions and opening the four gates, whether small or great, refined or coarse, their workings were nowhere absent.

What is manifest in numbers and measures—old laws and transmitted histories—still abound. What lies in the *Odes*, *Documents*, *Rites*, and *Music*—scholars of Zou and Lu, and girdle-wearing gentlemen—many can expound. The *Odes* convey the will; the *Documents* convey affairs; the *Rites* convey conduct; the *Music* conveys harmony; the *Changes* convey yin and yang; the *Spring and Autumn Annals* convey names and duties.

These arts spread throughout the world and were established in the Central States, where the hundred schools at times praised and transmitted them. Yet when the world fell into great disorder, worthies and sages were obscured, the Dao and virtue were not unified, and the world grasped at partial insights to esteem themselves. Like ears, eyes, nose, and mouth, each has its clarity but cannot communicate with the others—so too the hundred schools and their arts, each with its strengths, each useful at times. Yet they are not comprehensive, not impartial: they are scholars of a single corner.

To judge the beauty of heaven and earth, to analyze the principles of the myriad things, to examine the completeness of the ancients—few can fully embrace the beauty of heaven and earth or match the bearing of the numinous and bright. Thus the Way of inner sageliness and outer kingship has become fragmented and obscure, stifled and unexpressed. The people of the world each follow their desires to create their own methods. Alas! The hundred schools have gone forth and not returned—they will surely not unite.

Later scholars, unfortunate not to see the purity of heaven and earth or the great form of the ancients, will see the *Dao*-art torn apart by the world. Not extravagant toward later generations, not wasteful toward the myriad things, not dazzled by numbers and measures, using the inked line to correct themselves and prepare for the world’s emergencies—this is where the ancient *Dao*-art resided. Mo Di and Qin Guli heard its wind and delighted in it.

They overdid it, making themselves excessively frugal. They created "Non-Music," calling it "restraint of excess." They did not sing in life and wore no mourning in death. Mozi practiced universal love and mutual benefit, rejecting aggression; his Way did not incite anger. He was also fond of broad learning, not distinguishing between schools, not aligning with the former kings. He condemned the ancient rites and music. The Yellow Emperor had *Xianchi*, Yao had *Dazhang*, Shun had *Dashao*, Yu had *Daxia*, Tang had *Dahu*, King Wen had the music of the *Biyong*, and King Wu and the Duke of Zhou composed *Wu*. In ancient mourning rites, noble and base had their forms, high and low their ranks: the Son of Heaven had seven layers of inner and outer coffins, the feudal lords five, great officers three, and scholars two. Yet Mozi alone did not sing in life, wore no mourning in death, and used a paulownia coffin three inches thick without an outer coffin, making this his model. Teaching others thus, he did not love them; practicing it himself, he did not love himself.

This did not ruin Mozi’s Way. Yet singing but not singing, weeping but not weeping, joyful but not joyful—was this truly fitting? His life was toilsome, his death meager; his Way was overly austere. It made people anxious, made them sorrowful; his conduct was difficult to practice. I fear it cannot be the Way of the sage, for it turns against the hearts of the world, and the world cannot bear it. Though Mozi alone could endure it, how could the plain people of the world do so? Cut off from the world, he was far from the kingly Way.

Mozi spoke of the Dao, saying: ‘In ancient times, Yu controlled the floodwaters, dredged the Yangtze and Yellow Rivers, and opened the roads to the four barbarians and nine provinces. There were three hundred famous rivers, three thousand tributaries, and countless smaller ones. Yu personally wielded the spade and plow, blending the labors of the world, his calves bare of skin, his shins bare of hair, his hair washed by heavy rains, his body combed by fierce winds, establishing the ten thousand states. Yu was a great sage, yet he wearied his body for the world to this extent.’ Thus later Mohists wore coarse garments of felt and sandals of hemp, toiling day and night without rest, taking self-imposed hardship as their utmost. They said: ‘If we cannot do this, it is not Yu’s Way—we are unworthy to be called Mohists.’"

The disciples of Xiangli Qin, the followers of Wu Hou, the southern Mohists such as Ku Huo, Ji Chi, and Deng Lingzi and their ilk—all recited the *Mo Jing* yet diverged in their interpretations, calling one another "heterodox Mohists." They disparaged one another with debates over the "hard and white" and "same and different," responded to each other with arguments of "riding pairs" and "non-congruence," and revered the Grand Master as a sage. Each wished to be his successor, hoping to carry on his legacy, and to this day they have not ceased.

Mo Di and Qin Guli’s intentions were sound, but their conduct was misguided. They sought to compel later Mohists to emulate Yu’s self-imposed hardship—bare calves, hairless shins—merely vying in asceticism. This is the height of disorder and the nadir of governance. Yet Mozi was truly a man of lofty aspirations in the world, one whose like is sought in vain. Though he withered away, he never abandoned his cause—a man of true talent indeed! Unburdened by convention, unadorned by material things, uncompromising with others, and unyielding to the crowd.

He longed for the world’s peace to preserve human life, ceasing only when both self and others were sufficiently provided for, thus purifying his heart. Among the ancient arts of the Dao, Song Jian and Yin Wen heard of his teachings and embraced them. They fashioned the Hua Shan cap as a symbol, engaging with all things by first dispelling obstructions. They spoke of the mind’s capacity, calling it "the mind’s conduct," seeking to unite through shame and harmonize the world, proposing to establish this as their guiding principle. They endured insults without feeling dishonored, saving the people’s states, forbidding aggression and halting warfare to end the world’s strife. With this, they traversed the world, persuading rulers above and instructing the people below. Though the world rejected them, they persisted stubbornly, clamoring without cease—thus they were met with weariness above and below yet forced themselves upon others.

Yet their selflessness was too great, their personal needs too few. They said: "Let us fix five *sheng* of rice as sufficient. If the master fears hunger, the disciples, though starving, will not forget the world." Day and night without rest, they declared: "We must surely bring life to the world!" How lofty were these saviors of the age! They said: "The noble man does not nitpick, nor does he lend himself to things. What benefits nothing in the world is better left unclarified." Externally, they forbade aggression and halted warfare; internally, they sought to diminish desires and reduce excess. Their teachings, whether grand or minute, refined or coarse, extended only so far as this.

Impartial yet not partisan, yielding yet without bias, they stood apart without allegiance, following things without duality, unconcerned with deliberation, unswayed by cunning. They made no distinctions among things, moving with them as one. Among the ancient arts of the Dao, Peng Meng, Tian Pian, and Shen Dao heard of their teachings and embraced them. They took the equality of the ten thousand things as their starting point, saying: "Heaven can cover them but not bear them; earth can bear them but not cover them; the great Dao can encompass them but not distinguish them." Knowing that all things have their "can" and "cannot," they said: "Selection without bias, teaching without omission, the Dao leaves nothing behind."

Thus Shen Dao discarded knowledge and self, following only what could not be avoided, letting things flow naturally to form his principles. He said: "To know that one does not know is to approach harm by clinging to knowledge." Unburdened by responsibility, he mocked the world’s esteem for worthies; unbound by conduct, he rejected the world’s great sages. He pushed and pulled, severed and cut, adapting to things, setting aside right and wrong, seeking only to avoid harm. Unbound by wisdom or foresight, indifferent to past or future, he simply existed. He moved only when pushed, advanced only when dragged, like a whirling wind, a spinning feather, a grinding stone’s path—whole and without fault, his movements and stillness without error, never incurring blame.

Why was this so? For things without knowledge have no peril of self-assertion, no burden of wisdom’s use. Their movement and stillness never depart from principle, thus they live without praise. Therefore, he said: "To be like things without knowledge is enough. There is no need for worthies or sages—such rarity does not lose the Dao." Yet the proud and eminent mocked him, saying: "Shen Dao’s Dao is not the conduct of the living but the principle of the dead—how strange indeed!"

Tian Pian was the same. Studying under Peng Meng, he received "non-teaching." Peng Meng’s master said: "The ancient Daoists reached the point of neither affirming nor denying." Their manner was open and free, beyond words. They constantly opposed others, avoided gathering crowds, and did not fall into rigid judgments. What they called the Dao was not the Dao, yet their words did not err into falsity. Peng Meng, Tian Pian, and Shen Dao did not know the Dao, yet all had glimpsed it.

Taking the root as essence, things as coarse, accumulation as insufficiency, they stood alone in communion with the spiritually luminous. Among the ancient arts of the Dao, Guan Yin and Lao Dan heard of their teachings and embraced them. They built upon the constant of "non-being" and "being," governed by the Great One, taking gentleness, weakness, humility, and emptiness as their outward form, and the non-destruction of the ten thousand things as their substance.

Guan Yin said: "Within oneself, hold nothing; let things manifest naturally. In movement, be like water; in stillness, like a mirror; in response, like an echo. Fading like absence, silent like purity. Those who harmonize gain; those who grasp lose. Never precede others, always follow."

Lao Dan said: "Know the masculine, guard the feminine, and be the world’s stream. Know the bright, guard the humble, and be the world’s valley." While others sought precedence, he alone took the rear, saying: "I bear the world’s filth." While others sought fullness, he alone took emptiness, having no hoarding and thus abundance—abundant yet unadorned. In conducting himself, he was unhurried and unexhausted, effortless yet mocking cleverness. While others sought fortune, he alone sought preservation through humility, saying: "I merely avoid blame." Taking depth as his root, simplicity as his guiding principle, he said: "What is rigid breaks; what is sharp is blunted."

Ever tolerant toward all things, never carving away at others—this may be called the ultimate. Ah, Guan Yin and Lao Dan! Truly the vast and authentic persons of antiquity. Still and formless, ever-changing and constant, they moved with death and life, with heaven and earth, with the spirits and the bright. Vast and boundless, where did they go? Fleeting and swift, where did they alight? All things were encompassed, yet none could serve as their refuge. The ancient arts of the Dao resided in this, and Zhuang Zhou, hearing of their influence, delighted in it.

With meandering discourse, boundless words, and speech without limit, he gave free rein to his ideas without constraint, not relying on rigid perceptions. He saw the world as turbid and unfit for solemn speech, so he used carefree words to let meanings unfold, weighty words to convey truth, and parables to broaden understanding. Alone he communed with the spirit of heaven and earth, never condescending to the myriad things, nor entangled in right and wrong, yet dwelling among the common customs of the age. Though his writings were flawed and rough, they remained unharmed. Though his words were uneven and strange, they were still worthy of contemplation. His fullness could not be exhausted: above, he roamed with the Creator, and below, he befriended those beyond death and life, without beginning or end.

In his approach to the root, he was vast and expansive, profound and unrestrained. In his grasp of the essence, he was harmonious and ascended to the heights. Yet in responding to transformations and dissolving things, his principles never ran dry, his insights never faded. Vague and obscure, their depths were never fully plumbed.

Hui Shi was a man of many methods. His writings filled five carts, but his doctrines were contradictory, and his words often missed the mark. In his exploration of things, he declared: "What is greatest has no outside—call it the Great One. What is smallest has no inside—call it the Small One. What has no thickness cannot accumulate, yet it spans a thousand miles. Heaven and earth are low; mountains and marshes are level. The sun is both at its zenith and setting; things are both born and dying. Great sameness differs from small sameness—this is called the small sameness and difference. All things are entirely the same and entirely different—this is called the great sameness and difference. The south is both boundless and bounded. Today I go to Yue, yet I arrived yesterday. Linked rings can be unlinked. I know the center of the world—it lies north of Yan and south of Yue. Embracing all things, heaven and earth are one."

Hui Shi took this as his great principle, and when he surveyed the world to enlighten the debaters, the debaters of the world rejoiced in it together. They delighted in paradoxes: eggs have feathers; a chicken has three feet; Ying possesses the world; a dog can be a sheep; horses lay eggs; tadpoles have tails; fire is not hot; mountains have mouths; wheels do not touch the ground; eyes do not see; pointing does not reach, yet reaching is unceasing; tortoises are longer than snakes; squares are not square, and compasses cannot draw circles; chisels do not fit their handles; the shadow of a flying bird never moves; the speed of an arrow includes moments of neither motion nor stillness; a dog is not a canine; a yellow horse and a black ox make three; a white dog is black; a foal has never had a mother; if you take half of a one-foot stick each day, it will never be exhausted after ten thousand generations.

The debaters used these to engage with Hui Shi, and their arguments never ceased. Huan Tuan and Gongsun Long, followers of the debaters, adorned people’s minds and altered their intentions. They could win in speech but not convince the heart—such were the confines of the debaters. Hui Shi daily used his knowledge to contend with others, setting himself apart to create marvels with the world’s debaters. This was his foundation.

Yet Hui Shi’s eloquence led him to consider himself the most worthy, declaring, "How grand are heaven and earth!" He possessed boldness but no defense. In the south there was a man named Huang Liao who asked why heaven and earth did not collapse or sink, and the causes of wind, rain, thunder, and lightning. Hui Shi did not decline to answer, nor did he ponder before responding. He expounded on the myriad things without end, speaking ceaselessly, and though he thought his words too few, he added even more marvels. He took contrariness as truth and sought fame through victory, which is why he never fit in with the crowd. Weak in virtue but strong in things, his path was perilous.

Viewing Hui Shi’s abilities through the Dao of heaven and earth, he was like a gnat or a mosquito laboring in vain. What use was he to things? Even if one fills a single principle, one may still say it is insufficient—how much more so for valuing the Dao! Hui Shi could not find peace in this; he scattered himself among the myriad things without weariness, and in the end, he was known only for his skill in debate. Alas for Hui Shi’s talent—soaring yet unfulfilled, chasing the myriad things without return. He was like an echo chasing sound, or a form racing its shadow. How pitiful!
